The Ash'ari Solution: Existence as Essence
Presents the Ash'ari doctrine that existence is identical to essence, solving the parts problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that existence is identical to essence, and how this solves the problem of parts of existence.

Core Idea
For al-Ash'ari, existence is the same as the essence; thus everything exists by itself.

After rejecting the initial objection, the passage presents the true solution according to Imam al-Ash'ari. He resolves the puzzle by asserting that existence is not an additional attribute but the very reality of a thing. This radical doctrine avoids the need to posit parts of existence and redefines what we mean by 'attribute' and 'existence.'

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari solution
Read the core statement: 'the truth according to al-Ash'ari is that existence is characterized by existence, because it is the same essence and it is existent.'
This is the key: existence is not something added to essence.
2
Understand the solution to the parts problem
The solution is that parts of existence are existent, and it does not necessitate that the whole be an attribute of the part, because existence of everything is its very essence.
3
Grasp the redefinition of 'attribute'
The passage clarifies that 'attribute' here does not mean something external subsisting in the thing, but whatever is predicated of it, whether identical, included, or external.
Thus, saying 'the thing exists' is a predication of its essence.
4
Note the distinction on existence
The commentary distinguishes between 'mutlaq al-wujud' (absolute existence) and 'al-wujud al-mutlaq' (the absolutely existing), with al-Ash'ari denying the latter. Also, existence is redefined as manifesting effects, not the common notion of having existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
أجزاء الوجود متصفة بالعدم، ويحصل من اجتماعها الوجود كما أن أجزاء الدار متصفة بأنها ليست داراً، ويحصل من اجتماعها الدار غاية ما في الباب أن جزء الوجود إذا كان معدوماً كان الوجود أيضاً معدوماً، وقد عرفت أنه لا استحالة فيه (و) الحق (عند الشيخ ) الأشعري (اتصافه) أي اتصاف الوجود بالوجود لأنه نفس الحقيقة وإنها موجودة فحل الشبهة عنده أن أجزاء الوجود موجودة وليس يلزم منه كون الكل صفة للجزء لأن وجود كل شيء عنده عين حقيقته، وليس المراد بالصفة ما يكون خارجاً عن الشيء قائماً به بل ما يحمل عليه سواء كان عين حقيقته أو داخلاً فيها أو خارجاً
قوله : ( أي اتصاف الوجود أي مطلق الوجود لا الوجود المطلق إذ لا يثبته الشيخ .
قوله : لأن وجود كل شيء عنده عين حقيقته فكل شيء موجود بذاته لا عليه، وليس المراد بالوجود ما هو متفاهم العرف أعني ما قام به الوجود، بل ما يكون مظهر الآثار المطلوبة، والأحكام المختصة سواء كان بنفسه أو بأمر زائد عليه .
The First Observatory - The First Purpose: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. Parts of existence are characterized by non-existence, and from their aggregation existence results, just as parts of a house are characterized by not being a house, and from their aggregation the house results. The utmost in this topic is that if a part of existence is non-existent, then existence would also be non-existent, and you have known that there is no impossibility in it. (And) the truth (according to the Sheikh) al-Ash'ari (is its characterization), i.e., the characterization of existence by existence, because it is the same essence and it is existent. So the solution of the doubt in his view is that parts of existence are existent, and it does not necessitate that the whole be an attribute of the part, because existence of everything in his view is its very essence. And what is meant by attribute is not something external to the thing subsisting in it, but rather what is predicated of it, whether it is its very essence, or included in it, or external. His saying: (i.e., characterization of existence) i.e., absolute existence (mutlaq al-wujud), not the absolutely existing (al-wujud al-mutlaq), since the Sheikh does not affirm it. His saying: Because the existence of everything in his view is its very essence, so everything is existent by itself, not by something else. And what is meant by existence is not what is commonly understood, i.e., that to which existence attaches, but rather what manifests the desired effects and specific rulings, whether by itself or by an added thing.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the problem of whether existence is composite, and presents the Ash'ari solution. It begins by stating an objection: parts of existence are characterized by non-existence, yet their aggregation produces existence, akin to parts of a house not being a house but combining to form one. The objection then argues that if a part were non-existent, the whole would also be non-existent, leading to an impossibility. The commentator notes that this impossibility has already been rejected (it is not impossible). The correct view, according to the Ash'ari Sheikh, is that existence is characterized by existence because existence is the same as the essence itself; hence parts are existent. This avoids the problem of the whole being an attribute of the part, because for the Sheikh existence is identical to essence. The term 'attribute' (sifa) here does not mean something external subsisting in the thing, but rather whatever is predicated of it, whether identical to the essence, included, or external. The commentary then clarifies that 'characterization of existence' means absolute existence (mutlaq al-wujud), not the absolutely existing (al-wujud al-mutlaq), which the Sheikh denies. Further, since existence is the essence, each thing exists by itself, not by an additional attribute; existence means the manifestation of effects, not the common notion of something having existence.

Key Terms
صفة (sifa)
عين الحقيقة (ayn al-haqiqa)
حمل (haml)
قيام (qiyam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is an accidental attribute added to essence.
Correction: For al-Ash'ari, existence is the essence itself, not an added accident.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what does 'attribute' (sifa) mean in the context of existence?
A) Something external subsisting in the thing
B) Whatever is predicated of the thing, whether essence, included, or external
C) An accidental quality added to essence
D) Only the essence itself

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari solution identifies existence with essence, thereby avoiding the problem of parts and redefining attribute and existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you think of existence as something added to His essence, or as His very reality? How does this affect your understanding of Tawheed and your relationship with Him?
?
The passage redefines 'attribute' as any predicate. In your daily life, do you attribute qualities to Allah that are separate from His essence? How can you ensure your attributes of Allah are not externalized but flow from His very reality?

Alternative Solution via the Theory of States (Ahwal)
Presents the solution to the problem of parts of existence using the theory of states (ahwal) as an alternative to the Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theory of states (ahwal) offers an alternative solution to the problem of whether existence is simple or composite, and why this solution presupposes the doctrine of those who affirm states.

Core Idea
Parts of existence are neither existent nor non-existent—they are states (ahwal).

The previous Ash'ari solution to the problem of parts of existence (identifying existence with essence) was found insufficient because it still allows composition. A different approach is offered by those who affirm states (ahwal), a position held by certain theologians. In this passage, the author presents this alternative and explains its implications, showing that parts of existence are characterized neither by existence nor by non-existence, and thus are classed as states.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, noting the key terms: 'حال' (state), 'واسطة' (intermediary), 'مثبتي الأحوال' (affirmers of states). Then read the full translation to grasp the argument.
The passage includes both the main text and editor's glosses.
2
Identify the core claim
Find where the author states that parts of existence are characterized by neither existence nor non-existence. This is the heart of the alternative solution.
The phrase 'لا تتصف أجزاء الوجود ( لا) بهذا ولا بذاك' captures this.
3
Understand the necessity of the 'states' doctrine
The passage argues that this solution only works within the school of those who affirm states (muthbiti al-ahwal). Why? Because existence itself is considered a state, and so too are its parts.
Without states, one would have to say parts are either existent or non-existent, which leads to the original problem.
4
Analyze the gloss concerning predication vs. inherence
The commentary clarifies that 'characterization' (ittisaf) is meant as predication (haml), not inherence (urud). If it were inherence, the argument would be invalid. Grasp this distinction.
This shows the precision of kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
عنها، وقد عرفت أن ذكر مذهب الشيخ لا يناسب هذا المقام لأن الوجود إذا كان عين الحقيقة فمن الحقائق مركبات ومنها بسائط، وكذا الحال في الوجودات ( وقد يقال ) في حل الشبهة ( لا تتصف أجزاء الوجود ( لا) بهذا ولا بذاك ) أي لا بالوجود ولا بالعدم (وهو تصريح بإثبات بالواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم فلا يصح إلا على مذهب مثبتي الأحوال، فتكون أجزاء الوجود عندهم من قبيل الأحوال كما أن الوجود عندهم كذلك
قوله : ( وقد عرفت إلخ) لا يخفى أن ما ذكره غير معلوم مما سبق إلا أنه لكونه من القوة القريبة من الفعل بعد معرفة ما تقدم من عدم صحة اختيار الوجود بديهياً أو كسبياً على مذهب الشيخ لعدم قوله : بالوجود المطلق نزل منزلة المعلوم .

The First Observatory - The First Purpose: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. About it [the previous], and you have known that mentioning the Sheikh's school does not suit this place, because if existence is the same essence, then among essences there are composites and among them there are simples, and likewise the case with existences. (And it may be said) in solving the doubt: (Parts of existence are not characterized (by) this nor that), i.e., neither by existence nor by non-existence. (And it is an explicit affirmation of an intermediary between existent and non-existent, so it is only valid on the school of those who affirm states (ahwal). Thus parts of existence in their view are of the category of states, just as existence is in their view likewise.) His saying: (And you have known etc.) It is not hidden that what he mentioned is not known from what preceded, except that because it is of the proximate potency, after knowing what preceded of the invalidity of choosing existence as intuitive or acquired according to the Sheikh’s school due to his not affirming absolute existence, it is placed in the position of the known. His saying: (does not suit etc.) He only said that because it may be said that constructing the answer on a premise believed by the Sheikh—that existence is the same essence—does not necessitate constructing on his school so that it entails denial of absolute existence; thus it is not valid to choose that it is simple. His saying: (this place) i.e., the place of dispute concerning whether existence is simple or composite. His saying: (And it is an explicit affirmation etc.) If characterization is taken as predication, it is fine; but if it is meant as inherence, then no, as before. As for what is said that a state (hal) must be an attribute of an existent, it is not necessary from what was mentioned; it is nothing because if it is said it is not non-existent, it necessarily entails derivative realization, and because it is an assertion of an intermediary between them, and there is no intermediary besides the state essentially, so it is a state.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative solution to the problem of parts of existence, based on the theory of states (ahwal) affirmed by some theologians (e.g., the Mu'tazila and some Ash'aris). The text begins by stating that the earlier Ash'ari solution (identifying existence with essence) is not suitable here because existence as essence can be simple or composite, so it does not resolve the composition problem. The alternative solution asserts that parts of existence are characterized neither by existence nor by non-existence; they are intermediate states (ahwal). This presupposes the doctrine of those who affirm states (muthbiti al-ahwal), since existence itself in that view is also a state. The commentary then explains several glosses: First, the phrase 'you have known' refers to knowledge obtained indirectly. Second, the claim that mentioning the Sheikh's school does not suit the context because his premises do not force the denial of absolute existence; thus one could still hold existence as simple. Third, it confirms that the ‘explicit affirmation’ of an intermediate (wāsita) only works if characterization is taken as predication; if inherence is meant, it fails. It also defends the notion of state (hal) against the objection that a state must be an attribute of something existent; the response is that the parts are not non-existent, so they have a derived (taba‘i) realization, and the state is the only intermediary, thus it is a state.

أحوال (ahwal)
واسطة (wāsita)
موجود (mawjud)
معدوم (ma'dum)
مثبتي الأحوال (muthbiti al-ahwal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that states (ahwal) are purely imaginary or that they must be attributes of something fully existent.
Correction: The passage clarifies that states are affirmed as intermediaries between existent and non-existent; they are not non-existent but have derivative realization (tahaqquq taba'i). The objection that a state must be an attribute of an existent is rejected because the parts of existence are not non-existent, and the state is the only intermediary category.

Key Takeaway
The theory of states (ahwal) solves the problem of parts of existence by classing them as intermediaries between existence and non-existence, a solution only coherent within the doctrine of those who affirm states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson offers a nuanced solution to a philosophical problem by avoiding binary extremes. In your own spiritual life, where do you tend to see things as either 'existent' (present/valid) or 'non-existent' (absent/invalid)? Consider your daily prayers—do you label a prayer as either completely accepted or completely wasted? How might embracing an intermediate 'state'—where your effort is neither perfect nor null—change your relationship with your practice?
?
The theory of states requires precise language: parts of existence are 'not characterized by this nor that.' Could it be that your own weaknesses in faith are not simply either 'I have iman' or 'I have no iman,' but rather a state that is real though not fully present? What would it mean for you to treat your current faith level as a real state that can be nurtured, rather than as a failure if it's not complete?
?
The passage defends an intermediary despite the risk of logical objection. Think of a relationship in your life (with family, community, or Allah) that you've categorized as either 'good' or 'broken.' How might acknowledging an intermediate state—neither perfect nor ruined—open a path for repair and growth? What concrete step can you take this week to act on that intermediate reality?

Third Argument Against Composition: The External Distinction Refuted
Sanusi argues that the division argument against composition depends on the external distinction of genus and difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against composition in divine existence and why it is rejected by denying the external distinction of genus and difference.

Core Idea
The argument fails because genus and difference are not distinct in external reality.

This lesson examines a key argument against the composition of existence and the commentator's rebuttal. The argument relies on the external distinction of genus and difference, but the commentator denies that such distinction exists in the external world. We will explore the two premises of the argument and why they are rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly; the argument is incomplete.
2
Identify the two premises of the argument
The commentator explicitly states that the argument is built upon two premises. Find these premises in the text and note how they are presented.
The premises are: (1) external distinction of genus and difference, (2) their priority over the species.
3
Understand the denial of each premise
The commentator denies both premises. Look for the reasons given: definition depends on mental composition, not external parts; the distinction is only in the mind.
The denial of external distinction is the key rebuttal.

His saying (in the third argument for negating composition from existence): (The parts are described) (by existence with, or after, or before). Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the composition of existence and a rebuttal. The argument states that if existence were composite, its parts would be described as existing with, after, or before the whole. The commentator responds that this argument is built upon two premises: (1) the external distinction between genus and difference, and (2) their priority over the species in existence. Both premises are denied. The discussion then turns to the nature of genus and difference: the well-known view is that definition (ḥadd) depends on composition from genus and difference, not from externally distinct parts. Therefore, the distinction between genus and difference is only in the mind, not in external existence. The passage ends abruptly with 'because' (limā), suggesting a further explanation that is missing from the text.

?

?

Are you willing to submit your reasoning to the revealed truths, even when they challenge your mental categories? Existence is Composed of Non-Existent Parts
The author argues that existence's parts are qualified by non-existence without contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that existence is not composed of pure non-existences but rather of non-existent things, and how this avoids the charge of impossibility.

Core Idea
Existence is built from parts that are not existence, but that doesn't make it impossible.

The author addresses a potential objection: if existence is composed of parts, those parts must be non-existent, leading to the absurdity that existence is made of pure non-existences. He clarifies that the parts are not pure non-existences but rather 'non-existent things' (معدومات), which avoids the contradiction. This subtle distinction preserves the composability of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage and Translation
Start by reading the Arabic text aloud and then follow the English translation to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: الوجود, العدم, المعدوم.
2
Understand the Core Distinction
Identify the difference between 'pure non-existences' (عدمان) and 'non-existent things' (معدومات). The author insists that the parts are the latter, not the former.
This distinction is crucial to avoid the alleged impossibility.
3
Examine the Example of Ten
The author gives the example of ten composed of units. None of the units is ten itself, yet ten is real. This illustrates how parts can be non-existent relative to the whole.
Also consider the mental example of animal and man.
4
Follow the Three Temporal Possibilities
The author lists three ways the parts can relate temporally to the whole: simultaneous, after, or before. Understand why each is acceptable.
The third possibility is subtle: the parts exist in the mind before the whole, but that doesn't imply existence preceding itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
الخارج ( كما سيأتي ) تحقيقه ( أو نختار أنه ( أي جزء الوجود ( يتصف بالمعدوم ) أي بمفهوم المعدوم بل بالعدم ) ولا يكون الوجود ) حينئذ (محض العدمات ) حتى يكون محالاً ( بل محض معدومات)، فلا يلزم إلا كون الوجود مركباً من أجزاء متصفة بنقيضه ) وكذا كل مركب من أجزاء متمايزة الوجود في الخارج، فإنه مركب من أجزاء متصفة بنقيضه ( فالعشرة) مثلاً (محض أمور لا شيء منها بعشرة) أعني الوحدات التي ركبت منها العشرة وكذا الحال في الأجزاء الذهنية فإن الحيوان نفسه ليس عين الإنسان في الحقيقة وإن كانا متصادقين وليس يلزم من ذلك كون أحد في ضمنه ومتأخر عنه عند تحليله فعلى تقدير تركيب الوجود من الجنس والفصل، نختار أن أجزاءه تتصف في الذهن بالوجود مع وجود الكل وبعده، وقبله كسائر الأجزاء والكل ولا محذور في شيء من التقادير، أما على الأولين فظاهر، إذ لا جزئية لهما باعتبار هذين الوجودين، وأما على الثالث فلان اللازم حينئذ تقدم الوجود الذهني لأجزاء الوجود على الوجود الذهني للوجود لا تقدم الوجود على نفسه.
The First Observatory - The First Aim: On Defining Existence and Non-Existence. The external (as will come) its verification (or we choose that it, i.e., the part of existence, is qualified by the non-existent, i.e., by the concept of the non-existent, nay, by non-existence) and existence is not then (pure non-existences) such that it would be impossible (but pure non-existent things). So nothing follows except that existence is composed of parts qualified by its opposite, and likewise every composite of parts distinct in existence in the external, for it is composed of parts qualified by its opposite. (For example, ten) is (pure things, none of which is ten), meaning the units from which ten is composed. And similarly the case with mental parts, for animal itself is not identical to man in reality, although they are co-extensive, and it does not follow from that that one of them is within the other and posterior to it when analyzed. So on the supposition that existence is composed of genus and differentia, we choose that its parts are qualified in the mind by existence along with the existence of the whole, and after it, and before it, like other parts and wholes, and there is no harm in any of the suppositions. As for the first two, it is obvious, for there is no part-relation for them with respect to these two existences. As for the third, because the necessary consequence then is the priority of the mental existence of the parts of existence over the mental existence of existence, not the priority of existence over itself.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the view that existence is not composed of pure non-existences (عدمان) but rather of non-existent things (معدومات). The commentator begins by stating the position: we choose that each part of existence is qualified by the concept of the non-existent, not by pure non-existence. Thus existence is not a collection of pure non-existences, which would be impossible, but rather a collection of non-existent things. This avoids the impossibility because composition from non-existent parts is not contradictory. The commentator gives the example of ten: ten is composed of units, and none of those units is ten itself. Similarly, with mental parts like animal and man, animal is not identical to man, though they are co-extensive. Even if existence is composed of genus and differentia, the parts are qualified by existence mentally, and there is no issue. The commentator then considers three possibilities for the temporal relation of the parts' existence to the whole's existence: simultaneous, after, or before. In the first two, there is no problem. In the third, the mental existence of the parts precedes the mental existence of existence itself, which is not a problem because it is not existence preceding itself, but rather one mental concept preceding another.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم
المعدوم
العدمات
المعدومات
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is composed of parts, those parts must be pure non-existences, leading to impossibility.
Correction: The parts are non-existent things (معدومات), not pure non-existences (عدمان). This subtle distinction avoids contradiction because non-existent things are specific absences, not absolute nothing.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue existence is composed of?
A) Pure non-existences (عدمان)
B) Non-existent things (معدومات)
C) Neither, it is simple
D) Both equally

Key Takeaway
Existence is composed of parts that are non-existent in themselves but not pure non-existences, so composition from non-existence is not contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are an existent being composed of parts that are not you—your body, thoughts, and experiences. Do you feel a tension between the 'you' that is aware and the parts that make you up? How does this lesson's argument that existence can be composed of non-existent parts without contradiction reshape your understanding of your own identity and wholeness?
?
The author uses the example of ten and its units: none of the units is ten itself. Reflect on something you consider essential to your existence—perhaps a role, a relationship, or an achievement. Is it really you, or is it a part that, like a unit of ten, is not the whole? Can you accept that your existence is not diminished even if that part is non-existent?
?
Consider a limitation or lack (a 'non-existent thing') in your life that you often view as a contradiction to your potential. How might you reframe it as a constituent part of your existence, not a flaw but a component that makes your existence what it is? What would it mean to embrace that your being includes non-being without contradiction?

Refuting the impossibility of composition from non-existences
The commentator refutes the objection by distinguishing two types of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between parts being pure non-existences versus non-existent things and why the impossibility of composition from non-existences is not self-evident.

Core Idea
Not all composition from non-existence is impossible — only parts that are pure non-existences.

The commentator here addresses a key objection against the possibility of existence being composed of non-existent parts. He shows that the arguer confused two different categories, and even the problematic category lacks proof of impossibility. This passage sharpens our understanding of metaphysical composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage is short but dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the distinction
The commentator points out a crucial distinction: 'عدمان' (pure non-existences) vs 'معدومات' (non-existent things). Understand what each means.
Pure non-existences are absolute nothingness, while non-existent things are things that do not exist but have some quiddity.
3
Understand the reasoning
The arguer claims impossibility based on a thing subsisting through its opposite. The commentator denies that this is proof and questions the claim of self-evidence.
Reflect on why the claim of self-evidence is not accepted in rigorous argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage shows that the impossibility is not proven and that the distinction matters. Consider how this affects the larger argument about divine existence.
This is a typical move in kalam: narrowing the opponent's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حتى يكون محالاً) بناء على لزوم تقوم الشيء بنقيضه، وإنما ذكر هذه المقدمة للتنبيه على أن المستدل لم يفرق بين كون أجزائه عدمات وبين كونه معدومات والمحال هو الأول دون الثاني على أنه يمكن منع استحالة الأول أيضاً إذ لا دليل على امتناع تقوم الشيء بنقيضه، ودعوى البداهة غير مسموعة .
His statement: (such that it would be impossible) is based on the necessity of a thing's subsisting through its opposite. He only mentioned this premise to draw attention to the fact that the arguer did not distinguish between its parts being non-existences and it being non-existent things, and the impossible is the first, not the second. Moreover, the impossibility of the first can also be denied, for there is no proof against the impossibility of a thing's subsisting through its opposite, and the claim of self-evidence is not admissible.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'such that it would be impossible' in the base text. The commentator explains that the arguer (the opponent) assumes that if existence were composed of non-existent parts, it would entail a thing subsisting through its opposite, which is impossible. The commentator points out that the arguer failed to distinguish between the parts being pure non-existences (عدمان) and the parts being non-existent things (معدومات). The impossibility applies only to the first, not the second. Furthermore, even the impossibility of the first can be denied because there is no proof that a thing cannot subsist through its opposite; the claim that it is self-evident is not accepted.

Key Terms

تقوم الشيء بنقيضه
عدمان

بداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any composition of existence from non-existent parts is impossible.
Correction: The commentator limits the impossibility to composition from pure non-existences (عدمان), not from non-existent things (معدومات).

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what did the arguer fail to distinguish?
A) Existence and non-existence
B) Parts being non-existences versus non-existent things
C) Mental and external existence
D) Genus and differentia

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of composition from non-existences is not self-evident; it depends on the type of non-existence and requires proof.

Logical Consequences of Composing Existence from Non-Existent Parts
Commentator lists necessary consequences of a premise about existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessary logical consequences that follow from the premise that existence is composed of non-existent parts, and why the commentator sees no contradiction.

Core Idea
If existence is built from non-existence, even existence itself becomes non-existent.

In theological and philosophical discussions, one might propose that existence itself is composed of parts that are individually non-existent. This passage explores what logically follows from that premise. The commentator lists three consequences and insists none are harmful. Let's examine each step carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the commentator first refers to the original statement, then lists consequences using 'وأن' (and that).
The passage is brief but dense. Focus on the three 'أن' clauses.
2
Identify the three consequences
The passage lists three things that follow: (1) the parts are non-existent, (2) existence is predicated of them by agreement (مواطأة), (3) existence itself is non-existent. Write them down in your own words.
The second point involves technical term 'مواطأة' meaning 'by way of agreement' implying a conventional rather than real predication.
3
Understand the commentator's stance
After listing the consequences, the commentator states: 'ولا محذور في شيء من ذلك' (And there is no harm in any of that). This is crucial. He accepts the apparent paradox as unproblematic within the logical framework.
This shows that in this tradition, logical consistency is more important than intuitive plausibility.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this matters: the discussion is about the nature of existence and composition. If existence can be composed of non-existents, then the usual assumption that a whole must share the nature of its parts is not enforced.
This connects to broader debates about whether divine attributes or existence can be 'composite' in any sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا كون الوجود مركباً إلخ) واللازم منه أن تكون الأجزاء معدومة وأن يصدق عليها الوجود مواطأة لكونها أجزاء محمولة، وأن يكون الوجود معدوماً لكون أجزائه معدومة، ولا محذور في شيء من ذلك.
His statement: (except that existence is composed, etc.) And what follows from it is that the parts are non-existent, and that existence is predicated of them by way of agreement because they are predicate parts, and that existence itself is non-existent because its parts are non-existent. And there is no harm in any of that.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'except that existence is composed' in the base text. The commentator explains the necessary consequences if existence is composed of non-existent parts. First, the parts themselves are non-existent. Second, existence is predicated of these parts by way of agreement (مواطأة) because they are parts that are predicated (i.e., they are predicates in a composite). Third, existence itself is non-existent because its parts are non-existent. The commentator stresses that none of these consequences are problematic.

Key Terms
لازم
معدوم
مواطأة
أجزاء محمولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if existence is composed of non-existent parts, then existence itself cannot be non-existent because that would be a contradiction.
Correction: The commentator accepts that existence itself becomes non-existent, but sees no logical harm. The point is that in this argument, the premise forces the conclusion, and one must be consistent even if it seems odd.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows from existence being composed of non-existent parts?
A) Only that the parts are non-existent
B) That the parts are existent
C) That existence is predicated of the parts and existence itself is non-existent
D) That existence is impossible

Key Takeaway
The premise that existence is composed of non-existent parts leads to the conclusion that existence itself is non-existent, but this is accepted as logically unproblematic within the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you accept a premise that leads to a conclusion that seems absurd—like 'existence itself is non-existent'—would you reject the premise or accept the conclusion? What does your instinct reveal about your intellectual humility?
?
In your own life, have you ever held a belief that logically forces you to accept a truth you find uncomfortable? How did you handle it—by adjusting your belief or by avoiding the reasoning?
?
The commentator calmly says 'no harm' to a conclusion that defies common sense. Are there areas where you stubbornly avoid following an argument to its end because you fear where it might lead? What are you protecting?

Mental Existence and External Parts
Commentator explains units as external parts and mental existence as knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mental existence is merely knowledge and that units are considered external parts only in terms of distinction in original existence, even if only mentally.

Core Idea
Mental existence is not real existence – it is simply knowledge.

This passage from the commentary clarifies a subtle point about the units in a composite. The units are called 'external parts' not because they exist externally as separate objects, but because they are distinct in original existence, even if that existence is only mental. The commentator then explains that mental existence is nothing but knowledge, and that the priority of the mental existence of parts over the whole is not problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the commentator defines the units and what he means by 'distinct in original existence'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ولو في الذهن' (even if only in the mind).
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms 'الوجود الأصيلي' (original existence), 'الوجود الذهني' (mental existence), and 'العلم' (knowledge). How are they related in the passage?
The commentator equates mental existence with knowledge.
3
Grasp the distinction
The units are 'external parts' in the sense of conceptual distinction, not physical separation. The whole's existence is also knowledge. Consider how this relates to the earlier example of Zayd.
4
Reflect on the priority
The commentator says there is no harm in the mental existence of the parts being realized before the mental existence of the whole. Why is this not a problem?
Because it is just a priority of one mental concept over another, not a priority in real existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أعني الوحدات وهي أجزاء خارجية بمعنى أنها متمايزة في الوجود الأصيلي ولو في الذهن وإن لم تكن موجودات في الأعيان في الذهن عبارة عن العلم به، ووجود الكل أيضاً عبارة عن العلم بالكل، وقد يتحقق الأول قبل الثاني بلا محذور، إذ لا محذور في تقديم نفس الوجود الذهني على وجوده فتدبر.
His statement: (I mean the units, and they are external parts, meaning that they are distinct in original existence, even if only in the mind, even if they are not existent in external entities. In the mind, it is an expression of knowledge of it, and the existence of the whole is also an expression of knowledge of the whole. And the first may be realized before the second without harm, for there is no harm in prioritizing the very mental existence over its existence. So ponder.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'I mean the units' from the earlier example. The commentator explains that the units are external parts in the sense that they are distinct in original existence, even if only mentally, and even if they do not exist in external entities. Mental existence is just knowledge of the thing, and the existence of the whole is knowledge of the whole. It is possible for the mental existence of the parts to occur before the mental existence of the whole, and this is not problematic because it is just a priority of one mental concept over another.

Correction: The commentator clarifies that mental existence is merely knowledge of the thing, not a separate external reality.

Mental existence is knowledge, and units are 'external parts' only in terms of conceptual distinction in original existence, not as separate external objects.

Objection and Answer: Mental Parts and Conjunction of Opposites
The commentator answers an objection about mental parts being simultaneously existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves an apparent contradiction of existence and non-existence being predicated of the same mental parts.

Core Idea
Two different modes of predication can avoid a true contradiction.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a potential objection to his earlier argument. The objection claims that if mental parts are described as both existence and non-existence, a contradiction arises. The answer distinguishes between two types of predication, showing that the contradiction is only apparent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the claim that mental parts are qualified by each other and by the whole, which would lead to a conjunction of opposites.
Focus on the logical structure of the objection.
2
Identify the two predication modes
Notice the terms 'مواطأة' (agreement) and 'اشتقاق' (derivation). These are key to the answer.
Think about how predication can differ in meaning.
3
Apply the distinction
See how predication of 'existence' by agreement and 'non-existence' by derivation avoids contradiction.
Ask yourself: Could something be both 'existent' and 'non-existent' in different senses?
4
Reflect on the resolution
Confirm that no true conjunction of opposites occurs because the predications are not of the same type.
The harmful conjunction would be if both were predicated in the same manner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل بالعدم إن قلت الأجزاء الذهنية يتصف أحدها بالآخر، وبالكل أيضاً فإنه يصدق أن الناطق حيوان وأنه إنسان فلو اتصف أجزاء الوجود بالعدم، ولا شك أنها أجزاء ذهنية اتصف أيضاً بالوجود الذي هو الكل لما قلنا : فيلزم اتصافها بالوجود والعدم معاً، وإنه اجتماع النقيضين قلت بعد تسليم أن الاختيار ليس مبنياً على التنزل وتسليم التمايز الخارجي بين الجنس والفصل المانع عن التصادق اتصاف الأجزاء الذهنية بالكل بمعنى حمله عليها مواطأة واتصافها بالعدم هاهنا بمعنى قيامه بها وحمله عليها اشتقاقا فاللازم أن تصدق على تلك الأجزاء أنها معدومة وأنها وجود ولا محذور فيه بل المحذور أن يصدق عليها أنها موجودة وأنها معدومة.
His statement: (Rather, by non-existence. If you say: The mental parts are qualified by one another, and also by the whole, for it is true that 'rational' is an animal and that it is a man. So if the parts of existence are qualified by non-existence, and there is no doubt that they are mental parts, they are also qualified by existence, which is the whole, according to what we said: then their being qualified by existence and non-existence together follows, and that is a conjunction of opposites. I say: After granting that the choice is not based on concession, and granting the external distinction between genus and differentia preventing co-extensiveness, the qualification of the mental parts by the whole means predicating it of them by way of agreement, and their qualification by non-existence here means its subsisting in them and predicating it of them by way of derivation. So what follows is that those parts are truly described as non-existent and as existence, and there is no harm in that. Rather, the harmful thing would be that they are truly described as existent and as non-existent.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that presents an objection and its answer. The objection states that mental parts are qualified by each other and by the whole, e.g., 'rational' is an animal and a man. If the parts of existence are qualified by non-existence (as argued), then since they are mental parts, they would also be qualified by existence (the whole), leading to a conjunction of existence and non-existence in the same thing. The answer distinguishes between two types of predication: predicating the whole of the parts by agreement (مواطأة) and predicating non-existence of them by derivation (اشتقاق). When we say 'the parts are non-existent' (معدومة), we use derivation, meaning non-existence subsists in them. When we say 'the parts are existence' (وجود), we use agreement, meaning they are truly existence. There is no contradiction because 'existent' (موجودة) and 'non-existent' (معدومة) are not both predicated of them in the same way. The harmful conjunction would be if both 'existent' and 'non-existent' were predicated in the same manner, but that does not occur.

Key Terms
الأجزاء الذهنية
اتصاف
الكل
الوجود
العدم
اجتماع النقيضين
مواطأة
اشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'existence' and 'non-existence' are contradictory regardless of the mode of predication.
Correction: The commentator shows that if one is predicated by agreement and the other by derivation, no contradiction arises because they are not the same type of predication.

Checkpoint
In the answer, how is 'non-existence' predicated of the parts?
A) By agreement (مواطأة)
B) By derivation (اشتقاق)
C) Essentially
D) Accidentally

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction of existence and non-existence in mental parts is resolved by noting that they are predicated in different ways: by agreement and by derivation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been in a situation where you thought two things were contradictory, but after deeper analysis, you realized they were actually compatible because they applied in different ways? Reflect on your own beliefs: where might you be conflating different modes of predication or judgment without realizing it? What specific belief about Allah or existence might you be misunderstanding because you haven't distinguished the modes of attribution? Commit to examining one such belief this week.
?
This lesson shows that a surface-level contradiction can be resolved by understanding the mode of predication. When you read the Qur'an or statements of the Salaf, do you assume a single mode of predication for all attributes? What if some attributes are predicated by agreement and others by derivation? How does that affect your understanding of Allah's names and attributes? What step will you take to learn more about this?
?
The objector's mistake was assuming mental parts are qualified in the same way. Have you ever made a similar mistake in your own reasoning about God, thinking that because something applies in one sense it must apply in another? What is one area of your aqeedah where you might be committing this error? Resolve today to study the modes of predication in Islamic theology.

Refutation of the Third Argument from General Emanation
Refutation of the third argument based on general emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of the argument that the universal must be prior because emanation from God is general, and why this refutation hinges on the difference between a necessary agent and a free agent.

Core Idea
The third argument only works if God is a necessary agent, not a free agent.

In this passage, the commentator rebuts a third argument that claimed the universal is known before the particular because God's emanation is general. The refutation hinges on whether God is a necessary being whose action is automatic or a free agent who can choose. The Ash'ari position affirms free will, thus the argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the back-and-forth format: the arguer's claim, then the response.
The passage is a commentary on a prior text, so it uses 'his saying' to quote.
2
Identify the argument's core premise
The third argument assumes that God's emanation is general and necessary, leading to the priority of the universal. Find where this premise is stated.
Look for the phrase 'موجب بالذات' (necessary being).
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation rejects the necessity of emanation by asserting that God is a free agent (فاعل مختار). Therefore, He can create knowledge of the particular without the universal.
The text also clarifies that the argument's validity depends on one's theology of divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في الاستدلال على ذلك ثالثاً (الفيض عام قلنا: مبني على الموجب بالذات ) حتى يجب الفيض منه عند اجتماع الشرائط وارتفاع الموانع ونحن لا نقول به بل الحوادث كلها مستندة عندنا إلى الفاعل المختار فجاز أن يوجد العلم بالخاص دون العلم بالعام . قوله : (قلنا مبني على الموجب بالذات حاصله أنا لا نسلم عموم الفيض فإنه تعالى فاعل بالاختيار، فيجوز أن يفيض تصور الخاص ولا يفيض تصور العام وليس بموجب حتى يكون فيضه عاماً، والتخصيص بحسب الشرائط ورفع الموانع، فافهم فإنه مما خفي على أقوام . قوله : ( في الاستدلال على ذلك ثالثاً قد نبهناك سابقاً على أن هذا القول علة ثانية لأعرفية دعم لا علة ثالثة لأعرفية الوجود كما زعمه الشارح فيما يستفاد من ظاهر كلامه فتأمل . قوله : (قلنا مبني على الموجب بالذات يعني أن مراد المستدل وهو إثبات أعرفية العام إنما يتم في الموجب بالذات، وإلا فيجوز أن يختار المختار، فيض العلم بالخاص ولا يختار فيض العلم بالعام فالقول بأنه ليس مبنياً على الموجب لوجوب الفيض عن المختار أيضاً بعد ارتفاع الموانع وتحقق جميع الشرائط التي من جملتها تعلق إرادته عدول عن محصول الكلام .
His saying: 'In the third argument (for that), (the emanation is general, we said: it is based on the necessary being) such that emanation from Him is necessary when conditions are met and obstacles are removed, but we do not hold that; rather, all events are according to us dependent on the free agent, so it is possible that knowledge of the particular occurs without knowledge of the universal.' His saying: 'We said: based on the necessary being' its summary is that we do not concede the generality of emanation, for He, exalted, is a free agent, so it is possible that He emanates the concept of the particular and does not emanate the concept of the universal, and He is not a necessary being such that His emanation is general, and specification is according to conditions and removal of obstacles, so understand, for it is among things hidden from some people.' His saying: 'In the third argument, we have previously reminded you that this statement is a second cause for the priority [؟] not a third cause for the priority of existence as the commentator claimed from the apparent meaning of his words, so reflect.' His saying: 'We said: based on the necessary being' means that the intention of the arguer, which is to prove the priority of the universal, is only valid in the case of the necessary being; otherwise, it is possible for the free agent to choose to emanate knowledge of the particular and not choose to emanate knowledge of the universal. So the statement that it is not based on the necessary being because emanation from the free agent is also necessary after removal of obstacles and fulfillment of all conditions, including the attachment of His will, is a deviation from the gist of the discourse.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the third argument for the claim that knowledge of the universal is prior to knowledge of the particular. The argument claimed that emanation from God is general, so if we know the particular, we must also know the universal. The commentator refutes this by stating that this argument only holds if God is a necessary being (الموجب بالذات) whose emanation is necessary. However, the Ash'ari position is that God is a free agent (فاعل مختار), so He may choose to create knowledge of the particular without knowledge of the universal. The commentary then explains that the arguer's intention is only valid under the doctrine of necessary emanation, not under free will. It also notes a prior correction about the classification of this argument as a second cause rather than a third cause for the priority of existence.

Key Terms
فيض
موجب بالذات
فاعل مختار
أعم
أخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the priority of the universal is self-evident regardless of one's view of God.
Correction: The text shows that the argument for the universal's priority only works if one assumes God is a necessary agent; under free agency, the argument fails.

Checkpoint
According to the response, the third argument for the priority of the universal is based on which assumption about God?
A) God is a free agent
B) God is a necessary being
C) God is omniscient
D) God is omnipotent

Key Takeaway
The validity of the argument for the priority of the universal depends on whether one holds God to be a necessary or free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you relate to God as a free Agent who personally chooses to answer your prayers, or do you sometimes assume He is bound to act based on your actions? How might this affect your reliance on His wisdom?
?
If God were a necessary agent, what would that mean for your hope in His mercy? How does knowing He is free and unconstrained deepen your trust in His decisions?
?
When you face uncertainty, does your heart truly rest in the freedom of God to act as He wills, or do you secretly demand that He follow a predictable pattern? What would change if you fully embraced His absolute freedom?

The Universal's Conditions: Valid in External Existence
Response to argument about conditions; distinguishes external vs mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that the universal has fewer conditions holds only for realization in external individuals and not universally.

Core Idea
The argument about fewer conditions applies only to external existence, not to mental existence.

The author responds to an argument that the universal has fewer conditions than the particular. He clarifies that this is true when considering their realization in individual instances (هويات), because the universal is realized in more individuals. However, this does not hold for mental images, which we will explore in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and the response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author first presents the opponent's claim.
The argument is stated in the first line.
2
Understand the distinction
The author distinguishes between realization in individuals (هويات) and in the mind. Focus on why the argument works for external existence.
The key term is 'تحقق' (realization).
3
See the logical relation
In external existence, conditions of the universal are also conditions for any particular, but not vice versa. This makes the universal's conditions fewer.
Example: substance vs. human.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله) في هذا الاستدلال ) شروط العام ومعانداته أقل ) من شروط الخاص ومعانداته (قلنا : ذلك) الذي ذكرتموه إنما هو (بالنسبة إلى تحققهما) أي تحقق العام والخاص ) في الهويات إذ العموم والخصوص إنما يعرض للشيء باعتبار ذلك فالأعم يكون متحققاً في هويات وأفراد أكثر، والأخص في أفراد أقل فإذا ترتبت الأشياء في العموم والخصوص، كالجوهر بالنسبة إلى نوع الإنسان، بل صنفه فكل ما هو شرط لتحقق الأعم أو معاند له فهو شرط لتحقق الأخص، أو قوله : ( إنما هو بالنسبة إلى تحققهما أي كلياً كما هو مقصود المستدل . قوله : ( في الهويات) أي الأفراد لم يقل في الخارج ليشمل العام والخاص اللذين من الأمور الذهنية كالكيفيات النفسانية . في الاستدلال وبالعكس ، فيدور لأنا نمنع توقف العلم بالأعرفية على العلم بالبداهة على أنه وجه غير ما ذكره الشارح إلا أن يريد التوقف بحسب العلم فتأمل . معاند له فإنه لو لم يتحقق الأعم في ضمن فرد لم يتحقق الأخص في ضمنه بدون العكس، إذ قد يتحقق الأعم في ضمن فرد غير فرد الأخص ( لا ) بالنسبة إلى تحققهما في الذهن إذ لا علاقة بين الصورتين الذهنيتين) بحسب تحققهما في الذهن، فجاز أن تحصل صورة الخاص فيه بدون صورة العام ولا تعاند بين الصورة الذهنية بل هي متقاربة ألا يرى أن الضد أقرب حضوراً بالبال مع الضد منه بدونه، نعم إذا كان الأعم جزء الأخص وكان الأخص معلوماً بالكنه كان شرط تحقق الأعم في الذهن شرطاً لتحقق الأخص فيه وكذا معاند تحقق الأعم فيه إن فرض هناك معاند لتحقق الأخص فيه من غير عكس كلي ) قوله : ( فإنه لو لم يتحقق الأعم إلخ) يعني يمتنع تحقق أي أخص يفرض بدون تحقق الأعم فما يتوقف تحقق الأعم عليه من الشروط ورفع الموانع يكون موقوفاً عليه لكل أخص، ويجوز أن يتحقق الأعم بدون أي أخص يفرض في ضمن فرد أخص آخر، فلا يكون ما يتوقف عليه أي أخص يفرض موقوفاً عليه لتحقق الأعم، وإن كان مجامعاً له بناء على أنه لا وجود للأعم إلا في ضمن الأخص، وإلا لما تحقق في ضمن فرد أخص آخر، فيكون ما يتوقف تحقق الأعم عليه أقل مما يتوقف عليه الأخص هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم . قوله : (لا) بالنسبة إلى تحققهما في الذهن) أي ليس ما ذكرتموه من أقلية شروط الأعم أو معانداته كلياً بالنسبة إلى تحققهما في الذهن، أي بالوجود الظلي لأن تلك الأقلية إنما كانت لعلاقة العموم ،والخصوص، كما ذكرتموه ولا علاقة عموم وخصوص بين الصورتين الذهنيتين للأعم والأخص بحسب الوجود الظلي بل هما متباينتان إذ صورة الأعم مباينة لصورة الأخص لا تحمل عليها، وبما حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع ما قيل أن نفي جنس العلاقة بين الصور الذهنية غير صحيح، إذ علاقة اللزوم والتضايف والعلية ونحو ذلك متحققة . قوله : ( إذ لا تعاند إلخ) أي الظاهر أنه لو كان معانداتها بحسب الوجود الظلي لكان من الصور الذهنية ولا تعاند بين الصور الذهنية. قوله : ( نعم إلخ ) إشارة إلى أن أقلية شروط العام ومعانداته تتحقق بين صورتيهما، وإن لم يتحقق العموم والخصوص إذا كان الأعم جزءاً للأخص والأخص معلوماً بالكنه، فإنه حينئذ يكون وجود الأخص في الذهن موقوفاً على تحقق الأعم فيه، فتكون شروطه شروط الأعم مع شروط زائدة له باعتبار جزء آخر، ولأجل هذا قيدنا النفي في قوله بالنسبة إلخ بقولنا كلياً. قوله : ( نعم إذا كان الأعم جزء الأخص إلخ) وقد يقال : العام أكثر أفراداً فيكون الإحساس بها أوفر وفيضانه المترتب على الاستعداد الحاصل من الإحساسات المتعقلة بجزئياته أقرب فيكون أعرف وهذا جار في الذاتي والعرضي إذا كانت أفراده محسوسة.
And his saying: 'In this argument, the conditions of the universal and its obstacles are fewer than the conditions of the particular and its obstacles.' We said: 'That which you mentioned is only relative to their realization, i.e., the realization of the universal and the particular, in individuals (هويات), for universality and particularity only attach to a thing by virtue of that. Thus the universal is realized in more individuals and instances, and the particular in fewer instances. So when things are ordered in universality and particularity, like substance relative to the species of man, nay, to his kind, then everything that is a condition for the realization of the universal or an obstacle to it is a condition for the realization of the particular, or...' His saying: 'It is only relative to their realization' i.e., absolutely as the arguer intends. His saying: 'In individuals' i.e., instances; he did not say 'in external existence' to include the universal and particular which are mental matters like psychological qualities. In the argument and vice versa, it would be circular, for we deny the dependence of knowledge of the more known on knowledge of the self-evident, unless he intends dependence in terms of knowledge, so reflect. An obstacle to it: for if the universal is not realized in an individual, the particular is not realized in it without the reverse, since the universal may be realized in an individual other than the individual of the particular. No, relative to their realization in the mind, for there is no relation between the two mental images in terms of their realization in the mind, so it is possible that the image of the particular occurs in it without the image of the universal, and there is no obstacle between mental images; rather they are compatible. Do you not see that the opposite is more present in the mind with the opposite than without it? Yes, if the universal is a part of the particular and the particular is known per se (بالكنه), then the condition for the realization of the universal in the mind is a condition for the realization of the particular in it, and likewise an obstacle to the realization of the universal in it, if there is an obstacle to the realization of the particular in it, without complete reversal. His saying: 'For if the universal is not realized etc.' means it is impossible for any particular to be realized without the realization of the universal. So what the realization of the universal depends on in terms of conditions and removal of obstacles is depended upon by every particular. And the universal may be realized without any particular in an individual of another particular, so what any particular depends on is not depended upon for the realization of the universal, even if it is concurrent with it, based on the fact that the universal has no existence except within the particular, otherwise it would not be realized in an individual of another particular. Thus what the realization of the universal depends on is less than what the particular depends on. This is how it should be understood. His saying: 'No, relative to their realization in the mind' i.e., what you mentioned about the fewer conditions of the universal or its obstacles is not absolute relative to their realization in the mind, i.e., in shadowy existence, because that fewerness was only due to the relation of universality and particularity, as you mentioned, and there is no relation of universality and particularity between the two mental images of the universal and the particular in terms of shadowy existence; rather they are distinct, for the image of the universal is distinct from the image of the particular and is not predicated of it. And by what we have clarified for you, the refutation of what was said—that denying the genus of relation between mental images is incorrect, for relations of entailment, correlation, causality, etc. are realized—becomes evident. His saying: 'For there is no obstacle etc.' i.e., the apparent meaning is that if there were obstacles to them in terms of shadowy existence, they would be among mental images, and there is no obstacle between mental images. His saying: 'Yes etc.' is an indication that the fewerness of conditions of the universal and its obstacles is realized between their two images, even if universality and particularity are not realized, if the universal is a part of the particular and the particular is known per se. For then the existence of the particular in the mind is dependent on the realization of the universal in it, so its conditions are conditions of the universal with additional conditions for it due to another part. For this reason we qualified the negation in his saying 'relative to' etc. with the word 'absolutely'. His saying: 'Yes, if the universal is a part of the particular etc.' And it may be said: The universal has more individuals, so sensation of them is more abundant, and its emanation consequent upon the disposition resulting from the sensations connected to its particulars is nearer, so it is more known. And this applies to both essential and accidental universals if their individuals are sensible.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage responds to another argument that the conditions for the universal are fewer than for the particular, implying the universal is more known. The response distinguishes between realization in external individuals (هويات) and in the mind. In external existence, the universal is realized in more individuals, so its conditions are indeed fewer. But in the mind, there is no relationship of generality and specificity between the mental images of the universal and particular; they are separate. Therefore, the argument does not hold for mental existence. The passage discusses nuances: if the universal is a part of the particular and the particular is known per se (بالكنه), then the conditions in the mind may apply. It also addresses the objection that there is no relation between mental images, noting that relational concepts do exist, but the point is about the specific relation of generality. The commentary ends with an observation that the universal, having more individuals, might be more easily sensed and thus more known, which is a different point.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument that the universal has fewer conditions applies universally, regardless of domain.
Correction: The text shows it only applies to realization in external individuals (هويات), not in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to the response, the argument about fewer conditions holds for realization in what?
A) In the mind
B) In individuals (هويات)
C) In both
D) In neither

Key Takeaway
The argument that the universal has fewer conditions is valid only for realization in external individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you debate matters of faith, do you ever assume that a reasoning that works in one domain applies everywhere? Think of a specific example from your own arguments. How will you check the domain of your reasoning from now on?
?
The passage distinguishes between mental images and external reality. Is there a belief you hold that exists only in your mind without a solid external foundation? What would it take for you to ground it in reality?
?
If the universal has fewer conditions externally, does that change how you view the necessity of God's existence compared to contingent things? Examine your own conviction: is it built on the right kind of conditions?

The Mental Domain: No Generality Relation
Denial of argument for mental domain; exception explained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument about conditions does not apply to mental images, except when the universal is a part of the particular known per se.

Core Idea
In the mind, universal and particular images are distinct, so no fewer conditions; only if the universal is a component and the particular is known per se does the logic apply.

The previous lesson showed the argument holds for external existence. Here, we see that in the mental domain, there is no relation of generality between the images, so the argument fails. However, an exception arises when the universal is a part of the particular and the particular is known per se.

Guided Path
1
Read the denial for mental existence
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the explicit denial that the argument holds in the mind.
The key phrase is 'لا' (no) at the beginning.
2
Understand why no relation
Mental images of universal and particular are distinct (متباينة); there is no relation of generality between them.
Contrast with external existence where they are related.
3
Examine the exception
If the universal is a part of the particular and the particular is known per se (بالكنه), then conditions of the universal do apply to the particular in the mind.
This is a special case.

Source Text

شروط زائدة له باعتبار جزء آخر، ولأجل هذا قيدنا النفي في قوله بالنسبة إلخ بقولنا كلياً. قوله : ( نعم إذا كان الأعم جزء الأخص إلخ) وقد يقال : العام أكثر أفراداً فيكون الإحساس بها أوفر وفيضانه المترتب على الاستعداد الحاصل من الإحساسات المتعقلة بجزئياته أقرب فيكون أعرف وهذا جار في الذاتي والعرضي إذا كانت أفراده محسوسة.
And his saying: 'In this argument, the conditions of the universal and its obstacles are fewer than the conditions of the particular and its obstacles.' We said: 'That which you mentioned is only relative to their realization, i.e., the realization of the universal and the particular, in individuals (هويات), for universality and particularity only attach to a thing by virtue of that. Thus the universal is realized in more individuals and instances, and the particular in fewer instances. So when things are ordered in universality and particularity, like substance relative to the species of man, nay, to his kind, then everything that is a condition for the realization of the universal or an obstacle to it is a condition for the realization of the particular, or...' His saying: 'It is only relative to their realization' i.e., absolutely as the arguer intends. His saying: 'In individuals' i.e., instances; he did not say 'in external existence' to include the universal and particular which are mental matters like psychological qualities. In the argument and vice versa, it would be circular, for we deny the dependence of knowledge of the more known on knowledge of the self-evident, unless he intends dependence in terms of knowledge, so reflect. An obstacle to it: for if the universal is not realized in an individual, the particular is not realized in it without the reverse, since the universal may be realized in an individual other than the individual of the particular. No, relative to their realization in the mind, for there is no relation between the two mental images in terms of their realization in the mind, so it is possible that the image of the particular occurs in it without the image of the universal, and there is no obstacle between mental images; rather they are compatible. Do you not see that the opposite is more present in the mind with the opposite than without it? Yes, if the universal is a part of the particular and the particular is known per se (بالكنه), then the condition for the realization of the universal in the mind is a condition for the realization of the particular in it, and likewise an obstacle to the realization of the universal in it, if there is an obstacle to the realization of the particular in it, without complete reversal. His saying: 'For if the universal is not realized etc.' means it is impossible for any particular to be realized without the realization of the universal. So what the realization of the universal depends on in terms of conditions and removal of obstacles is depended upon by every particular. And the universal may be realized without any particular in an individual of another particular, so what any particular depends on is not depended upon for the realization of the universal, even if it is concurrent with it, based on the fact that the universal has no existence except within the particular, otherwise it would not be realized in an individual of another particular. Thus what the realization of the universal depends on is less than what the particular depends on. This is how it should be understood. His saying: 'No, relative to their realization in the mind' i.e., what you mentioned about the fewer conditions of the universal or its obstacles is not absolute relative to their realization in the mind, i.e., in shadowy existence, because that fewerness was only due to the relation of universality and particularity, as you mentioned, and there is no relation of universality and particularity between the two mental images of the universal and the particular in terms of shadowy existence; rather they are distinct, for the image of the universal is distinct from the image of the particular and is not predicated of it. And by what we have clarified for you, the refutation of what was said—that denying the genus of relation between mental images is incorrect, for relations of entailment, correlation, causality, etc. are realized—becomes evident. His saying: 'For there is no obstacle etc.' i.e., the apparent meaning is that if there were obstacles to them in terms of shadowy existence, they would be among mental images, and there is no obstacle between mental images. His saying: 'Yes etc.' is an indication that the fewerness of conditions of the universal and its obstacles is realized between their two images, even if universality and particularity are not realized, if the universal is a part of the particular and the particular is known per se. For then the existence of the particular in the mind is dependent on the realization of the universal in it, so its conditions are conditions of the universal with additional conditions for it due to another part. For this reason we qualified the negation in his saying 'relative to' etc. with the word 'absolutely'. His saying: 'Yes, if the universal is a part of the particular etc.' And it may be said: The universal has more individuals, so sensation of them is more abundant, and its emanation consequent upon the disposition resulting from the sensations connected to its particulars is nearer, so it is more known. And this applies to both essential and accidental universals if their individuals are sensible.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage responds to another argument that the conditions for the universal are fewer than for the particular, implying the universal is more known. The response distinguishes between realization in external individuals (هويات) and in the mind. In external existence, the universal is realized in more individuals, so its conditions are indeed fewer. But in the mind, there is no relationship of generality and specificity between the mental images of the universal and particular; they are separate. Therefore, the argument does not hold for mental existence. The passage discusses nuances: if the universal is a part of the particular and the particular is known per se (بالكنه), then the conditions in the mind may apply. It also addresses the objection that there is no relation between mental images, noting that relational concepts do exist, but the point is about the specific relation of generality. The commentary ends with an observation that the universal, having more individuals, might be more easily sensed and thus more known, which is a different point.

وجود ظلي
بالكنه
أعم
أخص

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mental images have the same logical relations of generality as external individuals.
Correction: The text asserts that in the mind, universal and particular images are distinct and not related by generality.

Checkpoint
In the mental domain, when does the logic of fewer conditions apply?
A) Always
B) Never
C) Only if the universal is a part of the particular known per se
D) Only if the particular is known per se

Key Takeaway
In the mind, universal and particular images are distinct, so no fewer conditions; the exception is when the universal is a component and the particular is known per se.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that your mental image of a spiritual truth (like 'the mercy of Allah') fully captures its reality? In what specific area of your faith might you be treating your mental construct as if it were the thing itself, rather than recognizing its limitation? What will you do today to remind yourself that your understanding is a shadow, not the essence?
?
The exception in this lesson shows that when a part is known per se, conditions apply. Where in your life have you taken partial knowledge of someone or something and concluded you fully understand them? How does this apply to your relationship with Allah—what aspects of His attributes do you mistakenly think you have grasped completely? Commit to one action that humbles your intellectual pride before Him.

Allah's Existence

The First Objection: Existence Requires Circular Dependence
Presents the first objection and its initial clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first objection argues that existence is inconceivable because its conception depends on non-existence, creating a vicious circle.

Core Idea
Existence's conception supposedly hinges on non-existence—a logical circle.

This lesson explores the first argument from those who deny the self-evidence of existence. They claim that to conceive existence, we must distinguish it from other things, which involves the concept of 'not being other'—a specific negation. This specific negation depends on absolute negation (non-existence), which itself presupposes existence. Thus, they argue, existence conception is circular and impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the opening lines where the objection is presented. Notice the claim that existence is not conceived at all, and the first argument: conception requires distinction from others.
The distinction involves 'not being other', which is a negation.
2
Identify the logical steps of the argument
Trace the chain: conception → distinction → specific negation (not other) → absolute negation (non-existence) → absolute existence. This is the proposed circular dependence.
The objector concludes that existence conception depends on itself, which is impossible.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'taṣawwur bi-l-kunn' (quidditative conception) vs 'taṣawwur bi-l-wajh' (conception by aspect). The objection targets the former.
The commentator clarifies that the dispute is about quidditative conception, not aspectual.
4
Reflect on the problem
Ask yourself: If this argument were valid, would it prove that existence is completely unknowable? The rebuttal will address this.
Think about the difference between conception entailing distinction vs. depending on distinction.
