: ( ابتداء ) قيد بذلك لأنه اللازم من القسمة وأما اشتراكه بين أقسام القسم، فباعتبار قسمة القسم إلى أقسامه ثانياً، فاللازم من قسمة الوجود إلى الواجب، والممكن اشتراكه فيهما، ثم يلزم من قسمة الممكن إلى الجوهر والعرض قسمة الوجود إليهما بواسطة هذه القسمة الثانوية، وهكذا فالتقييد المذكور بيان للواقع، وليس احترازياً وما نقل عنه قدس سره عنه قدس سره في حاشية أنه احتراز عن التقسيم ثانياً كقولنا : الحيوان إما أبيض أو أسود والأبيض إما حيوان أو غيره فإن تقسيم الأبيض تقسيم للحيوان وهو ليس بمشترك بين جميع أقسامه، وهذا الاحتراز على ظاهر ما قاله القوم : من أن قسم الشيء قد يكون أعم منه، فلعله منتحل إلى الشارح، وليس منه أما أولاً : فلفساده في نفسه، فإن تقسيم الأبيض إلى الحيوان وغيره ليس تقسيماً للحيوان أصلاً ، والألزم تقسيم الشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره، نعم لو قسم الأبيض إلى الإنسان وغيره كان كذلك، وأما ثانياً فلقوله : لأن حقيقة التقسيم إلخ فإنه يقتضي أن يكون المقسم مشتركاً في كل قسمة وأما ثالثاً فلان اللازم حينئذ اشتراك الوجود بين الأقسام الأولية، فلا يثبت المدعى أعني اشتراكه بين جميع الوجودات . مبني قوله : ( ينقسم إليها ابتداء) إشارة إلى ما اشتهر من جواز كون القسم أعم من المقسم من وجه كما في تقسيم الحيوان إلى الأبيض والأسود، ثم تقسيم كل منهما إلى الفرس والحجر، فلا يلزم اشتراك المقسم بين الأقسام وبهذا تبين أن قول الشارح، وهكذا نقسمه إلى وجودات الأنواع وأشخاصها مما لا بد منه إذ يورد على تقرير المصنف أن اللازم على تقدير التسليم اشتراك الوجود بين الأقسام الأولية التي هي وجودات الواجب والجوهر والعرض لا بين وجودات أقسام الجوهر، وأقسام العرض مع أن المدعى اشتراكه بين الجميع، والحق أن قوله ابتداء لظهور الاشتراك بين الأقسام الأولية، لا لأن وجوب الاشتراك فيها فقط لأن دليله أعني قوله : لأن حقيقة التقسيم ضم مختص إلى مشترك يفيد اشتراكه بين الأقسام مطلقاً، وذلك لأن القسم في المثال المذكور بينهما لفظاً ( لأنّا نقول : هذه يعني قسمة الوجود (قسمة عقلية لا تتوقف على وضع) ) والعلم به، ولذلك لا تختلف باللغات ( المتفاوتة (ويمكن) فيها ( الحصر العقلي ) الدائر بين النفي والإثبات ( بخلاف ذلك ) الذي ذكرتم من التقسيم للاشتراك اللفظي كتقسيم العين، فإنه موقوف على الوضع والعلم ،به ويختلف بحسب اختلاف اللغات، ولا يمكن فيه الحصر العقلي فالاشتراك المعنوي واجب في القسمة العقلية هذا
Or we divide existence into these entire existents, for the outcome in the two divisions is one. And the subject of division is shared among all its parts to which it is primarily divided, because the reality of division is joining a specific to a shared. It cannot be said: The division of existence into what you mentioned is due to verbal ambiguity (ishtirak lafzi) just as 'al-ayn' is divided into the spring and the seeing eye because it is shared (mushtarak) for existence according to the shaykh, so its sharing does not entail the sharing of existence, so it is nothing, for by existence is meant only being in external entities and its sharing has been established. If it is said: This is not existence but a necessary concomitant, then the dispute becomes verbal. Likewise it is said: This is a hesitation (tardid), not a division, according to the shaykh because hesitation does not realize except one of the hesitating matters, and here it is not so. His statement: 'Or we divide the existent etc.' means that the pronoun 'we divide it' is either for existence, so the speech is on deletion of the genitive, or for the existent considering its prior mention by estimation. His statement: 'For the outcome etc.' is because the division of the derivative by virtue of the root of derivation necessitates its division. His statement: 'Primarily' is a restriction because it is necessary from the division. As for its sharing among the parts of the division, it is by virtue of the secondary division; thus the necessary entailment from the division of existence into the necessary and the possible is its sharing between them, then from the division of the possible into substance and accident the division of existence into them by means of this secondary division, and so on. So the mentioned restriction is a clarification of the actual fact, not a precaution. And what is transmitted from him (may his secret be sanctified) in a gloss that it is a precaution against secondary division such as our saying 'animal is either white or black and white is either animal or other' — for the division of white is a division of animal, and it is not shared among all its parts. This precaution is according to the apparent meaning of what the people said: that the part of a thing may be more general than it. Perhaps it is ascribed to the commentator, but it is not from him. First: because of its corruption in itself, for the division of white into animal and other is not a division of animal at all; rather the necessary is division of the thing into itself and other. Yes, if he divided white into human and other, it would be so. Second: because of his statement: 'for the reality of division etc.' — it requires that the divided be shared in every division. Third: because the necessary entailment then would be the sharing of existence among the primary parts, so the claim — namely its sharing among all existents — would not be established. The phrase 'mabni'? His statement: 'It is divided into them primarily' refers to what is well-known: the permissibility of the part being more general than the divided from one aspect, as in dividing animal into white and black, then dividing each of them into horse and stone — so the sharing of the divided among the parts is not necessitated. By this it becomes clear that the commentator's statement 'and thus we divide it into existences of species and individuals' is indispensable, for it is brought against the author's formulation that the necessary entailment, assuming acceptance, is the sharing of existence among the primary parts which are the existences of the necessary, substance, and accident, not among the existences of the parts of substance and the parts of accident, while the claim is its sharing among all. The truth is that his statement 'primarily' is because of the apparentness of the sharing among the primary parts, not because the obligation of sharing is only in them, for his proof, namely his statement 'for the reality of division is joining a specific to a shared', benefits its sharing among the parts absolutely. And the reason is that the part in the mentioned example is between them verbally (because we say: This — meaning the division of existence — is a rational division (qisma 'aqliyya) that does not depend on linguistic convention) and knowledge of it, and therefore it does not differ according to different languages, and rational enumeration (al-hasr al-'aqli) — which revolves between negation and affirmation — is possible in it, unlike that which you mentioned of the division for verbal ambiguity like the division of 'al-ayn', for it depends on convention and knowledge of it, and differs according to difference of languages, and rational enumeration is not possible in it. So the shared meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi) is necessary in the rational division. This.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage argues that existence (wujud) is shared (mushtarak) among all its divisions in a real sense, not merely verbally. The passage begins with the original claim: the division of existence into necessary, possible, substances, accidents, etc. requires a shared element because every division (taqsim) involves joining a specific (mukhtass) to a shared (mushtarak). The author then anticipates an objection: this division could be like dividing the word 'al-ayn' (which homonymously means 'eye' or 'spring') — a verbal division that does not imply a real shared meaning. The response is that this is a rational division (qisma 'aqliyya), not a verbal one. Rational division does not depend on linguistic convention; it is the same across languages and allows rational enumeration (hasr 'aqli) based on affirmation and negation. Therefore, the shared meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi) is necessary. The rest of the passage is a detailed commentary on the original lines, clarifying the pronoun reference, the function of the word 'primarily' (ibtida'), and refuting a gloss that misinterpreted it as a precaution against secondary divisions. The commentator shows that the logical structure of division demands that the divided term be shared among all its primary parts, and the rational nature of this division secures that the sharing is real, not verbal.

Key Terms
taqsim (division)
ishtirak ma'nawi (shared meaning)
ishtirak lafzi (verbal ambiguity)
qisma 'aqliyya (rational division)
hasr 'aqli (rational enumeration)
mushtarak (homonymous/shared)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the division of existence is merely verbal, like dividing a homonymous word (ishtirak lafzi), and thus does not imply a real shared meaning.
Correction: The division of existence is a rational division (qisma 'aqliyya), which does not depend on linguistic convention and requires a shared meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi) across all its parts.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the division of existence and the division of a homonym like 'al-ayn' according to this passage?
A) Existence is more general than 'al-ayn'.
B) The division of existence is rational (qisma 'aqliyya) while the division of 'al-ayn' is verbal (lafziyya).
C) Existence has many meanings, but 'al-ayn' has only one.
D) The division of existence is based on revelation, while 'al-ayn' is based on language.

Key Takeaway
The division of existence is a rational division requiring a real shared meaning, not a verbal division like homonyms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that 'existence' means the same thing when you say 'I exist' and when you say 'Allah exists'? Or have you unconsciously slipped into verbal ambiguity, using the same word for two unrelated realities? Let this rational foundation pierce any complacency in your affirmation of God.
?
If the division of existence is a rational necessity, what excuse remains for not building your knowledge of God on clear rational proof? Are you content with inherited belief while the very structure of existence demands that you think?
?
When you face doubts about God's existence, do you realize that the shared meaning of existence is not a matter of language but of rational coherence? How will you now hold yourself accountable to think through the categories of existence rather than brushing aside intellectual questions?

The Insufficiency of Interpreted Verbal Division
Al-Jurjani dismisses a refined objection by distinguishing conceptual from real sharedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting a verbal division as a division of the named thing does not establish the real existential commonality required for the argument about existence.

Core Idea
Conceptual sharing does not prove real existential sharing.

A commentator attempted to undermine the earlier argument by suggesting that even a verbal homonym like 'al-ayn' can be interpreted as a division of the named thing, yielding a shared meaning. Al-Jurjani responds that this only achieves a conceptual sharedness, not the real existential unity of existence that the original argument requires. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the interpretation
Carefully read the first part of the Arabic passage where the commentator claims that the division of 'al-ayn' can be reinterpreted as a division of the named thing (musamma). Notice how this is supposed to bring a shared meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi) into the picture.
The objector is trying to rescue the verbal division from being mere hesitation (tardid).
2
Identify Al-Jurjani's counter
Find where Al-Jurjani refutes this: he says that even if we grant the interpretation for 'al-ayn', applying the same to existence only gives a conceptual shared meaning of the term 'existence', not the real sharedness of existence as a single reality in all existents.
3
Understand why this defeats the objection
Reflect on why this counter is decisive: the original argument required existence to be a single reality shared by all existents, not just a common concept. The commentator's move only shifted the level of analysis without addressing the metaphysical claim.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Summarize the lesson's takeaway
Write one sentence summarizing why interpreted verbal divisions fail to prove real existential commonality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قيل التقسيم في مثل العين إنما هو باعتبار تأويله بالمسمى بلفظ العين، فيؤول الاشتراك بالمعنوي ولولا هذا التأويل لكان ترديداً لا تقسيماً، الإشكال لجواز مثل ذلك في الوجود وقد ينقض هذان) الوجهان (بالماهية والتشخص ) فيقال : نحن نجزم بالماهية في ذلك السبب أي نجزم بأن له ماهية ونتردد في خصوصيات الماهيات ونقسم الماهية إلى الخصوصيات، وكذا الحال في قوله : (قسمة عقلية لا تتوقف إلخ إن أريد بالعقلية ما يقابل الاستقرائية فقوله : لا يتوقف ورد أنه يعود إلخ صفة تقييدية وإن أريد بها ما يقابل اللفظية فصفة كاشفة . قوله : ( فالاشتراك المعنوي إلخ وخلاصة الجواب تخصيص القسمة، فالاستدلال بالقسمة العقلية وتسليم ما قاله المعترض من عدم الاشتراك في القسمة اللفظية. قوله : ( وقد قيل إلخ قائله : شارح حكمة العين أي في الجواب عن الاعتراض المذكور، وحاصله إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة بإبطال السند المساوي، بأن التقسيم في صورة الاشتراك أيضاً، يستدعي الأشتراك المعنوي، إذ لولا ذلك لكان ترديداً إذ الفرق بين التقسيم والترديد، إنما هو بوجود القدر المشترك في التقسيم دون الترديد . اللفظي قوله : ( ورد إلخ) يعني أن الاشتراك المعنوي الذي أثبته المستدل في صورة الاشتراك اللفظي لا يقلع أصل الإشكال لأن المعترض حينئذ يعود ويقول : يجوز أن يكون تقسيم الوجود أيضاً بهذا التأويل، وهذا الاشتراك المعنوي أعني اشتراك مفهوم المسمى بلفظ الوجود لا يثبت ما هو المقصود أعني اشتراك الوجود بمعنى أنه معنى واحد تشترك فيه الموجودات بأسرها، وهو ظاهر فلا بد من الرجوع إلى ما ذكره المصنف ويكون التزام أن التقسيم للاشتراك اللفظي قسمة معنوية مستدركاً في الجواب . على هذا هو الحيوان الأبيض لا الأبيض مطلقاً، فلا ينقسم إلى الفرس والحجر وما يقال: من أنه قد يكون بين القسم والمقسم عموم من وجه أيضاً، فذلك غلط نشأ من اشتباه القسم بقيده، وقد يتوهم أن الاحتياج إلى ضميمة الشارح باق على هذا التوجيه أيضاً، لأن مقصود المورد أنه لا يلزم من قسمة الوجود إلى وجودات الواجب والجوهر والعرض، اشتراكه بين جميع أفراد الجوهر وأفراد العرض لأن قيد القسم قد يكون أعم من المقسم من وجه كما في مثال الحيوان، والأبيض وإن كان القسم نفسه أخص مطلقاً، وأنت خبير بأن هذا إنما يرد إذا سلم أن المنقسم إلى وجودات أفراد الجوهر والعرض قيد القسم لا نفسه كما في تقسيم الأبيض إلى الإنسان والفرس.
And it has been said: The division in the case of 'al-ayn' is only by virtue of interpreting it as the named thing (musamma) by the word 'al-ayn', so the sharing becomes a shared meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi); and without this interpretation it would be a hesitation (tardid), not a division. The problem is the permissibility of such in the case of existence. And these two aspects may be refuted by quiddity (mahiyya) and individuation (tashakhkhus). For it is said: We are certain of quiddity in that cause, i.e., we are certain that it has a quiddity, and we hesitate about the particularities of quiddities and we divide quiddity into the particularities. Likewise is the case in his statement: 'A rational division that does not depend [on convention] etc.' If by 'rational' is meant what is opposed to inductive, then his statement 'does not depend' etc. is a restrictive attribute; and if it is meant what is opposed to verbal, then it is an explicative attribute. His statement: 'So the shared meaning etc.' The summary of the answer is the specification of the division, so the argument is by the rational division and the acceptance of what the objector said about the lack of sharing in the verbal division. His statement: 'And it has been said etc.' The speaker is the commentator of Hikmat al-'Ayn, i.e., in answer to the mentioned objection, and its gist is establishing the prevented premise by nullifying the equal basis, that the division in the form of verbal ambiguity also demands a shared meaning, for without that it would be hesitation. Indeed the difference between division and hesitation is only the existence of the common element in division, not in hesitation. The verbal. His statement: 'And it is refuted etc.' means that the shared meaning which the arguer established in the form of verbal ambiguity does not uproot the original problem, because the objector then returns and says: It is permissible that the division of existence also be by this interpretation. And this shared meaning — i.e., the sharing of the concept of the named thing by the word 'existence' — does not establish what is intended, i.e., the sharing of existence in the sense that it is a single meaning in which all existents participate. That is obvious, so one must return to what the author mentioned, and the commitment that the division for verbal ambiguity is a meaningful division is redundant in the answer. On this basis, it is the white animal, not white absolutely, so it does not divide into horse and stone. And what is said: that there may be between the part and the divided a generality from one aspect also — that is an error arising from confusing the part with its condition. It may be imagined that the need for the supplementation of the commentator remains on this orientation as well, because the intent of the objector is that from the division of existence into the existences of the necessary, substance, and accident, its sharing among all individuals of substance and all individuals of accident is not necessitated, because the condition of the part may be more general than the divided from one aspect, as in the example of animal and white, even though the part itself is absolutely more specific. And you well know that this only applies if it is conceded that what is divided into the existences of individuals of substance and accident is the condition of the part, not the part itself, as in the division of white into human and horse.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection and its resolution. It begins with a statement from a commentator (Sharh Hikmat al-'Ayn) who claimed that even the division of a homonym like 'al-ayn' can be considered a division of the named thing (musamma) rather than the word, yielding a shared meaning. This would undermine the earlier argument that verbal division lacks shared meaning. However, the author (al-Jurjani) responds that even if we grant this interpretation for 'al-ayn', applying the same to existence would only give us a shared concept of the term 'existence', not the real existential commonality (i.e., existence as a single reality in all existents). Thus the original problem remains. The author then discusses a related issue: the division of existence into necessary, substance, and accident might not guarantee sharing among all individuals of substance and accident because the condition (qayd) of the part could be more general than the divided. But he clarifies that this confusion arises from mistaking the part itself for its condition. The overall point is that the rational division of existence necessarily implies a real shared meaning, and attempts to reduce it to a verbal interpretation fail to establish the required metaphysical unity.

Key Terms
ta'wil (interpretation)
musamma (named thing)
ishtirak ma'nawi (shared meaning)
tardid (hesitation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that interpreting a verbal division as a division of the named thing suffices to prove a shared meaning that undermines the original argument.
Correction: This only yields a conceptual shared meaning, not the real existential commonality that the argument requires.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, why does the objection based on interpreting the division of 'al-ayn' fail to refute the original argument about existence?
A) Because the interpretation is invalid for 'al-ayn'.
B) Because the interpretation only provides a conceptual shared meaning, not the real existential unity required.
C) Because existence is not a homonym like 'al-ayn'.
D) Because the commentator of Hikmat al-'Ayn was mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Interpreting a verbal division as a division of the named thing yields only a conceptual shared meaning, not the real existential commonality necessary for the argument about existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you can intellectually categorize a religious concept (like God's mercy or omnipotence), you have grasped its full reality? How might this lesson push you to seek a deeper, existential engagement with the divine attributes, rather than settling for conceptual labels?
?
Consider a theological belief you hold passionately. Is your conviction rooted in the conceptual coherence of the words, or in a direct, lived awareness of that reality? What would it take for your belief to move from a shared meaning in your mind to an actual transformation of your heart and actions?
?
The passage warns that interpreting concepts can give a false sense of certainty. In your own study of aqeedah, where might you be more concerned with 'correct' definitions than with the real, existential weight of what you affirm? What specific truth about Allah do you need to stop merely analyzing and start living as if it were absolutely true?

The Part vs. Condition Distinction in Rational Division
Al-Jurjani corrects a misunderstanding about the part and its condition in rational divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the part (qism) and its condition (qayd) in rational divisions, and why an objection that the condition is broader than the divided is invalid.

Core Idea
The part itself is always more specific than the divided; only its condition may be broader.

A further objection arises: in a rational division, the condition (qayd) attached to the part might be more general than the divided, which would undermine the argument for a real shared meaning. Al-Jurjani clarifies that this confusion stems from mistaking the part itself for its condition. The part is always more specific, but the condition can be broader. This lesson explains this subtle but crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about the condition
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'على هذا هو الحيوان الأبيض...' and its translation. The objector claims that the condition of the part (e.g., white in 'white animal') could be more general than the divided (animal), so the argument about existence might fail.
The example given: 'white animal' divides into horse and stone? Actually, it says it does not divide into horse and stone because white animal is not white absolutely.
2
Identify al-Jurjani's correction
Al-Jurjani states that this objection is based on an error: confusing the part itself (qism) with its condition (qayd). The part itself is always absolutely more specific than the divided, but the condition may be more general from one aspect.
3
Understand the implication for the main argument
Recognize that this clarification defends the original argument that the rational division of existence implies real shared meaning. The objection fails because it misidentifies the part.
4
Summarize the lesson
Write one sentence explaining why the part-condition distinction is important for the overall discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قيل التقسيم في مثل العين إنما هو باعتبار تأويله بالمسمى بلفظ العين، فيؤول الاشتراك بالمعنوي ولولا هذا التأويل لكان ترديداً لا تقسيماً، الإشكال لجواز مثل ذلك في الوجود وقد ينقض هذان) الوجهان (بالماهية والتشخص ) فيقال : نحن نجزم بالماهية في ذلك السبب أي نجزم بأن له ماهية ونتردد في خصوصيات الماهيات ونقسم الماهية إلى الخصوصيات، وكذا الحال في قوله : (قسمة عقلية لا تتوقف إلخ إن أريد بالعقلية ما يقابل الاستقرائية فقوله : لا يتوقف ورد أنه يعود إلخ صفة تقييدية وإن أريد بها ما يقابل اللفظية فصفة كاشفة . قوله : ( فالاشتراك المعنوي إلخ وخلاصة الجواب تخصيص القسمة، فالاستدلال بالقسمة العقلية وتسليم ما قاله المعترض من عدم الاشتراك في القسمة اللفظية. قوله : ( وقد قيل إلخ قائله : شارح حكمة العين أي في الجواب عن الاعتراض المذكور، وحاصله إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة بإبطال السند المساوي، بأن التقسيم في صورة الاشتراك أيضاً، يستدعي الأشتراك المعنوي، إذ لولا ذلك لكان ترديداً إذ الفرق بين التقسيم والترديد، إنما هو بوجود القدر المشترك في التقسيم دون الترديد . اللفظي قوله : ( ورد إلخ) يعني أن الاشتراك المعنوي الذي أثبته المستدل في صورة الاشتراك اللفظي لا يقلع أصل الإشكال لأن المعترض حينئذ يعود ويقول : يجوز أن يكون تقسيم الوجود أيضاً بهذا التأويل، وهذا الاشتراك المعنوي أعني اشتراك مفهوم المسمى بلفظ الوجود لا يثبت ما هو المقصود أعني اشتراك الوجود بمعنى أنه معنى واحد تشترك فيه الموجودات بأسرها، وهو ظاهر فلا بد من الرجوع إلى ما ذكره المصنف ويكون التزام أن التقسيم للاشتراك اللفظي قسمة معنوية مستدركاً في الجواب . على هذا هو الحيوان الأبيض لا الأبيض مطلقاً، فلا ينقسم إلى الفرس والحجر وما يقال: من أنه قد يكون بين القسم والمقسم عموم من وجه أيضاً، فذلك غلط نشأ من اشتباه القسم بقيده، وقد يتوهم أن الاحتياج إلى ضميمة الشارح باق على هذا التوجيه أيضاً، لأن مقصود المورد أنه لا يلزم من قسمة الوجود إلى وجودات الواجب والجوهر والعرض، اشتراكه بين جميع أفراد الجوهر وأفراد العرض لأن قيد القسم قد يكون أعم من المقسم من وجه كما في مثال الحيوان، والأبيض وإن كان القسم نفسه أخص مطلقاً، وأنت خبير بأن هذا إنما يرد إذا سلم أن المنقسم إلى وجودات أفراد الجوهر والعرض قيد القسم لا نفسه كما في تقسيم الأبيض إلى الإنسان والفرس.
And it has been said: The division in the case of 'al-ayn' is only by virtue of interpreting it as the named thing (musamma) by the word 'al-ayn', so the sharing becomes a shared meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi); and without this interpretation it would be a hesitation (tardid), not a division. Commentary
This passage presents a further objection and its resolution. It begins with a statement from a commentator (Sharh Hikmat al-'Ayn) who claimed that even the division of a homonym like 'al-ayn' can be considered a division of the named thing (musamma) rather than the word, yielding a shared meaning. Key Terms
qism (part)
qayd (condition)
ta'ammum (generality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that in a rational division, the condition attached to a part can be more general than the divided, thus undermining the requirement that the part is more specific.
Correction: This confuses the part itself with its condition. The part is always more specific; the condition may be more general, but that does not affect the part's specificity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what is the error in the objection that the condition of the part can be more general than the divided?
A) The condition is always more specific than the divided.
B) The part itself is absolutely more specific; the condition may be more general, but that does not affect the argument.
C) The divided is always more general than both the part and its condition.
D) The objection does not apply to existence because existence has no conditions.

Key Takeaway
The part in a rational division is always more specific than the divided; confusing the part with its condition leads to a false objection about generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Jurjani’s precision shows that a small logical slip can undermine an entire argument. Where in your own understanding of Allah's existence or attributes have you accepted a common error because you failed to notice a similar confusion? Write down one such area and seek clarity from the source.

Proof from Rational Division of Existence
Proof that existence is common based on rational division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational division of existence proves that existence is a common (mushtarak) concept and how it differs from conventional division.

Core Idea
Rational division proves existence is one shared meaning.

This lesson examines the first proof for the commonality of existence. The commentator argues that the division of existence into necessary, substance, and accident is a rational division, not dependent on language. This distinction is crucial because it shows that existence must have a single common meaning that the mind can grasp regardless of language. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between 'rational division' and 'conventional division'.
2
Identify the two types of division
The passage contrasts rational division (like of existence) with conventional division (like of 'ayn). List their characteristics as given in the text.
Rational division does not depend on linguistic convention and allows rational enumeration.
3
Examine the objection and its response
The passage raises an objection about the division of 'ayn possibly being interpreted as having a common meaning. Ask yourself why this objection does not affect the proof.
The key is that the division of existence is inherently rational, not dependent on interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في أنه مشترك بينهما لفظاً ( لأنّا نقول : هذه يعني قسمة الوجود (قسمة عقلية لا تتوقف على وضع) ) والعلم به، ولذلك لا تختلف باللغات ( المتفاوتة (ويمكن) فيها ( الحصر العقلي ) الدائر بين النفي والإثبات ( بخلاف ذلك ) الذي ذكرتم من التقسيم للاشتراك اللفظي كتقسيم العين، فإنه موقوف على الوضع والعلم ،به ويختلف بحسب اختلاف اللغات، ولا يمكن فيه الحصر العقلي فالاشتراك المعنوي واجب في القسمة العقلية هذا، وقد قيل التقسيم في مثل العين إنما هو باعتبار تأويله بالمسمى بلفظ العين، فيؤول الاشتراك بالمعنوي ولولا هذا التأويل لكان ترديداً لا تقسيماً، الإشكال لجواز مثل ذلك في الوجود
The first observation - the second objective: that it (existence) is shared in wording (because we say: this, meaning the division of existence, is a rational division that does not depend on convention) and knowledge of it; therefore it does not differ with different languages, and rational enumeration is possible in it, restricted to negation and affirmation, unlike that (division) which you mentioned of the division into equivocal sharing, like the division of 'ayn (eye/spring), for it depends on convention and knowledge of it, and it differs according to the difference of languages, and rational enumeration is not possible in it. So commonality of meaning is necessary in rational division. And it was said that the division in something like 'ayn is only by considering its interpretation as the thing named by the word 'ayn, so it reduces to commonality of meaning, and were it not for this interpretation it would be equivocation (tardid) and not division. The problem is the permissibility of the like of that in existence.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the commonality (ishtirak) of existence. It states that the division of existence into the existences of the Necessary, substance, and accident is a rational division. A rational division has two characteristics: it does not depend on linguistic convention (waḍ'), so it does not vary with languages; and rational enumeration (al-ḥaṣr al-ʿaqli) by affirmation and negation is possible in it. This is contrasted with the division of an equivocal term like 'ayn (which can mean eye, spring, etc.), which depends on convention, varies with languages, and cannot be rationally enumerated. Therefore, since the division of existence is rational, the concept being divided must be one common meaning (maʿnan wāḥid mushtarak). Otherwise, the division would be impossible. The passage then mentions a contention: it is possible that the division of 'ayn could also be interpreted as involving a common meaning (the thing named by the word 'ayn), which would make it similar to the division of existence. The commentator notes that this contention is addressed by the author's original argument that the division is rational and does not depend on convention, so the opponent's equivocal division does not affect the proof.

Key Terms
قسمة عقلية
حصر عقلي
اشتراك معنوي
اشتراك لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational division depends on linguistic convention.
Correction: Rational division, like that of existence, does not depend on convention; it is based on the inherent meaning that the mind grasps regardless of language.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof from rational division require existence to be common (mushtarak)?
A) Because the division of existence is conventional.
B) Because rational division does not vary with languages and allows rational enumeration, implying a single shared concept.
C) Because existence is an equivocal term like 'ayn.
D) Because the division of existence is based on linguistic convention.

Key Takeaway
Existence must be a common (mushtarak) concept because its rational division does not rely on linguistic convention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists' and 'you exist', do you truly believe existence is one shared meaning, or do you unconsciously treat it as two different senses? This lesson demands you investigate your own linguistic assumptions. Will you now commit to using the word 'existence' with the awareness that it signifies a single reality common to both Creator and creation?
?
Your daily speech is filled with words that carry multiple conventions (like 'seeing', 'knowledge', 'power'). Does this lesson's rational division method offer you a tool to purify your understanding of divine attributes? How will you apply this principle of rational division to other terms you ascribe to Allah?
?
If you were to encounter someone who insists that existence is purely equivocal (like 'ayn'), what specific statement from this lesson would you use to press them to reconsider? Write down that statement now and commit to using it as a reminder for yourself when slip into thinking of existence as merely a word.

Allah's Existence
intermediate
8 min
Objection Using Mahiyya and Tashakhkhus and the Distinction of Rational Division
Objection and initial response distinguishing two meanings of rational division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector uses quiddity (māhiyya) and individuality (tashakhkhuṣ) to challenge the argument from rational division, and how the author responds by distinguishing two senses of 'rational division'.

Core Idea
The type of rational division determines whether commonality is implied.

The objector presents a counterexample: we are certain that things have a quiddity and individuality, yet these are divided into particularities. This seems to mirror the division of existence. The author's response hinges on clarifying what 'rational division' means. In this lesson, we examine the objection and the initial distinction between two senses of rational division.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objector argues using quiddity and individuality. Notice how he draws an analogy to the division of existence.
2
Identify the two senses of rational division
The author then discusses the phrase 'rational division' and gives two interpretations: one opposed to inductive, and one opposed to verbal. Mark how each leads to a different description of the division.
This distinction is key to understanding the response.
3
Understand the summary of the answer
The passage ends with the summary: the argument relies on rational division in the sense of verbal division, and the objector's point about verbal division not implying commonality is admitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ينقض هذان الوجهان بالماهية والتشخص فيقال : نحن نجزم بالماهية في ذلك السبب أي نجزم بأن له ماهية ونتردد في خصوصيات الماهيات ونقسم الماهية إلى الخصوصيات، وكذا الحال في قوله : (قسمة عقلية لا تتوقف إلخ إن أريد بالعقلية ما يقابل الاستقرائية فقوله : لا يتوقف ورد أنه إلخ صفة تقييدية وإن أريد بها ما يقابل اللفظية فصفة كاشفة . قوله : ( فالاشتراك المعنوي إلخ وخلاصة الجواب تخصيص القسمة، فالاستدلال بالقسمة العقلية وتسليم ما قاله المعترض من عدم الاشتراك في القسمة اللفظية. قوله : ( وقد قيل إلخ قائله : شارح حكمة العين أي في الجواب عن الاعتراض المذكور، وحاصله إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة بإبطال السند المساوي، بأن التقسيم في صورة الاشتراك أيضاً، يستدعي الأشتراك المعنوي، إذ لولا ذلك لكان ترديداً إذ الفرق بين التقسيم والترديد، إنما هو بوجود القدر المشترك في التقسيم دون الترديد . اللفظي قوله : ( ورد إلخ) يعني أن الاشتراك المعنوي الذي أثبته المستدل في صورة الاشتراك اللفظي لا يقلع أصل الإشكال لأن المعترض حينئذ يعود ويقول : يجوز أن يكون تقسيم الوجود أيضاً بهذا التأويل، وهذا الاشتراك المعنوي أعني اشتراك مفهوم المسمى بلفظ الوجود لا يثبت ما هو المقصود أعني اشتراك الوجود بمعنى أنه معنى واحد تشترك فيه الموجودات بأسرها، وهو ظاهر فلا بد من الرجوع إلى ما ذكره المصنف ويكون التزام أن التقسيم للاشتراك اللفظي قسمة معنوية مستدركاً في الجواب . على هذا هو الحيوان الأبيض لا الأبيض مطلقاً، فلا ينقسم إلى الفرس والحجر وما يقال: من أنه قد يكون بين القسم والمقسم عموم من وجه أيضاً، فذلك غلط نشأ من اشتباه القسم بقيده، وقد يتوهم أن الاحتياج إلى ضميمة الشارح باق على هذا التوجيه أيضاً، لأن مقصود المورد أنه لا يلزم من قسمة الوجود إلى وجودات الواجب والجوهر والعرض، اشتراكه بين جميع أفراد الجوهر وأفراد العرض لأن قيد القسم قد يكون أعم من المقسم من وجه كما في مثال الحيوان، والأبيض وإن كان القسم نفسه أخص مطلقاً، وأنت خبير بأن هذا إنما يرد إذا سلم أن المنقسم إلى وجودات أفراد الجوهر والعرض قيد القسم لا نفسه كما في تقسيم الأبيض إلى الإنسان والفرس. التشخص، فيلزم كون الماهية والتشخص مشتركين وهو باطل لأن الماهيات متخالفة الحقائق والتشخصات متميزة، فلا تكون مشتركة بل متخالفة الهويات ( والتحقيق أنه إن أريد مجرد الاشتراك ) أي إن أريد من الاستدلال بهذين الوجهين مجرد أن الوجود معنى واحد مشترك بين الموجودات سواء أكانت أفراده متماثلة في الحقيقة أو لا (فهما) أي مفهوما الماهية والتشخص (أيضاً عارضان للماهيات المخصوصة والتشخصات الجزئية ) مشتركان بينهما وإن كانت أفرادهما متخالفة الحقائق والهويات فلا نقض بهما ( وإن أريد التماثل في الوجود أي إن أريد أنه مشترك وأفراده متماثلة متفقة في الحقيقة (فلا يلزم هذا المراد من هذين الوجهين ) والنقض (بهما أي بالماهية والتشخص ) وارد ) عليهما لأن أفرادهما متخالفة لا متماثلة، وأنت خبير بأن المتبادر من دعوى الاشتراك مطلقاً هو المعنى الأول .
And these two aspects may be refuted by quiddity and individuality, so it is said: We are certain of quiddity in that cause, i.e. we are certain that it has a quiddity, and we are uncertain about the particularities of quiddities, and we divide quiddity into particularities. And likewise is the case in his saying: (A rational division that does not depend etc. If by rational is meant what is opposed to inductive, then his saying: does not depend [on convention] is a restrictive description, and if by it is meant what is opposed to verbal, then it is a revealing description. His saying: (So the commonality of meaning etc.) and the summary of the answer is the specification of the division, so the argument is by the rational division and the admission of what the objector said about the lack of commonality in the verbal division. His saying: (And it was said etc.) its speaker is the commentator of Hikmat al-‘Ayn, i.e. in answer to the mentioned objection, and its summary is the proof of the prevented premise by invalidating the equal ground, that the division in the form of commonality also demands commonality of meaning, for otherwise it would be equivocation, since the difference between division and equivocation is only by the existence of the common factor in division without equivocation. The verbal: His saying: (And it was rebutted etc.) means that the commonality of meaning which the arguer established in the form of verbal commonality does not remove the original problem, because the objector then returns and says: It is permissible that the division of existence also be by this interpretation, and this commonality of meaning, i.e. the commonality of the concept of what is named by the word existence, does not prove what is intended, i.e. the commonality of existence in the sense that it is a single meaning in which all existents share, and this is apparent, so there is no escape from returning to what the author mentioned, and the commitment that the division for verbal commonality is a meaningful division is superfluous in the answer. On this basis, it is the white animal, not the white absolutely, so it is not divided into horse and stone. And what is said: that there may be a general-specific relationship between the divided and the division in some respects, that is an error arising from confusing the division with its restriction. And it may be imagined that the need for the commentator's supplement remains on this interpretation as well, because the purpose of the objector is that from the division of existence into existences of the Necessary, substance, and accident, its commonality does not follow among all individuals of substance and all individuals of accident, because the restriction of the division may be more general than the divided in some respects, as in the example of animal and white, even though the division itself is absolutely more specific. And you are aware that this only applies if it is admitted that what is divided into existences of individuals of substance and accident is the restriction of the division, not itself, as in the division of white into man and horse. Individuality, so it follows that quiddity and individuality are common, and that is false because quiddities have different realities and individualities are distinguished, so they are not common but rather have different identities. (And the investigation is that if mere commonality is intended), i.e. if what is intended from the argument by these two aspects is merely that existence is a single meaning common among existents, whether its individuals are similar in reality or not, (then the two) i.e. the concepts of quiddity and individuality (are also accidents for specific quiddities and particular individualities) common among them, even if their individuals have different realities and identities, so there is no refutation by them. (And if similarity in existence is intended), i.e. if it is intended that it is common and its individuals are similar, agreeing in reality, (then this intention does not follow from these two aspects), and the refutation (by them, i.e. by quiddity and individuality, is valid) against them, because their individuals are different, not similar. And you are aware that what is understood from the claim of commonality absolutely is the first meaning.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof from rational division and the commentator's detailed response. The objector argues using quiddity (māhiyya) and individuality (tashakhkhuṣ) as counterexamples. He says: we are certain that a thing has a quiddity, yet we hesitate among particular quiddities and divide quiddity into particularities. Similarly, we have a rational division of quiddity, which would then imply quiddity is common, but quiddities differ in reality, so commonality is false. The commentator then clarifies the author's response. First, he distinguishes two senses of 'rational division': either opposed to inductive (taqribī) or opposed to verbal (lafẓī). Then he states the answer: restrict the division to rational division as opposed to verbal, and admit the opponent's point about verbal division not implying commonality. He then discusses a further response: the division in the case of equivocal terms like 'ayn can be interpreted as a meaningful division (if we take the concept of the thing named), but even that does not prove the intended commonality of existence as a single meaning shared by all existents. The real response is to return to the author's original argument. Then the commentator addresses the objection about quiddity and individuality in detail. He offers two interpretations of what is meant by 'commonality': (1) mere sharing of a concept (ishtirāk ma'nawī), in which case quiddity and individuality are also common, so there is no refutation; (2) similarity of individuals (tamāthul), in which case the objection is valid, but this is not what is intended from the original argument. The commentator concludes that the common understanding of commonality is the first meaning (mere sharing of a concept).

Key Terms
الماهية
التشخص
القسمة العقلية
الاشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All rational divisions imply a common meaning shared by the divided items.
Correction: The author distinguishes between rational division in the sense of inductive (which may not imply commonality) and rational division in the sense of verbal (which does imply commonality). Only the latter supports the argument for commonality of existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the author mean by 'rational division' in the argument?
A) A division that does not depend on convention, opposed to inductive.
B) A division based on common meaning, opposed to verbal.
C) A division based on induction.
D) A division that is both inductive and verbal.

Key Takeaway
The objector's counterexample fails because the division of quiddity is not a rational division in the sense that implies commonality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are certain that your actions have a 'quiddity' (essence), yet you often waver in certainty about the existence of the One who created you. What specific doubt holds you back, and what step will you take today to remove it by reflecting on the arguments for Allah's existence?
?
The objector divides quiddity into particularities and concludes no commonality. In your own thinking, do you sometimes divide matters in a way that obscures the oneness of Allah? Identify one area where you have fragmented your understanding and commit to seeing it through the lens of tawhid.
?
This passage distinguishes two types of rational division—one that leads to commonality and one that does not. When you search for the truth about Allah, which type of reasoning do you rely on? Will you now choose the path that leads to commonality, even if it demands more rigor?

The Reply from the Commentator of Hikmat al-‘Ayn and Its Rebuttal
Commentator's alternative response and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative response to the objection — proving the prevented premise by invalidating the equal ground — and why this response ultimately fails to resolve the problem.

Core Idea
Even if we prove commonality in a different way, the original problem remains.

A commentator offers a different approach: he argues that the division of quiddity also requires commonality, thus invalidating the objector's equal ground. However, the author rebuts that this commonality of meaning does not prove the intended commonality of existence — the specific commonality the arguer needs. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's argument
The passage introduces a statement from the commentator of Hikmat al-‘Ayn. He tries to prove the prevented premise (that division implies commonality) by showing that even the division of quiddity must be based on a common concept.
2
Understand the rebuttal
The author responds that this commonality of concept does not suffice for the argument, because the objector can reinterpret the division of existence in the same way, and it does not prove the intended commonality of existence as a single reality.
3
See the conclusion
The passage concludes that there is no escape but to return to the author's original argument, and that the commentator's commitment is superfluous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof from rational division and the commentator's detailed response. The objector argues using quiddity (māhiyya) and individuality (tashakhkhuṣ) as counterexamples. He says: we are certain that a thing has a quiddity, yet we hesitate among particular quiddities and divide quiddity into particularities. Similarly, we have a rational division of quiddity, which would then imply quiddity is common, but quiddities differ in reality, so commonality is false. The commentator then clarifies the author's response. First, he distinguishes two senses of 'rational division': either opposed to inductive (taqribī) or opposed to verbal (lafẓī). Then he states the answer: restrict the division to rational division as opposed to verbal, and admit the opponent's point about verbal division not implying commonality. He then discusses a further response: the division in the case of equivocal terms like 'ayn can be interpreted as a meaningful division (if we take the concept of the thing named), but even that does not prove the intended commonality of existence as a single meaning shared by all existents. The real response is to return to the author's original argument. Then the commentator addresses the objection about quiddity and individuality in detail. He offers two interpretations of what is meant by 'commonality': (1) mere sharing of a concept (ishtirāk ma'nawī), in which case quiddity and individuality are also common, so there is no refutation; (2) similarity of individuals (tamāthul), in which case the objection is valid, but this is not what is intended from the original argument. The commentator concludes that the common understanding of commonality is the first meaning (mere sharing of a concept).

Key Terms

السند المساوي
الاشتراك المعنوي

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the commentator's argument?
A) Because it proves too much and leads to absurdity.
B) Because the commonality it establishes is only conceptual, not the real commonality sought.
C) Because it relies on an invalid assumption.
D) Because the commentator is not a reliable authority.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's attempt to prove commonality via invalidating equal ground fails because it only establishes conceptual commonality, not the intended commonality of existence as a single reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to prove something about Allah to yourself or others, do you ever stop at showing that the words and concepts are shared, without pressing further into the reality behind them? What would it take for you to move from conceptual assent to true certainty?
?
The commentator's argument satisfied him, but it was found wanting. In your Islamic learning, where might you be holding onto a proof that makes sense intellectually but doesn't change your heart or actions? What would it cost you to honestly evaluate it?
?
If you truly believed that the existence you experience is a single reality flowing from the Necessary Being, how would your daily awareness of His presence shift? What would you have to let go of to live in that conviction?

The Final Investigation: Two Meanings of Commonality
Final investigation distinguishing two meanings of commonality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's final resolution: distinguishing between 'mere commonality of concept' and 'similarity of individuals' to determine when the objection is valid.

Core Idea
The objection only holds if commonality means similarity of individuals, but the argument only requires mere commonality of concept.

In the final part, the author investigates the objection regarding quiddity and individuality by clarifying two possible interpretations of 'commonality'. If commonality means mere sharing of a concept, then quiddity and individuality are also common, so there is no refutation. If it means similarity of individuals, then the objection is valid, but that is not what the original argument intended. This lesson unpacks this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion about the division of white
The passage first addresses a potential confusion about the division of 'white' and its restriction. Understand the example.
2
Identify the two meanings of commonality
Find where the author says 'the investigation is that if mere commonality is intended' and contrast with 'if similarity is intended'. Note the outcomes.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the common understanding of commonality in the claim is mere sharing, so the objection is invalid.

(And the investigation is that if mere commonality is intended), i.e. if what is intended from the argument by these two aspects is merely that existence is a single meaning common among existents, whether its individuals are similar in reality or not, (then the two) i.e. the concepts of quiddity and individuality (are also accidents for specific quiddities and particular individualities) common among them, even if their individuals have different realities and identities, so there is no refutation by them. (And if similarity in existence is intended), i.e. if it is intended that it is common and its individuals are similar, agreeing in reality, (then this intention does not follow from these two aspects), and the refutation (by them, i.e. by quiddity and individuality, is valid) against them, because their individuals are different, not similar. And you are aware that what is understood from the claim of commonality absolutely is the first meaning.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof from rational division and the commentator's detailed response. The objector argues using quiddity (māhiyya) and individuality (tashakhkhuṣ) as counterexamples. He says: we are certain that a thing has a quiddity, yet we hesitate among particular quiddities and divide quiddity into particularities. Similarly, we have a rational division of quiddity, which would then imply quiddity is common, but quiddities differ in reality, so commonality is false. The commentator then clarifies the author's response. First, he distinguishes two senses of 'rational division': either opposed to inductive (taqribī) or opposed to verbal (lafẓī). Then he states the answer: restrict the division to rational division as opposed to verbal, and admit the opponent's point about verbal division not implying commonality. He then discusses a further response: the division in the case of equivocal terms like 'ayn can be interpreted as a meaningful division (if we take the concept of the thing named), but even that does not prove the intended commonality of existence as a single meaning shared by all existents. The real response is to return to the author's original argument. Then the commentator addresses the objection about quiddity and individuality in detail. He offers two interpretations of what is meant by 'commonality': (1) mere sharing of a concept (ishtirāk ma'nawī), in which case quiddity and individuality are also common, so there is no refutation; (2) similarity of individuals (tamāthul), in which case the objection is valid, but this is not what is intended from the original argument. The commentator concludes that the common understanding of commonality is the first meaning (mere sharing of a concept).

الاشتراك
الماهية
التشخص

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection to the argument from rational division is valid because quiddity and individuality are not common.
Correction: The author shows that if commonality means merely sharing a concept, then quiddity and individuality are indeed common. The objection only works if commonality means similarity of individuals, but that is not the intended meaning in the original argument.

Checkpoint

B) When commonality means similarity of individuals.

D) Never.

Key Takeaway
The objection only refutes the argument if commonality is interpreted as similarity of individuals, but the argument only requires mere commonality of concept.

The Unity of Non-Existence: Third Proof for the Commonality of Existence
The third proof argues that non-existence is a single concept, implying existence is common.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the commonality of existence based on the unity of the concept of non-existence, including the key objection and its response.

Core Idea
Non-existence is a single concept; its opposite, existence, must also be common.

The third proof for the commonality of existence turns to the concept of non-existence (al-'adam). The argument is elegant: if non-existence is one undifferentiated concept, then existence—its opposite—cannot be multiple in reality. However, this argument faces a sharp objection from those who distinguish between specific non-existences. In this lesson, we unpack the proof, its rationale, and the theological debate it engenders.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the density and interplay between matn and sharh.
The passage includes commentary interjections; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the main claim and support
Find the statement 'non-existence is a single concept' and the reason given: 'no differentiation in it by essence'.
Also note the clarification about quiddities and individualities.
3
Understand the objection
Look for the objection starting with 'if you say' – it argues that unity of non-existence does not prevent rational enumeration between specific existence and specific non-existence.
The objection distinguishes between the concept and specific negations.
4
Grasp the reply
The reply (though cut off) clarifies that the arguer intends to negate specific non-existences based on absence of differentiation, not merely conceptual unity.
The key is the distinction between non-existence by essence and by relation.

The third aspect is that non-existence is a single concept, since there is no differentiation in it, i.e. in non-existence by essence, so there is no multiplicity in it. Since his saying: (Because) quiddities have different realities, i.e. what is predicated as quiddity like man and horse differ in their realities, so quiddity is not common. His saying: (And individualities) i.e. what is predicated as individuality like the individuality of Zayd and the individuality of ‘Amr are distinct from each other, otherwise they would not necessitate the distinction of persons, so they are not common in anything but rather differ according to their identities, i.e. their personal quiddities, by being individualized by themselves, not by an additional individuality, otherwise there would be an infinite regress; so reflect, for the inept have imagined that this eloquent statement is feeble. (His saying: by essence) is a qualification because in it there is multiplicity and distinction according to relation, like the non-existence of the condition, the non-existence of the conditioned, the non-existence of Zayd, and the non-existence of ‘Amr. For it was said: If it is granted that the concept of non-existence, which is negation, is one with no multiplicity at all, neither originally nor derivatively, then the purpose would be achieved by it, necessarily because the removal of multiple things is multiple in some way, and there would be no need to add the invalidity of enumeration to prove the purpose by admitting a false premise. In it, his saying: (The third) that non-existence is a single concept. For if you say: The unity of the concept of non-existence does not prevent the realization of rational enumeration between specific existence and specific non-existence, in the sense of negating that existence, for there is no intermediary between a thing being existent or not existent, whether the negation is a single meaning common among its individuals or each negation is a real particular having no commonality with other negations except verbally. I say: The intent of the arguer in the unity of the concept of non-existence is to negate specific non-existences, based on the absence of differentiation among non-existences, not merely the realization of a common concept with
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the commonality of existence, based on the unity of the concept of non-existence (al-'adam). The argument states that non-existence has no intrinsic differentiation (lā tamāyuz fīhi bi-al-dhāt), so it is a single concept without multiplicity. The commentator then clarifies by distinguishing non-existence by essence from non-existence by relation (such as non-existence of a condition, etc.), which does have multiplicity. The proof is that since non-existence is one, its opposite (existence) must also be one (i.e., common). The passage then includes an objection: even if non-existence is one, that does not prevent rational enumeration between specific existence and specific non-existence, because the negation of a specific existent is specific. The answer is that the arguer intends by the unity of non-existence to negate specific non-existences, based on the absence of differentiation among non-existences. The passage is cut off, but the idea is that the commonality of existence is supported by the unity of non-existence.

Key Terms
العدم
السلب
الحصر العقلي
الوجود الخاص
العدم الخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unity of non-existence means all non-existences are identical in every respect.
Correction: Non-existence is one in concept (by essence), but specific non-existences are distinct due to their relations to specific subjects (e.g., non-existence of Zayd vs. non-existence of ‘Amr).

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the third proof from the unity of non-existence?
A) That non-existence has intrinsic differentiation.
B) That the unity of non-existence does not prevent rational enumeration between specific existence and specific non-existence.
C) That the proof is circular.
D) That the proof relies on conventional division.

Key Takeaway
The unity of non-existence by essence supports the commonality of existence, but specific non-existences are multiple due to relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof hinges on non-existence by essence having no differentiation. When you look at the world, you see distinct things that perish. Does the unity of non-existence help you see beyond the surface and realize that all perishable things are equally dependent on the One who alone exists by essence? How can you train your mind to see the commonality of existence rather than the multiplicity of things?
?

Commit to one such topic and resolve to explore it with the same depth as this proof.

The Third Argument: Non-Existence as a Single Concept
Al-Jurjani presents the third argument that existence is a single concept based on the unity of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the unity of non-existence necessitates the unity of existence as a single common concept.

Core Idea
Existence must be one because non-existence is one — otherwise the dichotomy fails.

Al-Jurjani now presents a clever argument based on the nature of non-existence. If non-existence is a single concept with no internal divisions, then existence must also be a single concept; otherwise, the basic rational division of things into existent or non-existent would be impossible. This argument reinforces the claim that existence is a common meaning shared by all things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Jurjani uses a very condensed style here, typical of philosophical texts.
2
Identify the premise about non-existence
Find where the author states that non-existence is a single concept and why. Understand the reasoning that there is no distinction within non-existence by essence.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the implication for existence
The author argues that existence must also be a single concept, otherwise the rational dichotomy between existence and non-existence would be invalid. Consider why the dichotomy is necessary.
The dichotomy is a self-evident rational principle.
4
Examine the example of Zayd
Read the example carefully. It illustrates how if existence were multiple concepts, the dichotomy would not be exhaustive. Ask yourself: Why does saying 'Zayd is either existent with a particular existence or not existent at all' fail to cover all possibilities?
This example makes the abstract argument concrete.
5
Connect to the broader theme
Reflect on how this argument supports the claim that existence is a common predicate (common universal) that applies to everything that exists, despite the diversity of existents themselves.
It is important not to conflate the concept of existence with the essences of things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث أن العدم مفهوم واحد إذ لا تمايز فيه بالذات فلا تعدد فيه إذ تعدد بلا تمايز فكذا مقابله أعني الوجود معنى واحداً وإلا بطل الحصر العقلي فيهما يعني أن قولك الشيء إما موجود أو معدوم، حصر عقلي لا يخرج عنه قطعاً، فإذا كان العدم مفهوماً واحداً والوجود مفهومات متعددة بطل ذلك الحصر العقلي ضرورة أنه لا حصر في العدم المطلق والوجود الخاص فإنك إذا قلت : زيد إما أن يكون موجوداً بوجود خاص، أو لا يكون موجوداً أصلاً، لم يكن ذلك حاصراً لجواز أن يكون موجوداً بوجود مغاير لذلك الوجود الخاص.
The third aspect: that non-existence is a single concept since there is no distinction in it by essence, so there is no multiplicity in it, since multiplicity without distinction [is impossible]. Likewise its counterpart, namely existence, is a single meaning, otherwise the rational dichotomy between them would be invalid. That is, your statement "a thing is either existent or non-existent" is a rational dichotomy that the intellect definitely does not go outside of. So if non-existence is a single concept and existence is multiple concepts, that rational dichotomy becomes invalid because there is no dichotomy between absolute non-existence and particular existence. For if you say: Zayd is either existent with a particular existence, or not existent at all, that is not exhaustive because it is possible that he exists with an existence different from that particular existence.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the claim that existence is a common concept among existents. The argument is based on the unity of non-existence. The author begins by stating that non-existence is a single concept because it has no intrinsic distinction within itself; multiplicity without distinction is impossible. Therefore, existence must also be a single concept, otherwise the rational dichotomy between existence and non-existence would be invalid. The rational dichotomy is the principle that any thing is either existent or non-existent, a principle that the intellect necessarily accepts. If non-existence is one but existence is many, then the dichotomy becomes broken because there is no exhaustive division between absolute non-existence and particular existence. The author illustrates with the example of Zayd: saying "Zayd is either existent with a particular existence, or not existent at all" does not cover the possibility that he might exist with a different existence. Thus, the argument concludes that existence must be a single common concept.

Key Terms
العدم
الوجود
الحصر العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that all existents are identical in essence.
Correction: No, it only claims that the concept of existence is the same for all existents, not that they are identical in other aspects.

Checkpoint

A) The commonality of non-existence
B) The multiplicity of non-existence
C) The impossibility of a rational dichotomy

Key Takeaway
The third argument relies on the unity of non-existence to prove that existence is a single common concept.

Objection and Answer: The Rational Dichotomy and the Possibility of Existence through Another's Existence
Al-Jurjani responds to an objection that the rational dichotomy holds even if existence is multiple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the objection that the rational dichotomy remains exhaustive even if existence is multiple, by distinguishing between pure rational judgment and judgment with external premises.

Core Idea
The rational dichotomy stands only when the intellect judges purely without external assumptions.

Al-Jurjani now faces an objection: if existence through another is impossible, then everything is either self-existent or non-existent, so the rational dichotomy seems unshaken. His response clarifies the very nature of a rational dichotomy—it must be a judgment of pure intellect, unaided by any external premise. In this lesson, we will follow the objection and its subtle refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objector's claim: since existence through another is impossible, the only options are existence by itself or non-existence, so the dichotomy holds.
2
Identify the response
Note the word 'قلت' indicating the author's response. He says the dichotomy is invalidated. Find the reason he gives.
3
Understand the definition of rational dichotomy
The response defines rational dichotomy as what the intellect judges as exhaustive when considered purely, without relying on any external premise. This is key to the refutation.
This definition is the crux of the argument.
4
Apply the definition to the objection
The impossibility of existence through another is an external premise. Without it, the intellect would not judge the dichotomy as exhaustive. Thus the dichotomy is not purely rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: كون الشيء موجوداً بوجود غيره أمر محال فكل شيء، إما أن يكون موجوداً بوجوده الخاص وإما أن لا يكون موجوداً أصلاً، فلا يبطل الحصر العقلي قلت: بل يبطل لأن الحصر العقلي ما لو جرد النظر إليه يجزم العقل بالانحصار
For if you said: A thing existing through another's existence is impossible; so every thing is either existent with its own particular existence or not existent at all, so the rational dichotomy is not invalidated. I say: Rather it is invalidated, because the rational dichotomy is what the intellect judges to be exhaustive when considered purely, without external premises.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objector argues that since a thing cannot exist through another's existence, the rational dichotomy between existence with its own particular existence and non-existence is exhaustive, so the dichotomy stands even if existence is multiple. The author responds that the rational dichotomy is defined as that which the intellect judges to be exhaustive when considered purely, without relying on any external premise. Here, the judgment that a thing cannot exist through another's existence is an external premise. Without it, the intellect would not judge the dichotomy to be exhaustive. Therefore, the dichotomy is not purely rational and the argument that existence must be a single concept remains valid.

Key Terms

الوجود الخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rational dichotomy is not invalidated by the impossibility of existence through another because that impossibility is an external premise.
Correction: The rational dichotomy must be judged by pure intellect without external premises; hence the impossibility is an external premise that cannot be used to support the dichotomy.

Key Takeaway
The rational dichotomy is defined by pure intellectual judgment, independent of any external premises.

The Objection and Response on the Commonality of Existence
The author presents an objection and the response by recasting the claim as a negative proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim that existence is not common can be defended by reframing it as a negative proposition, thereby avoiding the need for a real common meaning.

Core Idea
A negative proposition does not require the subject to exist in reality, only in conception.

In this dense passage, the author presents an objection against the opponent who holds that existence is not common (mushtarak). The objection claims that the opponent's own argument forces him to admit a common meaning. The author then provides a clever response: recast the claim as a negative. This move shifts the burden and avoids the need for a real common existence. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part carefully. The objection argues that if existence were not common, the opponent would have to prove each existence individually, but his proof covers all, implying a single common meaning.
Notice the logic: impossibility of one proof applying to many different things forces the opponent to assume a common concept.
2
Grasp the response
The answer is to take the claim as a negative: 'There is no common meaning called existence.' Recognize that a negative proposition only requires conceiving the subject, not its real existence.
The commentator explains the difference between affirmative and negative judgments.
3
Follow the sub-objection and final clarification
The text then discusses a potential objection about the need for a universal conception even in negatives, and the author refutes it using an analogy from logic.
Note the analogy from logic and the final remark about the universal concept itself.

If the concept of existence were different, that claimant would need to prove for each one individually, of the existences of essences, that it is not common, because it is impossible for a single proof to apply to multiple things considering the particularity of each one. But he acknowledges that his proof that existence is not common encompasses every existence. Therefore, he must conceive a single meaning encompassing all existences, and he has judged that meaning with an affirmative true judgment that it is not common, so that meaning must be realized. Thus, he is forced to acknowledge that existence is common. (The answer is that we take) the claim (as negative) not affirmative or modified. And by what we have mentioned, it becomes clear that mere conception of a single meaning is not sufficient for inference, because it is possible that this conception is merely a supposition of the intellect and its consideration, so it does not necessitate the establishment of that meaning in reality but rather the intellect's supposition. And that the answer by taking that proposition as negative is complete. And what is said: that the foundation of inference is that in the claim there must be a conception of a single, general meaning, so that it can be proven by a single proof, whether the claim is affirmative or negative, so the mentioned distinction is not beneficial in the answer, is an erroneous illusion. His statement: (It is not impossible for a single proof to apply, etc.) For if you say: Actual application is impossible, but potential application is not impossible, by presenting a proof that can be presented in other than the case in which it was presented, so that presentation suffices. Its analogy is what the commentator clarified in the beginning of Bayan Sharh al-Miftah, that if he explains a particular by a way, knowing its running in all particulars equally, the universal rule is established without doubt, and it is called a depiction of the universal proof in a particular example for familiarity. His statement: (And he has judged that meaning) It appears that he made the universal concept itself also among the individuals and generalized the judgment to all of them; otherwise it would suffice to say: He must conceive a single meaning encompassing the existences which is called common existence. Then, what is meant by 'true' in his statement 'with an affirmative true judgment' is truth in the opinion of the one inferring. So reflect.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a detailed response concerning whether existence (wujud) is a common concept (mushtarak). The objection is that if existence were not common, the opponent would have to prove separately for each particular existence that it is not common, which is impossible because a single proof cannot apply to multiple distinct things. However, the opponent's own proof claims to cover all existences, implying he must conceive a single meaning that encompasses them, and he judges that meaning as not common, which forces him to admit that existence is common. The commentary then presents the response: we take the claim as a negative (saliba) proposition rather than an affirmative (mujiba). We say 'there is no common meaning among them called existence,' which does not require a real common existence; it only requires conceiving such an existence. The commentary explains that for an affirmative proposition to be true, there must be a real subject, but a negative only needs conception. It also addresses a potential objection: that even a negative needs a universal conception to be proven by a single proof. The response is that mere conceptualization by the intellect suffices, and the negative does not demand extramental existence. The passage also discusses the possibility of one proof potentially applying to many cases, citing an analogy from logic. Finally, it critiques an alternative interpretation of the original text.
