الماهية (mahiyyah)
الواجب (al-Wajib)
الممكن (mumkin)
العلة (cause)
التقدم (priority)
التسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes that the cause is the essence, which is what it wants to prove.
Correction: The proof does not assume identity at the start. It assumes the opposite (that existence is additional) and derives a contradiction. The identification of the cause as the essence follows from the exclusion of any other cause, not from an initial assumption. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum, not circular.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what follows if the existence of the Necessary were additional to its essence?
A) It would be identical to essence.
B) It would be contingent and need a cause.
C) It would be impossible.
D) It would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The existence of the Necessary Being is not an addition to its essence; the two are identical, as any other assumption leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists', do you truly believe that His existence is not an added quality but that He is existence itself? How would your daily reliance on Him change if you held this truth firmly in your heart, knowing He depends on nothing outside Himself?
?
The proof shows that any addition to Allah's essence leads to absurdity. Is there any attribute or concept you mentally add to Allah's essence that you treat as separate, like a quality He 'has' rather than what He 'is'? How can you purify your understanding of His names and attributes in light of this?
?
If the Necessary Being's existence is identical to His essence, then He is utterly self-sufficient and independent. In what areas of your life do you act as if He needs your actions or worship to sustain Him? How can you realign your worship to reflect His absolute self-sufficiency and your absolute need for Him?

Objection and Response on the Need for a Cause for the Description
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and response on the need for a cause for the description of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proof of existence's addition requires an external existence, and the response that distinguishes the need for a cause for the description itself, as well as the further distinction between mental and external addition.

Core Idea
The need for a cause applies to the description of existence, not to existence itself.

In kalam arguments, objections often challenge the starting assumptions. Here, an objector denies that existence of the Necessary is external, thereby questioning the need for a cause. The response refines the argument: it is the description of the essence by existence that requires a cause, not existence as an external entity. This distinction is crucial for understanding the identity thesis (al-'ayniyya).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection (فإن قلت) carefully. The objector says the proof assumes existence is an external existent, which is denied. Understand the starting challenge.
The objector is targeting the foundation of the proof that existence is additional.
2
Understand the response's clarification
Read the response (قلت) that distinguishes the need for a cause: it is not for existence itself but for the description (اتصاف) of the essence by existence. Note that the description requires a cause, whether the essence or something else.
The response does not concede that existence itself needs a cause, only the attribute-attribution relation.
3
Examine the reasoning on priority
The response further argues that the cause can be prior by essence, not necessarily by existence. This shows that the description's need for a cause does not imply a temporal cause.
This reinforces that the need is logical, not causal in the usual sense.
4
Distinguish the two interpretations
Identify the two scenarios: if the addition is external (real attribute), the argument stands; if it is merely mental (abstracted by the mind), the need is only for mental abstraction, which is harmless. This shows the response only works for external addition.
The commentator warns this is subtle and requires contemplation.
5
Appreciate the conclusion on identity
The proponent of identity (العينية) denies any addition even mentally in the same sense. The response does not contradict this; it only explains the logical requirement for description if addition were assumed.
This protects the identity thesis from being undermined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : كون وجود الواجب على تقدير الزيادة ممكناً محتاجاً إلى علة مبني على أن وجوده موجود خارجي، وهو ممنوع قلت ليس المراد أنه محتاج إلى علة، توجده، بل المراد أنه على تقدير زيادته وقيامه بالماهية كان صفة لها فاتصاف الماهية بها لا بد له من علة هي إما الماهية أو غيرها ) وأجيب عنه بأن العلة ) لا شك أنها ( متقدمة ) على المعلول ( وأما ) أن تقدمها عليه يجب أن يكون بالوجود فممنوع فإن التقدم الثابت للعلة بالقياس إلى المعلول (قد يكون بغير الوجود كتقدم الماهية عن حصول الوجود للماهية فهو وجود رابطي للوجود وليس ذلك واجباً، ولا ممتنعاً بل ممكناً فيحتاج إلى علة هذا ماقالوا في تنقيح هذا المقام وفيه بحث، لأنه إنما يتم إذا كان القائل بزيادته في الواجب قائلاً بزيادته في الخارج، وأن الاتصاف به حقيقي وأما إذا كان قائلاً بزيادته في الذهن بحسب نفس الأمر بمعنى أنه في حد ذاته بحيث إذا حصل في الذهن انتزع منه الوجود أمراً زائداً، على حقيقته فاللازم ليس إلا احتياجه إلى علة في هذا الانتزاع ولا محذور في ذلك فإنه محتاج فيه إلى الذهن أيضاً، وليس هذا اعترافاً بعينية الوجود في الواجب لأن القائل بالعينية يقول : باتحاد الوجود والماهية في نفس الأمر وعدم تغايرهم بالحقيقة فتدبر فإنه دقيق .
If you say: That the existence of the Necessary, on the assumption of being additional, is contingent and in need of a cause is based on the fact that His existence is an external existent, and that is denied. I say: The intended meaning is not that it is in need of a cause that brings it into existence, but rather that on the assumption of its being additional and subsisting by the essence, it is an attribute of it, so the description of the essence by it must have a cause, either the essence or something else. And it was answered that the cause is undoubtedly prior to the effect, but that its priority over it must be in existence is denied, because the priority established for the cause in relation to the effect may be other than existence, like the priority of the essence over the occurrence of existence for the essence; that would be a relational existence for existence, and it is neither necessary nor impossible but contingent, so it needs a cause. This is what they said in refining this position, and there is discussion about it, because it only works if the one who asserts its addition in the Necessary asserts its addition externally and that the description is real. But if he asserts its addition in the mind in terms of the mind's reality, meaning that in itself, if it occurs in the mind, existence is abstracted from it as something additional to its reality, then the necessary consequence is only its need for a cause in this abstraction, and there is no harm in that, because it is in need of the mind as well. And this is not an admission of the identity of existence in the Necessary, because the proponent of identity says: that existence and essence are united in reality and not distinct in truth. Contemplate, for it is subtle.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage contains an objection (فإن قلت) and its answer (قلت) followed by further clarification. The objection challenges the proof by denying that existence is an external existent, thus the need for a cause is not established. The answer distinguishes between needing a cause for the existence itself and needing a cause for the description of the essence by existence. The description (اتصاف) requires a cause. The response then addresses the nature of priority: the cause can be prior other than by existence, such as the essence being prior in a logical sense. The text then discusses two interpretations: if the addition is external (external existence), the argument stands; if the addition is merely mental (abstracted by the mind), then the need is only for the mental abstraction, which is harmless. The proponent of identity (العينية) holds that existence and essence are identical in reality, so no addition exists even mentally in the same sense. The commentator warns that this is a subtle point requiring contemplation.

Key Terms
الزيادة (addition)
الوصف (attribute)
الاتصاف (description)
الذهن (mind)
الوجود الرابطي (relational existence)
العينية (identity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the response concedes that existence is additional in reality.
Correction: The response only explains the logical need for a cause for the description, not that existence itself is additional. It also clarifies that if addition is only mental, the need is only for mental abstraction, which does not affect the identity thesis.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the objection, what does the need for a cause apply to?
A) The existence itself as an external entity.
B) The description of the essence by existence.
C) The essence itself.
D) The mind that conceives existence.

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause pertains to the description of the essence by existence, not to existence itself, and this distinction preserves the identity thesis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intellectually distinguish Allah's existence from His essence, do you treat that distinction as real in Him, or do you remember that it is only a tool for your limited mind? How does this passage demand you guard against dividing Allah in your heart?
?
Are you willing to submit to the precision of scholars like al-Jurjani, or do you excuse yourself from deep theological reasoning, perhaps fearing it is 'too much'? What would it mean for your love of Allah to embrace such subtlety as a protection for tawhid?
?
If the need for a cause in the description of existence does not imply that existence itself has a cause, then what does that say about the way you think about Allah's attributes? Are you content to let your mind rest in the truth that He is not like anything, even in the way we reason about Him?

The First Proof and Its Ash'ari Answer
First proof and Ash'ari answer for existence as additional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that existence is additional to essence—based on the possible essence's acceptance of non-existence—and the Ash'ari answer that reinterprets 'acceptance' as a mere notion without real subsistence.

Core Idea
Essence's acceptance of non-existence proves existence is additional unless acceptance has no ontological basis.

This lesson unpacks the first of four proofs for the view that existence is additional to essence in the possible. The proof hinges on the claim that possible essence in itself accepts non-existence, while essence taken with existence does not—implying existence is not identical to essence. The Ash'ari answer challenges the very reality of non-existent essences, transforming the debate from a logical to an ontological one. We'll trace the argument and its rebuttal, clarifying the doctrinal stakes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall flow, then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage includes both the proof and the answer, with embedded commentary.
The text is dense; don't worry about every detail on first pass.
2
Identify the proof's structure
The proof is a hypothetical syllogism: if existence were identical to essence or a part of it, then the essence in itself would not accept non-existence. But it does accept non-existence, so existence is additional. Locate each premise and the conclusion.
The key premise is that possible essence in itself accepts non-existence; if it didn't, it would be necessary.
3
Understand the Ash'ari answer
The answer denies that 'accepting non-existence' means the essence has any real subsistence in the state of non-existence. For the Ash'aris, non-existent things have no reality, so the premise loses its force.
This response is grounded in the Ash'ari rejection of the Platonic/Peripatetic notion of 'the subsistence of the non-existent' (ثبوت المعدوم).
4
Reflect on the theological significance
The disagreement over existence being additional ties into deeper questions about essence, existence, and the reality of non-existence. Consider how this affects arguments for God's existence from contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لما مر من لزوم جواز كونها موجودة ومعدومة معاً. قوله : ( وأجيب إلخ) حاصله أنه إن أريد بالقبول معناه الحقيقي أعني الاتصاف الذي يقتضي مجامعة القابل والمقبول فلا نسلم بطلان التالي بمنع أن الماهية من حيث لأنه فرع القول بثبوت المعدوم ولا ثبوت له عندنا وإن أريد به الطريان سواء اجتمع معه أو لا، فلا حيث هي تقبله، قوله: (بل كانت تأبى العدم من حيث هي هي أيضاً أي مثل الماهية الواجبية أو مثل المأخوذ مع الوجود .
And the third [view] is that it (existence) is additional to reality in both the Necessary and the possible. So here are two investigations: the first is that it is additional to essence in the possible, for four proofs. (The first is that the possible essence, in itself, accepts non-existence; otherwise...) the cause. He said: 'Or bringing itself into existence – this premise is rejected by the theologians because the aspect of agency and receptivity are combined, so it is permissible for it to be prior in essence, not in existence, and this does not close the door to proving the Maker, as is not hidden. And the correct view in my opinion is that it is not bringing into existence here, but rather it is the exigency of the essence for existence, and the exigent does not necessarily have to be a bringer into existence – do you not see that essences are exigent for their concomitants, yet are not agents of them, based on what has been established that their making is one? How can it be, while external bringing into existence must have a bringer into existence? And a bringer into existence in the external, and there is nothing here except the essence qualified by existence. And the consideration of multiplicity in it, in terms of it being a bringer into existence in one respect and in another respect it is a bringer into existence through qualification by existence, is only in the mind.' He said: 'Regarding the essence in itself, it is said: These two premises – namely the essence in itself accepts non-existence and the essence taken with existence does not accept non-existence – when combined, yield by the second figure that the essence is unlike the benefactor of existence to another, because the intuitive reason judges that what does not exist cannot be beneficial for the existence of another, and from here one deduces the existence of the Maker from the world.' He said: 'Without regard to existence and non-existence' – that is, without regard to existence in particular and non-existence in particular, so his statement is correct. Otherwise it would be impossible, etc. If you say: It is permissible for it to constitute it by considering one of the two matters, existence and non-existence, so hesitation about one of them does not contradict the aforementioned certainty. I say: Mentioning non-existence is incidental because constitution is only conceived by considering existence alone, and that is what is meant by the objection. And if it (the possible essence) does not accept non-existence, possibility would be removed from it and it would be qualified by essential necessity. And there is no doubt that the possible essence when taken with existence repels it (non-existence); otherwise it could be existent and non-existent simultaneously. And if existence were the essence itself (of the possible) or a part of it, it would not be so, but rather it would repel non-existence in itself as well. As for the assumption that existence is itself: because existence repels the acceptance of its opposite. As for the assumption that it is a part of it: because then the essence in itself is taken with existence, so it does not accept non-existence, as previously mentioned. (And it was answered) concerning this proof: (If you mean by 'accepting non-existence' that it (the possible essence) is established in the external world devoid of existence, qualified by non-existence, then it is rejected – because the essence in the state of non-existence has no subsistence in itself according to us; rather, in itself it is not an existent essence – and that is the desired objective, so there is no need for the remaining premises. And it is nothing, because it does not follow from it that the essence is not identical to existence, for everything other than it (existence), when taken with itself – whether taken as a qualification of it or a part of it – necessarily entails the difference between the absolute and the qualified, and the part and the whole.' He said: 'It repels it' – i.e., the essence in itself is different from the essence taken with existence in acceptance and its absence. He said: 'It would not be so' – i.e., the essence in itself would not be different from that taken with existence, but rather in itself it would also repel non-existence, just as that taken with existence repels it. So the contrast is correct, and the meaning of 'also' becomes clear without effort. His statement 'so' does not refer to his statement 'accepts non-existence' so that the contrast would be invalid, because 'does not accept non-existence' and 'repels non-existence' have the same meaning, and his statement 'also' would not be correct because its meaning then would be that it does not accept non-existence just as it does not accept something else. The essence of the inference is a hypothetical syllogism whose form is: If existence were the essence itself or a part of it, then the essence in itself would not be different from the essence taken with existence in accepting non-existence or not accepting it; rather, they would be united in non-acceptance. The consequent is false. As for the implication, it is what the commentator mentioned; as for the falsity of the consequent, it is what the author mentioned: that the essence in itself accepts non-existence, while the essence taken with existence does not accept it. Understand, for feet have slipped here. He said: 'Because existence repels, etc.' How could it not? The essence that is its subject does not accept it, so how could it accept itself? He said: 'Taken with existence' – in terms of it being existent. He said: 'Because of what previously passed concerning the necessity of the permissibility of it being existent and non-existent simultaneously.' He said: 'And it was answered, etc.' Its summary is that if by 'acceptance' is meant its true meaning – i.e., the qualification that requires co-presence of the receiver and the received – then we do not grant the falsity of the consequent by denying that the essence in itself accepts it, because that is a branch of the doctrine of the subsistence of the non-existent, and it has no subsistence according to us. And if by it is meant occurrence, whether it coexists with it or not, then it accepts it. He said: 'Rather, it repels non-existence in itself as well' – i.e., like the necessary essence or like that taken with existence.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the third view that existence is additional to reality in both the Necessary and the possible. It then begins the first investigation: existence is additional to essence in the possible, supported by four proofs. Only the first proof is given here: possible essence in itself accepts non-existence; if it did not, it would be necessary. If existence were identical to essence or a part of it, the essence in itself would also repel non-existence, which contradicts its accepting non-existence. The answer to this proof denies that the essence in the state of non-existence has any subsistence in itself, so the premise that it 'accepts non-existence' is reinterpreted not as a real characteristic but as a mere notion. The commentary explains the logical structure: a hypothetical syllogism showing that if existence were identical or part, the essence in itself would be the same as the essence taken with existence in repelling non-existence, but the consequent is false because the essence in itself accepts non-existence while the essence taken with existence does not. The answer refutes this by distinguishing the meaning of 'acceptance'. The passage also includes extensive commentary on the logical form and potential misunderstandings.

Key Terms
إمكان
وجوب ذاتي
قبول العدم
ثبوت المعدوم
ماهية ممكنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'accepting non-existence' means the essence has a real subsistence in the state of non-existence, so the proof stands.
Correction: The Ash'aris deny that non-existent essences have any reality (ثبوت), so 'acceptance' is merely a mental notion, not a real property. Thus the proof is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari answer to the first proof that possible essence accepts non-existence?
A) The essence in itself does accept non-existence, but that does not affect existence being additional.
B) The essence in non-existence has no subsistence, so 'acceptance' does not imply a real property.
C) The proof is invalid because essence cannot be considered apart from existence.
D) The essence repels non-existence just as existence does.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for existence being additional relies on the possible essence's acceptance of non-existence, but the Ash'ari answer nullifies the proof by denying that non-existent essences have any reality, reinterpreting 'acceptance' as a mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof seems straightforward: because a possible thing can cease to exist, its existence must be separate from its essence. But the Ash'ari answer questions what 'can cease to exist' really means. Have you ever held a belief that seemed logically airtight, only to realize you were assuming something about reality that not everyone shares? How did that realization affect your certainty?
?
The Ash'aris deny that non-existent essences have any reality. This means when you think about something that doesn't exist, your thought is not about a 'thing' in any real sense. Does that challenge your intuitive understanding of possibility? What would it mean for your own faith if you had to admit that some of your theological reasoning rests on assumptions that are not divinely revealed?
?
This lesson shows that even the most careful logical proofs in kalam are subject to deep disagreement over first principles. Are you comfortable with that uncertainty, or do you feel a need to resolve it? What is the danger of insisting on your own reasoning when the scholars themselves differ? How can you remain humble before the truth while still seeking to understand?

The Fourth Aspect: Absolute vs. Specific Existence
Al-Jurjani discusses the fourth aspect and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth aspect of the argument only proves the addition of absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) and not specific existence (al-wujud al-khass).

Core Idea
The fourth aspect distinguishes absolute existence addition from specific.

Al-Jurjani presents the fourth aspect (al-wajh al-rabi‘) as a distinct argument within the discussion of whether existence is additional to essence. He emphasizes that this aspect specifically demonstrates the addition of absolute existence, unlike the previous aspects which covered both absolute and specific. Understanding this nuance is essential for grasping the full scope of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the mention of 'الوجه الرابع' and the discussion of predication examples.
The passage includes commentary, so focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the main claim of the fourth aspect
Look for the sentence that states 'الوجه الرابع هذا الوجه يدل على زيادة الوجود المطلق' and understand what it means.
This is the key conclusion.
3
Compare with previous aspects
Note that previous aspects indicated addition of both absolute and specific existence, while the fourth only indicates absolute.
The phrase 'بخلاف الوجوه السابقة فإنها دالة على زيادة المطلق والخاص' is crucial.
4
Examine the predication examples
The passage uses examples like 'السواد موجود' to illustrate why existence must be additional. Understand the reasoning.
These examples show the benefit of predication.
5
Reflect on the scope
Understand that the fourth aspect is limited to absolute existence, which is a more general argument.
This is important for avoiding overgeneralization.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها
السواد (موجود) مع كونه مفيداً فائدة معنوية معتداً بها ( كقولنا السواد سواد والموجود موجود ) وهو مما لا يعتد به والأظهر أن يقال : وكان قولنا السواد موجود كقولنا السواد ذو سواد والوجود ذو وجود قيل: ولو كان الوجود جزءاً لكان قولنا السواد موجوداً كقولنا السواد لون أو ذو لون وليس فيه فائدة جديدة إذا كان السواد معقولاً بالكنه بخلاف حمل الوجود عليه الوجه ( الرابع أنه لو لم يكن ) الوجود ( زائداً ) على الماهية
•
قوله : ( كقولنا السواد سواد بناء على أن معنى الموجود والوجود واحد والسواد عين الوجود فحمل الموجود حمل السواد هذا إن اعتبر الاتحاد في جانب المحمول، وإن اعتبر في جانب الموضوع كان كقولنا الموجود موجود.
قوله: (وهو مما لا يعتد به إن اعتبر التغاير بين الموضوع والمحمول بالاعتبار كما في هذا زيد وإن لم يعتبر لا يصح الحمل.
قوله : ( والأظهر أن يقال : (إلخ لا به حينئذ لا يحتاج إلى اعتبار اتحاد الوجود والموجود في المعنى مع أن حمل الشيء على نفسه غير مفيد اشتقاقا كما أنه غير مفيد مواطأة إن اعتبر التغاير وكلاهما غير صحيح إن لم يعتبر .
قوله : ( كقولنا السواد لون أو ذو لون) التقديران باعتبار كونه جزءاً محمولاً أو غير محمول. قوله : ( بخلاف حمل الوجود (عليه فإنه مفيد وإن تصور السواد بالكنه، وفيه أنه إنما يتم إذا تصور السواد بالكنه وهو ممنوع ، ومن هذا ظهر عدم تمام الاستدلال على تقدير كونه نفس الماهية أيضاً بأنه إنما يلزم عدم إفادة الحمل إذا تصور ،الماهية والوجود بالكنه أما إذا تصور كلاهما أو أحدهما بالوجه العارض فلا واختلاف العنوان له مدخل في الإفادة وعدمها، فإن قولنا : الإنسان حيوان مفيد إذا تصور الموضوع من حيث الضاحك غير مفيد إذا تصور من حيث أنه حيوان .
قوله : (الوجه الرابع هذا الوجه يدل على زيادة الوجود المطلق بخلاف الوجوه السابقة فإنها دالة على زيادة المطلق والخاص.
موجود حينئذ أنه ليس بمرتفع على ما مر أن معنى عدم الماهية على تقدير عينية الوجود لها ارتفاعها بالكلية، وأما القول بأن نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه بالاشتقاق مفيد، بل هو مبحث للعقلاء، فإن النسبة بين الوجود ونفسه اشتقاقا معركة للآراء، فقد ذكرنا في مباحث شبه القادحين في البديهيات اندفاعه وكيف لا والمغايرة الاعتبارية أن كفى في نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه بلفظ ذو وكان صحة الحمل مبنياً عليها كان إنكار عدم الإفادة مكابرة إذ لا فائدة في اعتبار المغايرة بين الشيء ونفسه ، وحمله على نفسه بواسطة ذو وإن لم يكف كما هو الظاهر إذ التغاير الاعتباري لا يكفي في كل نسبة كما في كون شيء فوق شيء وأمثاله والنسبة بين الشيء ونفسه بالصاحبية والاتصاف من هذا القبيل فهذا الحمل ليس بصحيح فضلاً عن الإفادة . قوله : ( الرابع إلخ ) لو تم لدل على زيادة الوجود المطلق دون الخاص.
'The first observation - the third aim: That existence is the essence itself or a part of it.
Blackness (is existent) while being beneficial with a meaningful benefit worthy of consideration (like our saying: blackness is blackness and the existent is existent) and that is not considered worthy. And the more apparent is that it be said: our saying 'blackness is existent' is like our saying 'blackness has blackness' and 'existence has existence.' It was said: If existence were a part, our saying 'blackness is existent' would be like our saying 'blackness is a color' or 'has a color,' and there is no new benefit in it if blackness is understood by its essence, unlike the predication of existence upon it. The fourth aspect: That if existence were not additional to essence...

He said: (Like our saying: blackness is blackness) based on the meaning of 'the existent' and 'existence' being one, and blackness is identical to existence, so the predication of 'existent' is the predication of 'blackness.' This if unity is considered on the side of the predicate; if considered on the side of the subject, it would be like our saying: 'the existent is existent.'

He said: (And it is not considered worthy) if the difference between subject and predicate is considered by consideration, as in 'this is Zayd'; if not considered, the predication is not correct.

He said: (And the more apparent is that it be said: etc.) then it does not need to consider the union of existence and 'existent' in meaning, although the predication of a thing upon itself is not beneficial derivationally, just as it is not beneficial tautologically if difference is considered, and both are incorrect if not considered.

He said: (Like our saying: blackness is a color or has a color) the two considerations are based on it being a part predicated or not predicated. He said: (Unlike the predication of existence upon it) for it is beneficial even if blackness is understood by its essence; but in it, it is only completed if blackness is understood by its essence, which is denied. From this, the incompleteness of the argument on the assumption that it is the essence itself also appears: that it necessitates lack of benefit of predication only if the essence and existence are understood by their essence; but if both or one of them is understood through an accidental aspect, it is not so, and the difference of aspect has a role in the benefit and non-benefit. For our saying: 'Man is an animal' is beneficial if the subject is considered from the aspect of 'the laughing one,' not beneficial if considered from the aspect of 'he is an animal.'

He said: (The fourth aspect) This aspect indicates the addition of absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) unlike the previous aspects, for they indicate the addition of both absolute and specific.

He said: 'Existent' then that it is not elevated, as has passed that the meaning of non-existence of the essence on the assumption of identity of existence to it is its complete negation. As for the saying that the relation of a thing to itself derivationally is beneficial—rather it is a topic of discussion among intellectuals—the relation between existence and itself derivationally is a conflict of opinions. We have mentioned in the investigations of the objections of the critics of self-evidents its refutation; how not? And the considerative distinction is not sufficient in the relation of a thing to itself via the word 'possessor of' (dhu), and the correctness of predication is based upon it? Denial of lack of benefit would be obstinate denial, for there is no benefit in considering the distinction between a thing and itself, and its predication upon itself via 'possessor of'—even if it were sufficient, as is apparent, for the considerative distinction is sufficient in every relation, as in something being above something and the like, and the relation between a thing and itself in terms of possessorship and qualification is of this kind; so this predication is not correct, let alone beneficial.

He said: (The fourth, etc.) If it were complete, it would indicate the addition of absolute existence, not specific.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage discusses the 'fourth aspect' (al-wajh al-rabi‘) of the argument for the addition (ziyada) of existence to essence. The author begins by examining whether the predication of 'existent' (mawjud) of an essence like blackness (sawad) is beneficial. If existence were identical to essence, saying 'blackness is existent' would be like saying 'blackness is blackness' — a trivial and unbeneficial tautology. But predication of existence is actually informative, indicating that existence must be additional. The commentary then explores different scenarios: if existence were a part, the predication would be like 'blackness is a color' (a genus), which would be informative only if blackness is not already understood as a color. The author argues that the predication of existence is beneficial even if the essence is known in its essence (bi-l-kunh), unlike the part scenario. The fourth aspect specifically shows that existence is additional in the absolute sense, while previous aspects showed addition in both absolute and specific senses. The passage also addresses counterarguments about self-predication (like 'existence is existent'), dismissing them as lacking genuine informativeness. The overall point distinguishes between showing existence as additional in a general sense versus a specific sense.

الوجود الخاص
الوجه الرابع
زيادة
حمل
مفيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth aspect proves that existence is additional in all cases (specific existence).
Correction: The fourth aspect only proves the addition of absolute existence, not specific existence. Previous aspects were needed for specific existence.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth aspect (al-wajh al-rabi‘) specifically demonstrate?
A) Existence is identical to essence.
B) Existence is additional in the absolute sense only.
C) Existence is a part of essence.
D) Existence is additional in both absolute and specific senses.

Key Takeaway
The fourth aspect argues for the addition of absolute existence, but not specific existence; the two must be distinguished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God exists,' do you treat that as a tautology (like 'blackness is blackness') or as a meaningful predication that adds something about reality? How does this distinction shake your certainty about what you affirm?
?
The fourth aspect proves absolute existence addition but not specific. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse general truths with specific lived realities? Where might this conflation be causing you to overlook the concrete demands of faith?
?
The argument fails if essence is not understood by its essence. What does that imply about the limits of human reason in grasping the nature of existence itself? Are you willing to accept that your conceptual understanding of existence may be imperfect, and how does that affect your humility before God?

Refutation of Identity
Al-Jurjani refutes the identity of existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot be identical to essence, based on the argument that existence is common while essences are necessarily diverse.

Core Idea
Existence is common; essences are diverse — they cannot be identical.

Al-Jurjani begins his refutation by considering the possibility that existence is exactly the same as essence. He argues that this is impossible because existence is a concept shared by all existents, whereas the essences of different things are necessarily distinct. This lesson explains this argument and addresses a counterview from those who claim all existents are one essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
Notice the structure: the author first states the two possibilities, then refutes identity.
2
Understand the refutation of identity
The key point is that existence is common (mushtarak) across all existents, while the essences (haqa'iq) of things are necessarily different. Therefore, existence cannot be identical to any particular essence.
3
Examine the counterview
The author mentions a claim that all existents are a single essence that multiplies through descriptions. He dismisses this as obstinate denial (mukabara), asserting that the diversity of essences is known necessarily.
This shows that the argument relies on self-evident diversity.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها
لكان إما نفسها أو جزؤها والأول باطل لأنه ( أي الوجود ) مشترك ) لما مر ( دونها ) أي دون الماهية لأن حقائق الموجودات متخالفة بالضرورة وما يقال من أن الكل ذات واحدة تتعدد بحسب الأوصاف لا غير فالمتقيدون بطور العقل يعدونه مكابرة لا يلتفت إليها ( وكذا الثاني) باطل إذ لو كان الوجود ( جزءاً ) للماهيات لكان أعم الذاتيات المشتركة بين الموجودات إذ لا ذاتي لها أعم منه ( فكان جنسا لها ) إن كان محمولاً عليها وإلا كان جزءاً مشتركاً مثل الجنس ) وتتمايز أنواعه ) المندرجة تحته ( بفصول) أو بأجزاء مختصة مثل الفصول ( هي أيضاً موجودة) لكونها مقومة وأجزاء للماهيات الموجودة ( فيكون ( الوجود ( جنساً لها ) أي لتلك الفصول أيضاً إذ الفرض أنه جنس للموجودات ( فلها ) أي فللفصول (فصول) أخر ( كذلك ) أي موجودة أيضاً ) ويلزم التسلسل وترتب أجزاء الماهية الواحدة إلى غير النهاية ( وأنه محال إذ المركب لا بد له من الانتهاء إلى البسيط ) لأن البسيط مبدأ المركب فلو انتفى انتفي

قوله : ( وما يقال إلخ ) قائله أهل المكاشفة من الصوفية، والحكماء، وهو أن كل الموجودات ذات واحدة وهي الوجود البحت المتشخص بالإطلاق عما سواه، حتى عن الإطلاق أيضاً، ومقابله العدم الصرف لا تميز فيه ولا وصف له فالتميز مختص بالوجود وهو متعدد بحسب تعدد الأوصاف الاعتبارية للنفس الأمرية الوجوبية والإمكانية، وله بكل اعتبار حكم عقلي وشرعي وحسي لا يمكن إجراؤه عليه باعتبار آخر، والذات البحت منزه عن كلها والأحكام كما تختلف بالحقيقة تختلف بحسب اختلاف الاعتبار، إذا كان مطابقاً لنفس الأمر هذا هو الكلام المجمل وتفصيله يقتضي بسطاً لا يليق بهذا الموضع

قوله : ( يعدونه مكابرة ويقولون : إن اختلاف الماهيات بالذات معلوم بالضرورة . قوله : ( لكان أعم الذاتيات المشتركة) أي ذاتياً فوق جميع الذاتيات المشتركة بين الحقائق الموجودة . قوله : (إذ لا ذاتي لها أعم منه لأن جميع الموجودات الممكنة منحصرة في المقولات العشر ) وذاتياتها أخص من الوجود فعلى تقدير جزئيته يكون فوق جميع الذاتيات فقوله : إذ لا ذاتي لها أعم منه كناية عن كون كل ذاتي لها أخص منه على ما هو المتبادر في العرف، ويجوز أن يكون بمعناه الحقيقي، وحينئذ يحتاج إلى ضم مقدمة معلومة في محله وهو لا ذاتيات للماهية في مرتبة واحدة . قوله : ( أنواعه ) أو ما في حكم الأنواع .

قوله : ( وكذا الثاني إذ لو كان إلخ) فإن قلت : هل يجوز الاستدلال على إبطاله بأن يقال أيضاً : الوجود معقول ثان وجزء الموجودات موجود البتة قلت : قيل لا لأن المقصود بالإبطال جزئية الوجود من الماهيات وللماهية الكلية اعتبارات ذهنية ينتزعها العقل من الأمور الموجودة أعني الأشخاص على ما هو التحقيق وفيه نظر.

قوله : ( فلها فصول آخر ) لم يقل : أو أجزاء مختصة اكتفاء بذكره سابقاً.
'The first observation - the third aim: That existence is the essence itself or a part of it.
Then it would be either the essence itself or a part of it. The first is false because existence is common (as mentioned before) while essences are not (i.e., they are not common), because the realities of existents are necessarily diverse. As for the claim that all is a single essence that becomes multiple according to descriptions only, those who follow the way of reason consider it obstinate denial not to be heeded. And likewise the second is false, for if existence were a part of the essences, it would be the most general of the shared essential properties among existents, since there is no essential property more general than it. Then it would be a genus for them if predicated of them; otherwise it would be a shared part like a genus. Their types falling under it would be differentiated by differentiate or by specific parts like differentiate, which are also existent because they are constitutive and parts of existent essences. Then existence would be a genus for those differentiate as well, since it is assumed to be a genus for existents. Then they (the differentiate) would have other differentiate likewise (i.e., existent) and this leads to infinite regress and the arrangement of the parts of a single essence into infinity, and that is impossible because a composite must ultimately terminate in the simple, for the simple is the beginning of the composite; if it were absent, the composite would be absent.

He said: (And what is said etc.) Its proponent is the people of unveiling among the Sufis and philosophers: that all existents are one essence, which is pure existence specified by absoluteness from what is other than it, even from absoluteness itself, and its opposite is pure non-existence with no distinction and no description; distinction is peculiar to existence, and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of considerative descriptions of the necessary and possible presential entities. For every consideration, there is an intellectual, legal, and sensory ruling that cannot be applied to it with another consideration. The pure essence is transcended from all of these. Rulings, just as they differ in reality, differ according to the difference of consideration, if it corresponds to the actual state. This is the summary; its detail requires an exposition not befitting this place.

He said: (They consider it obstinate denial) and they say: The difference of essences in themselves is known necessarily. He said: (It would be the most general of shared essential properties) i.e., an essential property above all shared essential properties among existing realities. He said: (Since there is no essential property more general than it) because all possible existents are restricted to the ten categories, and their essential properties are more specific than existence. So on the assumption of its being a part, it would be above all essential properties. His saying 'since there is no essential property more general than it' is a metaphor for every essential property being more specific than it, as is the common understanding; and it may be taken in its literal sense, in which case it needs a premise known in its place: that there are no essential properties for the essence at a single rank. He said: (Its types) or what is in the ruling of types.

He said: (And likewise the second, if it were etc.) So if you say: Is it permissible to argue for its invalidity by also saying: Existence is a secondary intelligible, and a part of existents is definitely existent? He said: It is said no, because the intention in invalidating is the part-hood of existence from essences, and the universal essence has mental considerations that the mind extracts from existing things, i.e., individuals, according to the verification; but there is consideration in it.

He said: (Then they would have other differentiate) He did not say 'or specific parts' due to sufficiency of its earlier mention.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the position that existence (al-wujud) is identical to the essence (al-mahiyya) or a part of it. The author presents two arguments. First, existence is common (mushtarak) across all existents, whereas the essences of things differ necessarily; therefore existence cannot be identical to any specific essence. Second, if existence were a part of essence, it would be the most general essential property, acting as a genus for all existents. Differentiate within species would also have existence as a part, leading to an infinite regress of parts (tasalsul), which is impossible because composite things must terminate in a simple (basit) component. The commentary then addresses an objection from Sufis and philosophers who claim all existents are one essence (pure existence). The author dismisses this as obstinate denial (mukabara), asserting that the diversity of essences is known necessarily. Further commentary clarifies that the alternative view is not accepted by rational thinkers. The argument is definitive against the view that existence is a part of essence.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)
مشترك (mushtarak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence is exactly the same as the essence of a thing, especially since both refer to what a thing is.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot existence be identical to essence?
A) Because existence is a part of essence.
B) Because existence is common while essences are diverse.
C) Because existence is a mental construct.
D) Because essences are not real.

Key Takeaway
Existence is a common property, not identical to any specific essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is a common gift from Allah, but your essence (your soul, character, faith) is uniquely yours, are you nurturing that essence to reflect your purpose? What specific aspect of your inner self needs urgent reform today?

?
Your existence remains whether you obey or sin, but your essence—your spiritual reality—is corrupted by disobedience. Look at your past 24 hours. What action have you taken that polluted your essence, and what concrete step will you take within the next hour to begin purifying it?

Refutation of Part-hood
Al-Jurjani refutes the part-hood of existence with a regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot be a part of essence, as it would lead to an infinite regress of parts.

Core Idea
If existence were a part, it would be a genus, causing infinite regress.

After refuting identity, Al-Jurjani turns to the possibility that existence is a part of essence. He argues that if existence were a part, it would be the most general shared essential property, acting as a genus for all existents. This would lead to an infinite chain of differentiate, which is impossible because any composite must ultimately be composed of simples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Pay attention to the logical chain.
2
Understand the first consequence
If existence is a part, it would be the most general shared essential property, so it would be a genus for all existents.
A genus is a category that includes species differentiated by specific differences (differentiae).
3
Follow the regress argument
The differentiae of species must also be existent, so existence would be a part of them as well. This leads to an infinite regress of differentiate.
The author gives the example of types (anwa') and differentiate (fusul).
4
Understand why regress is impossible
A composite must ultimately resolve into simple components; infinite regress is impossible because there would be no simple foundation.
This is a standard principle in Aristotelian metaphysics.

Source Text

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها
لكان إما نفسها أو جزؤها والأول باطل لأنه ( أي الوجود ) مشترك ) لما مر ( دونها ) أي دون الماهية لأن حقائق الموجودات متخالفة بالضرورة وما يقال من أن الكل ذات واحدة تتعدد بحسب الأوصاف لا غير فالمتقيدون بطور العقل يعدونه مكابرة لا يلتفت إليها ( وكذا الثاني) باطل إذ لو كان الوجود ( جزءاً ) للماهيات لكان أعم الذاتيات المشتركة بين الموجودات إذ لا ذاتي لها أعم منه ( فكان جنسا لها ) إن كان محمولاً عليها وإلا كان جزءاً مشتركاً مثل الجنس ) وتتمايز أنواعه ) المندرجة تحته ( بفصول) أو بأجزاء مختصة مثل الفصول ( هي أيضاً موجودة) لكونها مقومة وأجزاء للماهيات الموجودة ( فيكون ( الوجود ( جنساً لها ) أي لتلك الفصول أيضاً إذ الفرض أنه جنس للموجودات ( فلها ) أي فللفصول (فصول) أخر ( كذلك ) أي موجودة أيضاً ) ويلزم التسلسل وترتب أجزاء الماهية الواحدة إلى غير النهاية ( وأنه محال إذ المركب لا بد له من الانتهاء إلى البسيط ) لأن البسيط مبدأ المركب فلو انتفى انتفي

قوله : ( وما يقال إلخ ) قائله أهل المكاشفة من الصوفية، والحكماء، وهو أن كل الموجودات ذات واحدة وهي الوجود البحت المتشخص بالإطلاق عما سواه، حتى عن الإطلاق أيضاً، ومقابله العدم الصرف لا تميز فيه ولا وصف له فالتميز مختص بالوجود وهو متعدد بحسب تعدد الأوصاف الاعتبارية للنفس الأمرية الوجوبية والإمكانية، وله بكل اعتبار حكم عقلي وشرعي وحسي لا يمكن إجراؤه عليه باعتبار آخر، والذات البحت منزه عن كلها والأحكام كما تختلف بالحقيقة تختلف بحسب اختلاف الاعتبار، إذا كان مطابقاً لنفس الأمر هذا هو الكلام المجمل وتفصيله يقتضي بسطاً لا يليق بهذا الموضع

قوله : ( يعدونه مكابرة ويقولون : إن اختلاف الماهيات بالذات معلوم بالضرورة . قوله : ( لكان أعم الذاتيات المشتركة) أي ذاتياً فوق جميع الذاتيات المشتركة بين الحقائق الموجودة . قوله : (إذ لا ذاتي لها أعم منه لأن جميع الموجودات الممكنة منحصرة في المقولات العشر ) وذاتياتها أخص من الوجود فعلى تقدير جزئيته يكون فوق جميع الذاتيات فقوله : إذ لا ذاتي لها أعم منه كناية عن كون كل ذاتي لها أخص منه على ما هو المتبادر في العرف، ويجوز أن يكون بمعناه الحقيقي، وحينئذ يحتاج إلى ضم مقدمة معلومة في محله وهو لا ذاتيات للماهية في مرتبة واحدة . قوله : ( أنواعه ) أو ما في حكم الأنواع .

قوله : ( وكذا الثاني إذ لو كان إلخ) فإن قلت : هل يجوز الاستدلال على إبطاله بأن يقال أيضاً : الوجود معقول ثان وجزء الموجودات موجود البتة قلت : قيل لا لأن المقصود بالإبطال جزئية الوجود من الماهيات وللماهية الكلية اعتبارات ذهنية ينتزعها العقل من الأمور الموجودة أعني الأشخاص على ما هو التحقيق وفيه نظر.

قوله : ( فلها فصول آخر ) لم يقل : أو أجزاء مختصة اكتفاء بذكره سابقاً.
'The first observation - the third aim: That existence is the essence itself or a part of it.
Then it would be either the essence itself or a part of it. The first is false because existence is common (as mentioned before) while essences are not (i.e., they are not common), because the realities of existents are necessarily diverse. As for the claim that all is a single essence that becomes multiple according to descriptions only, those who follow the way of reason consider it obstinate denial not to be heeded. And likewise the second is false, for if existence were a part of the essences, it would be the most general of the shared essential properties among existents, since there is no essential property more general than it. Then it would be a genus for them if predicated of them; otherwise it would be a shared part like a genus. Their types falling under it would be differentiated by differentiate or by specific parts like differentiate, which are also existent because they are constitutive and parts of existent essences. Then existence would be a genus for those differentiate as well, since it is assumed to be a genus for existents. Then they (the differentiate) would have other differentiate likewise (i.e., existent) and this leads to infinite regress and the arrangement of the parts of a single essence into infinity, and that is impossible because a composite must ultimately terminate in the simple, for the simple is the beginning of the composite; if it were absent, the composite would be absent.

He said: (And what is said etc.) Its proponent is the people of unveiling among the Sufis and philosophers: that all existents are one essence, which is pure existence specified by absoluteness from what is other than it, even from absoluteness itself, and its opposite is pure non-existence with no distinction and no description; distinction is peculiar to existence, and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of considerative descriptions of the necessary and possible presential entities. For every consideration, there is an intellectual, legal, and sensory ruling that cannot be applied to it with another consideration. The pure essence is transcended from all of these. Rulings, just as they differ in reality, differ according to the difference of consideration, if it corresponds to the actual state. This is the summary; its detail requires an exposition not befitting this place.

He said: (They consider it obstinate denial) and they say: The difference of essences in themselves is known necessarily. He said: (It would be the most general of shared essential properties) i.e., an essential property above all shared essential properties among existing realities. He said: (Since there is no essential property more general than it) because all possible existents are restricted to the ten categories, and their essential properties are more specific than existence. So on the assumption of its being a part, it would be above all essential properties. His saying 'since there is no essential property more general than it' is a metaphor for every essential property being more specific than it, as is the common understanding; and it may be taken in its literal sense, in which case it needs a premise known in its place: that there are no essential properties for the essence at a single rank. He said: (Its types) or what is in the ruling of types.

He said: (And likewise the second, if it were etc.) So if you say: Is it permissible to argue for its invalidity by also saying: Existence is a secondary intelligible, and a part of existents is definitely existent? He said: It is said no, because the intention in invalidating is the part-hood of existence from essences, and the universal essence has mental considerations that the mind extracts from existing things, i.e., individuals, according to the verification; but there is consideration in it.

He said: (Then they would have other differentiate) He did not say 'or specific parts' due to sufficiency of its earlier mention.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the position that existence (al-wujud) is identical to the essence (al-mahiyya) or a part of it. The author presents two arguments. First, existence is common (mushtarak) across all existents, whereas the essences of things differ necessarily; therefore existence cannot be identical to any specific essence. Second, if existence were a part of essence, it would be the most general essential property, acting as a genus for all existents. Differentiate within species would also have existence as a part, leading to an infinite regress of parts (tasalsul), which is impossible because composite things must terminate in a simple (basit) component. The commentary then addresses an objection from Sufis and philosophers who claim all existents are one essence (pure existence). The author dismisses this as obstinate denial (mukabara), asserting that the diversity of essences is known necessarily. Further commentary clarifies that the alternative view is not accepted by rational thinkers. The argument is definitive against the view that existence is a part of essence.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)
جنس (jins)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
بسيط (basit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence could be a part of essence without any further implications.

Checkpoint
What is the main implication of existence being a part of essence, according to the passage?
A) It would make existence a quality.
B) It would lead to an infinite regress of parts.
C) It would make essences identical.
D) It would contradict the definition of existence.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be a part of essence because that would lead to an infinite regress of parts, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that existence is simply a property that things have, without considering the logical consequences of that assumption? What does this argument reveal about the need for precision in your own beliefs about reality?
?
If infinite regress is impossible for physical composites, what does that imply about your own reliance on chains of reasoning? Are there beliefs you hold that would collapse if examined as rigorously as this argument examines part-hood?
?
This argument forces you to accept that existence cannot be a part of essence. How does that reshape your understanding of what it means for something to exist? Does it challenge any comfortable or lazy assumptions you have about the nature of things?

The Proof of Identity (دليل العينية)
Al-Ash'ari's proof of identity via conditional reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof that existence and essence cannot be distinct in the external world.

Core Idea
If existence and essence were distinct, absurdities would follow, so they must be identical.

The commentator now presents the core argument for the identity of existence and essence, attributed to Imam al-Ash'ari. This proof uses a conditional: if existence and essence were separate in the external world, then essence would possess existence before its conjunction, leading to logical impossibilities. Understanding this proof is essential for grasping the Ash'ari position on the divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof phrase by phrase
Carefully read the Arabic passage from 'وهو الحق' to 'فحوى دليله'. Notice the conditional structure: 'وإلا لكان...'.
Pay attention to the analogy with body and blackness.
2
Identify the conditional hypothesis
The proof assumes the opposite of the claim (i.e., existence and essence are distinct) and derives an impossibility.
The absurdities are those mentioned in the second aspect of the Sheikh's earlier discussion.
3
Understand the analogy
Just as body and blackness have distinct identities in the external world for subsistence, the proof shows that if existence and essence were distinct, essence would have a separate identity from existence, leading to a regress.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The proof concludes that existence cannot be distinct in identity from the identities of existing essences, so they must be the same reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولا يدل على اتحادهما في الصدق الذي هو المدعى، ومحمل كلام الشيخ إلا بأن يستلزم عدم التمايز الخارجي الاتحاد في الهوية، وليس كذلك لأنه يجوز أن يكون عدم التمايز بأن لا يكون للوجود هوية خارجية بأن يكون His statement: (And it is the truth) corresponding to reality (and otherwise the essence would have a distinct identity in the external world (apart from existence) and existence would also have another identity so that it could subsist by the identity of blackness in the external world, just as the body has an external identity apart from blackness and blackness has another identity so that blackness could subsist by the body in the external world (then it would have) i.e., the essence (before) the conjunction (of existence) to it (existence), so the previously mentioned impossibilities would follow. And that is the meaning of the statement of the Sheikh (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) and the purport of his proof, for it indicates the impossibility of existence being distinct in identity from the identities of existing essences. And in it there is a discussion, because what he mentioned indicates that existence and the existent are not distinct in the external world, like the distinctness of blackness and the black, except that this does not entail that the identity of existence in the external world is identical to the identity of the existent. His statement: (And existence would have etc.) He added it to the text, because it is necessitated by his statement 'otherwise', i.e., that the mentioned negation does not hold, i.e., they do not have two distinct identities, rather they have two distinct identities in the external world, not because the ordering of his statement 'then it would have etc.' is dependent on it, for it is necessitated merely by the essence having a distinct identity in the external world. His statement: (Of the impossibilities) i.e., mentioned in the second aspect of the Sheikh. His statement: (The statement of the Sheikh) i.e., his statement that it is identical to the essence. His statement: (And the purport (of his proof) the first and second, as is not hidden from the intelligent. His statement: (And in it there is a discussion) i.e., in his statement 'it is the truth' there is a discussion, because what he mentioned from his statement 'otherwise it would have etc.' indicates the negation of external distinctness between them, but does not indicate their union in application which is the claim. And the interpretation of the Sheikh's statement is that he necessitates that the absence of external distinctness implies union in identity, but it is not so, because it is possible that the lack of distinctness is such that existence has no external identity, being a mental consideration acccident to it, and then they do not unite in what they apply to: essence is an external thing and what existence applies to is a mental thing. And by this is repelled what is imagined from the apparent meaning of his deduction 'so that what one of them applies to etc.' that union in application depends on union in identity; it is not so because it will be explained in the discussion of essence that the interpretation of predication as union in external identity is only valid for essential attributes, not for privative ones like 'Zayd is blind', since there is no external identity for blindness, otherwise it would be an external existent; and the comprehensive interpretation for both is union in application, for there is no impossibility in the application of privative terms to external existents. And that is because his intent here is that the absence of distinctness does not entail union in application, nor does it entail union in identity; it is not not that the basis of his statement: (so that it could subsist etc.) i.e., like the subsistence of an accident in its locus; otherwise the absolute external subsistence necessitates the existence of the identity of the subsisting thing, but rather necessitates the identity of that in which it subsists. No.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is the completion of the proof of identity and the objection to it. The commentator explains that the Sheikh's conditional proof ('otherwise the essence would have a distinct identity apart from existence') is meant to show that if existence and essence were distinct externally, then the essence would have existence before existence conjoins it, leading to absurdities. This is the meaning of al-Ash'ari's statement and proof. However, there is a discussion: the proof only shows that existence and the existent are not externally distinct like blackness and the black object, but that does not entail that the identity of existence is the same as the identity of the existent. It could be that existence has no external identity at all, being a mental consideration. In that case, they do not share the same referent: essence is external, existence is mental. This objection is repelled by noting that union in application does not require union in external identity, as shown by privative predicates like 'blind'. The commentator also clarifies a point about the wording 'so that it could subsist' meaning the subsistence of an accident in its locus.

اتحاد في الهوية

Checkpoint
What does the conditional 'otherwise' (وإلا) suppose?
A) Existence and essence are identical.
B) Existence and essence are distinct in the external world.
C) Existence is a mental consideration.
D) Essence precedes existence.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that if existence and essence were externally distinct, essence would already have existence, which is impossible; thus they must be identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own essence and existence are not distinct in external reality, what does that say about the unity of your being before Allah? Are you merely a collection of attributes, or a single reality accountable to Him? How does this affect your understanding of your own existence as a trust from Him?
?
The proof shows that existence cannot be 'added' to essence without absurdity. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes act as if your deeds are additions to an otherwise defined self? What would change if you realized that your very existence is inseparable from your purpose to worship Allah?
?
If Allah's essence is His existence, and He is absolutely one, how does this demand a complete reliance on Him? Are there areas in your life where you treat your existence as independent, as if you add to it by your own power?

The Objection to the Proof and Its Rebuttal
Objection to the proof of identity and its resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proof only negates external distinctness but does not prove identity of referent, and how privative predicates resolve the issue.

Core Idea
Lack of external distinctness does not guarantee identity; one term could be purely mental.

The commentator now raises an important objection: the proof only shows that existence and essence are not externally distinct, but it does not prove that they are identical in reference (union in application). Existence could be a mental concept with no external identity. This leads to a discussion about predication and privative terms, ultimately clarifying that union in application does not require external identity.

Guided Path
1
Locate the objection
Find the phrase 'وفيه بحث' and read the subsequent text. Understand that the objection is against the claim that the proof establishes identity.
The objection challenges the inference from lack of external distinctness to identity.
2
Grasp the core distinction
The objection distinguishes between external distinctness (تمایز خارجی) and union in application (اتحاد في الصدق). The proof only negates the first, not the second.
Existence could be a mental consideration, so it does not share the same external referent as essence.
3
Understand the counterexample
Privative terms like 'blind' apply to external subjects but have no external identity themselves. This shows that union in application does not require external identity.
The example 'Zayd is blind' works even though blindness has no external reality.
4
Appreciate the resolution
The commentator concludes that the objection is repelled: lack of external distinctness neither entails union in identity nor union in application, but the Ash'ari position can be maintained by focusing on union in application.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولا يدل على اتحادهما في الصدق الذي هو المدعى، ومحمل كلام الشيخ إلا بأن يستلزم عدم التمايز الخارجي الاتحاد في الهوية، وليس كذلك لأنه يجوز أن يكون عدم التمايز بأن لا يكون للوجود هوية خارجية بأن يكون أمراً اعتبارياً عارضاً له الذهن، وحينئذ لا يتحدان فيما صدقا عليه الماهية أمر خارجي وما صدق عليه الوجود أمر ذهني، وبهذا اندفع ما يتوهم من ظاهر تفريع قوله : حتى يكون ما صدق عليه أحدهما إلخ. أن الاتحاد في الصدق على الاتحاد في الهوية وليس كذلك لأنه سيبين في بحث الماهية أن تفسير الحمل بالاتحاد في الهوية الخارجية إنما يصح في الذاتيات دون العدميات نحو زيد أعمى إذ لا هوية خارجية للعمى، وإلا لكان موجوداً خارجياً والتفسير الشامل لهما الاتحاد في الصدق إذ لا استحالة في صدق العدميات على الموجودات الخارجية، وذلك لأن مقصوده هاهنا أن عدم التمايز لا يستلزم الاتحاد في الصدق أن لا يستلزم الاتحاد في الهوية، وليس فليس لا أن مبني قوله: (حتى يمكن قيامها إلخ) أي كقيام العرض بمحله وإلا فمطلق القيام الخارجي يقتضي تحقق هوية القائم بل يقتضي هوية المقوم به. لا
His statement: (And it is the truth) corresponding to reality (and otherwise the essence would have a distinct identity in the external world (apart from existence) and existence would also have another identity so that it could subsist by the identity of blackness in the external world, just as the body has an external identity apart from blackness and blackness has another identity so that blackness could subsist by the body in the external world (then it would have) i.e., the essence (before) the conjunction (of existence) to it (existence), so the previously mentioned impossibilities would follow. And that is the meaning of the statement of the Sheikh (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) and the purport of his proof, for it indicates the impossibility of existence being distinct in identity from the identities of existing essences. And in it there is a discussion, because what he mentioned indicates that existence and the existent are not distinct in the external world, like the distinctness of blackness and the black, except that this does not entail that the identity of existence in the external world is identical to the identity of the existent. His statement: (And existence would have etc.) He added it to the text, because it is necessitated by his statement 'otherwise', i.e., that the mentioned negation does not hold, i.e., they do not have two distinct identities, rather they have two distinct identities in the external world, not because the ordering of his statement 'then it would have etc.' is dependent on it, for it is necessitated merely by the essence having a distinct identity in the external world. His statement: (Of the impossibilities) i.e., mentioned in the second aspect of the Sheikh. His statement: (The statement of the Sheikh) i.e., his statement that it is identical to the essence. His statement: (And the purport (of his proof) the first and second, as is not hidden from the intelligent. His statement: (And in it there is a discussion) i.e., in his statement 'it is the truth' there is a discussion, because what he mentioned from his statement 'otherwise it would have etc.' indicates the negation of external distinctness between them, but does not indicate their union in application which is the claim. And the interpretation of the Sheikh's statement is that he necessitates that the absence of external distinctness implies union in identity, but it is not so, because it is possible that the lack of distinctness is such that existence has no external identity, being a mental consideration acccident to it, and then they do not unite in what they apply to: essence is an external thing and what existence applies to is a mental thing. And by this is repelled what is imagined from the apparent meaning of his deduction 'so that what one of them applies to etc.' that union in application depends on union in identity; it is not so because it will be explained in the discussion of essence that the interpretation of predication as union in external identity is only valid for essential attributes, not for privative ones like 'Zayd is blind', since there is no external identity for blindness, otherwise it would be an external existent; and the comprehensive interpretation for both is union in application, for there is no impossibility in the application of privative terms to external existents. And that is because his intent here is that the absence of distinctness does not entail union in application, nor does it entail union in identity; it is not not that the basis of his statement: (so that it could subsist etc.) i.e., like the subsistence of an accident in its locus; otherwise the absolute external subsistence necessitates the existence of the identity of the subsisting thing, but rather necessitates the identity of that in which it subsists. No.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is the completion of the proof of identity and the objection to it. The commentator explains that the Sheikh's conditional proof ('otherwise the essence would have a distinct identity apart from existence') is meant to show that if existence and essence were distinct externally, then the essence would have existence before existence conjoins it, leading to absurdities. This is the meaning of al-Ash'ari's statement and proof. However, there is a discussion: the proof only shows that existence and the existent are not externally distinct like blackness and the black object, but that does not entail that the identity of existence is the same as the identity of the existent. It could be that existence has no external identity at all, being a mental consideration. In that case, they do not share the same referent: essence is external, existence is mental. This objection is repelled by noting that union in application does not require union in external identity, as shown by privative predicates like 'blind'. The commentator also clarifies a point about the wording 'so that it could subsist' meaning the subsistence of an accident in its locus.

Key Terms
اتحاد في الصدق
اتحاد في الهوية
أمر اعتباري
العدميات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lack of external distinctness between two concepts implies they are identical in every respect.
Correction: One concept may be purely mental (like existence as a mental consideration), so they are not identical in referent. Union in application is possible without external identity.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection (وفيه بحث)?
A) The proof is invalid because it assumes the conclusion.
B) The proof only shows no external distinctness, not union in application.
C) Existence and essence cannot be identical because they are different concepts.
D) The Sheikh's statement is contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The objection clarifies that existence may be a mental concept, so the proof's conclusion must be understood as union in application, not external identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you sometimes treat your mental self-image as more real than your actual external existence? How does the distinction in this passage challenge you to examine the difference between mental constructs and reality? What step can you take today to ground yourself more firmly in divine reality?
?
Are there areas in your life where you assume that because you cannot externally distinguish two aspects (like your intentions and actions), they must be identical? How does the possibility that one is merely a mental consideration affect your accountability before Allah?
?
The objection shows that not every difference in mental conception corresponds to a difference in external reality. Where in your faith might you be overextending a mental concept as if it had external existence? What would it mean to let your mental constructs align with the truth as revealed by Allah?

Objection that Existence is a Secondary Intelligible and the Response
Commentator explains objection that existence is a ma'qul thani and the response affirming external identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that existence is a secondary intelligible with no external identity and the commentator's response that existence and essence are united in external reality while distinct in concept.

Core Idea
Existence is identical to essence externally, though distinct in the mind.

This lesson takes you into a central debate in Islamic metaphysics: is existence just a mental concept with no external reality? The objector says yes, because existence is a secondary intelligible. The commentator responds with a careful analysis of identity, predication, and union, showing that existence is indeed an external property united with essence. To follow the argument, we need to understand key terms like hawiyya (identity) and ma'qul thani.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the objector's claim that existence is a secondary intelligible and therefore has no external identity. Note how the objector uses the example of blackness and predication in equivalence.
2
Understand the response
The commentator argues that existence is predicated on essence in the same way as blackness, implying union in external identity. Pay attention to the discussion of predication (haml) and the distinction between concept and external reality.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The commentator addresses a misconception about union in truth vs union in identity. See how he defends the author's position against the charge of circular reasoning.
4
Reflect on predication
Consider the commentator's explanation that predication is 'union of two distinct things in the mind in external reality'. This is key to understanding how existence and essence relate.
This concept resolves many apparent contradictions.

استلزم ذلك للاتحاد في الهوية كان الاتحاد الهوية أيضاً معلوماً بعد الالتفات إليهما، فلا يبقى الشك بعد ذلك في وجود الوجود في أن ذلك بعد العلم بوجود السواد من أعرف النظريات فلا يرد أنه يجوز أن يكون الشك هذا على اتحاد الهويتين بحث إذ قد يتحد الماصدق بلا اتحاد الهوية كما في حال العدميات مثل زيد أعمى، وصريح كلام المصنف يدل على اتحاد الماصدقات لا الهويات إذ لم يصرح باتحاد الهويتين بل بنفي تمايز الهويتين وانتفاؤه قد يكون بانعدام هوية أحدهما، وجوابه أن سياق كلام المصنف يدل على أنه استدل على اتحاد الماصدق بانتفاء تمايز الهويتين بناء على استلزامه المحذورات، أو أنه أراد باتحاد الماصدق اتحاد الهوية وإلا
