Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the third thing is a proven entity that must exist in both the Necessary and the contingent.
Correction: The passage shows that no proof has been established for the third thing, and the correct position is to deny the equality of the two existences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the 'third thing' (al-amr al-thalith) refers to what?
A) The essence of the Necessary
B) The portion of being in the Necessary
C) The individual of existence that is additional to essence in the contingent
D) The universal concept of existence

Key Takeaway
The argument for a third thing is refuted by the lack of proof, and the correct approach is to deny the equality of the two existences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the existence of God is not a 'third thing' but rather a denial of equality with your own existence, what does that mean for how you approach Him in prayer? Are you truly acknowledging His uniqueness, or do you unconsciously treat His existence as just a bigger version of yours? Today, before you pray, deliberately remind yourself: 'His existence is not like mine.'
?
The commentator rejects an unproven claim about God. Are there beliefs you hold about God that you have never questioned or examined for evidence? Would you be willing to let go of any assumption about God that lacks proof? Pick one belief you hold about God and test it against the Quran and reason this week.
?
The argument for a third thing arises from trying to force a similarity between God and creation. Where in your life do you unconsciously equate God's attributes (like His knowledge, power, or mercy) with human versions? How does this affect your trust in Him? Commit to one specific thought: 'God's knowledge is not like mine—it is perfect and encompasses all.'

The Commentator's Detailed Refutation and Resolution
The commentator's detailed refutation and the resolution by denying the equality of existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's detailed refutation of the argument for the third thing, including the discussion of the equality of existences and the role of gradation (tashkik).

Core Idea
Denying the equality of the two existences removes the need for a third thing; mere possibility suffices.

Continuing from the initial refutation, the commentator elaborates on why the third thing is unnecessary. He explains that if one denies the equality of the existence of the Necessary and the contingent—either by appealing to the doctrine of gradation (tashkik) or by mere denial—the proof for a third thing collapses. This lesson unpacks that nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Trace the commentator's reasoning
Follow the commentator's explanation of each 'qawl' (saying). Notice how he clarifies the intended meaning of 'non-existence' in the Necessary.
2
Understand the critique of the virtuous person's answer
The commentator discusses a response from 'some virtuous person' and shows its weakness: it admits addition of existence in the Necessary as well.
3
Grasp the denial of equality
The resolution is that one should deny the equality of the two existences in essence, either by appealing to gradation or by mere denial.
4
See how the author's 'two objections' resolves the issue
The commentator concludes that this is what the author intended by mentioning 'two objections', and it undermines the objector's critique.

الواجب أمرين فرد من الوجود هو عين ماهيته، وحصة من الكون عارضة لذلك الفرد فيكون ما صدق عليه الوجود زائداً على الماهية في الممكن وعيناً لها في الواجب ( و ) لكن لم يقم عليه ) أي على ذلك الأمر الثالث ( دليل) أصلاً ( بل ولا قال به أحد فإن التزمه ) في الممكن ( ملتزم ) إظهاراً للفرق ( التزمنا ) نحن (عدمه في الواجب ) وقلنا ليس فيه إلا ماهية ليست هي فرداً من الوجود كما زعمتم بل هي معروضة لحصة الكون، فيكون وجوده أعني تلك الحصة زائدة على ماهيته ( وطالبناه بإثباته في الممكن هذا ما ذكره وقد عرفت أنت أن حقيقة الجواب هو منع تساوي وجودي

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
كثيراً ما نتعامل مع صفات الله كما لو كانت مثل مفاهيمنا البشرية. هل أنت ممن يتصور أن وجود الله 'مثل' وجود خلقه في الجوهر؟ كيف يؤثر هذا الاعتقاد الخفي على خشوعك وتوكلك؟ هل سألت نفسك يوماً: هل أتخيل الله في ذهني بصورة مشتقة من المخلوقات؟ توقف الآن واطلب من الله أن يطهر قلبك من هذا التصور.

The Objection Based on Tashkik and Its Refutation
Objection using tashkik and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection based on gradation (tashkik) of existence is raised against the proof of the third thing in contingent beings and why it is refuted by the earlier argument.

Core Idea
Gradation of existence does not prove identity in the contingent because identity was already refuted.

In this lesson, we examine an important objection to the proof of the third thing in contingent beings. The objector argues that existence is predicated by gradation (tashkik), so it could be identical to essence in the contingent, following Ibn Sina's view. The commentator explains why this objection fails, pointing to the previous refutation. We will read the complex text and unpack its logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the entire passage once to get the flow. Note that it contains both the objection and the answer. Don't analyze yet; just absorb.
The passage begins with background about the previous answer, then introduces two objections, and focuses on the first.
2
Identify the objector's main premise
Find the sentence starting with 'فإن الوجود مقول على أفراده بالتشكيك'. The objector claims existence is predicated by gradation, not univocally, and that for the Necessary it is prior, older, and stronger. Therefore, existence is accidental to its individuals.
The objector uses tashkik to argue that existence can be different in intensity.
3
Understand the objector's conclusion
The objector concludes that since existence is graded, in contingent things existence could be identical to essence (as per Ibn Sina's school). Thus, the proof does not necessitate a third distinct thing in the contingent.
The key phrase: 'فلا يلزم منه زيادة تلك الأفراد في الممكن لجواز أن يكون عيناً في الممكن أيضاً'.
4
See the commentator's answer
The commentator replies that this objection is repelled ('فمدفوع') because the author's proof already refuted the possibility of identity in the contingent. He urges reflection until the truth becomes clear.
The answer is brief but relies on the earlier refutation of identity.

المقال .
The Necessary and the contingent in the entire essence, even though they share an accident predicated of them which is the concept of absolute existence, whether its predication to them is univocal or gradational. And his saying: 'As for its portion etc.' is additional clarification of the answer, so the discussion of this addition by way of denial is outside the law of debate, and by way of refutation is of no benefit because the denial remains. And you will know from the author's speech what indicates that there are three things in the contingent. When the answer of that virtuous person was falsified, he said: 'Yes, here are two objections that occur to the two aspects.' He indicated the first of them by his saying: 'Indeed, existence is predicated of its individuals by gradation (tashkik), not by univocity, for in the existence of the Necessary it is more prior and more ancient and stronger. So existence predicated by gradation is accidental to that which it is predicated of among its individuals, since the essence and its parts are not predicated by gradation of their individuals, as is well-known among them. So the things of which 'existence, not existent' is predicated—i.e., each of them—share the concept of existence, and it does not necessitate the addition of those individuals in the contingent, because it is possible that it is identical in the contingent as well, as is the school of al-Shaykh. So what the author mentioned does not necessitate the establishment of the third thing.' It is repelled by the fact that the author's statement in the mentioned proof—and we have refuted it by rejecting this possibility—so reflect until the reality of the discourse becomes clear.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the previous argument and its answer. The objector argues that existence is predicated of its individuals by gradation (tashkik), not univocally. In the Necessary, existence is more prior, ancient, and strong; thus existence as a graded concept is accidental to what it is predicated of. Since essences and their parts are not predicated by gradation, the things of which 'existence' is said share only the concept of absolute existence. Therefore, it does not force the conclusion that the individuals of existence are additional in the contingent; they could be identical to essence, as the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) held. The commentator answers that this objection is repelled by the author's proof, which has already refuted the possibility of identity in the contingent. The reader is urged to reflect until the truth becomes clear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the gradation of existence (tashkik) necessarily implies that existence is additional to essence in contingent beings, or that it proves the existence of a third thing.
Correction: The objector uses tashkik to argue the opposite: that because existence is graded, it could be identical to essence in some individuals, thus not proving addition. The commentator shows that this objection fails because the possibility of identity was already refuted.

Checkpoint

C) The Necessary and contingent share no common concept

Key Takeaway
The tashkik objection does not undermine the proof because the possibility of identity in the contingent was already refuted.

The objector assumed that gradation of existence automatically makes existence accidental. But the refutation shows that assumption is irrelevant because the foundational proof was already established. In your life, have you ever allowed a seemingly sophisticated argument or a popular philosophical trend to shake your conviction in a clear truth from revelation or sound reasoning? Setting Up the Problem of Existence and Essence
Sets up the problem of existence and essence, introduces the shared concept of absolute existence, and discusses proper disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate over existence and essence is framed, including the shared concept of absolute existence and the correct way to object in theological disputation.

Core Idea
The concept of absolute existence is a common accident shared by the Necessary and contingent, regardless of how it is predicated.

This lesson introduces a foundational debate in Islamic theology: Is existence identical to essence or an additional attribute? The author first clarifies that both the Necessary Being and contingent beings share a common accident—the concept of absolute existence—whether that concept applies uniformly or ambiguously. He then warns against improper objections that violate the rules of disputation, and hints at a tripartite structure within the contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the main claims about existence and essence. Note the technical terms.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the shared accident
Focus on the statement that the Necessary and contingent share a common accident: the concept of absolute existence. Ask yourself: does this imply existence is an added attribute?
The author does not yet answer the main question; he only describes a shared concept.
3
Examine the disputation rules
Note the author's dismissal of two types of objections: simple denial (manʿ) and attempted refutation (ibṭāl) of a clarification. Why are they invalid or useless?
Denial is procedurally invalid; refutation is futile because the original denial remains.
4
Reflect on the foreshadowing
The author promises that the text will show three things in the contingent. Consider what those might be and why this matters for the overall argument.
This sets up the later discussion of essence, existence, and the accident of existence.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها
الواجب، والممكن في تمام الماهية، وإن كانا متشاركين في عارض صادق عليهما هو مفهوم الوجود المطلق، سواء كان صدقه عليهما تواطؤاً أو تشكيكاً وإن قوله : وأما حصته إلى آخره فمزيد توضيح للجواب، فالمناقشة في هذه الزيادة بطريق المنع خارجة عن قانون المباحثة، وبطريق الإبطال لا تجدي نفعاً لبقاء المنع بحاله، وستعرف من كلام المصنف ما يدل على أن في الممكن أموراً ثلاثة
The first observatory — the third objective: That existence is identical to the essence or part of it. The Necessary and the contingent are (alike) in complete essence, even though they share in an accident that applies to them both, which is the concept of absolute existence, whether its predication of them is by uniformity (tawāṭuʾ) or by ambiguity (tashkīk). And his statement: 'As for its portion, etc.' is a further clarification of the answer. So objecting to this addition by way of denial (manʿ) falls outside the law of disputation, and by way of refutation (ibṭāl) it is of no benefit because the denial remains in place. And you will know from the author's words what indicates that in the contingent there are three things.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage sets up the problem of whether existence (al-wujūd) is identical to essence (al-māhiyya) or part of it, specifically for the Necessary Being (al-wājib) and the contingent (al-mumkin). The author states that both share a common accident (ʿāriḍ) which is the concept of absolute existence (mafhūm al-wujūd al-muṭlaq), regardless of whether this concept applies to them uniformly (tawāṭuʾ) or by ambiguity (tashkīk). The mention of 'his portion' is a clarification of a previous answer. The author then rules out two invalid objections: one that denies the claim by simply rejecting it (manʿ), which is procedurally invalid in disputation; and another that tries to refute the clarification (ibṭāl), which is useless because the original denial remains. Finally, the author promises that the work will show that in the contingent there are three things (essence, existence, and the accident of existence).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simply denying a clarification (manʿ) is a valid objection in theological disputation.
Correction: The author explains that denial without evidence falls outside the law of disputation; proper objection must be based on argument.

Checkpoint

B) The concept of absolute existence

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that the debate over existence and essence hinges on a shared concept of absolute existence, and that proper disputation requires evidence-based objections, not mere denial.

First Objection: Existence as Ambiguous and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation on existence by ambiguity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that existence is predicated by ambiguity (tashkīk) and why it fails to disprove an additional component in contingents.

Core Idea
Ambiguous predication of existence does not necessitate accidentality; it only opens possibility.

This lesson tackles a crucial objection in the debate over whether existence is an addition to essence. The objector argues that since existence is said of the Necessary and contingents in different ways (by ambiguity), it cannot be a real attribute added to contingent essences. The refutation shows that the objector's conclusion does not follow, because ambiguity alone does not rule out identity in contingents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the objector's claim: existence is predicated by ambiguity (tashkīk), not univocally, so it is an accident. Then notice the conclusion that no third thing is needed in contingents.
The objector relies on the idea that ambiguous terms are accidental to their subjects.
2
Identify the refutation's core move
The refutation points out that the author's proof has already ruled out the possibility that existence is identical to essence in the contingent. Therefore, the objector's argument fails because it does not engage with that established proof.
The refutation is concise: 'we have invalidated it by repelling this possibility.'
3
Examine the glosses on 'prior, ancient, stronger'
The commentary explains that the Necessary's existence is prior, ancient, and stronger because it is required by His essence, is causal, and has abundant effects. These are not properties of a mere accident.
These terms highlight the ontological difference between the two types of existence.
4
Consider the weakness in the objection's premise
The passage notes that the claim 'essences are not predicated by ambiguity' is weak, as even essences might admit of degrees. This undermines the objector's foundation.
This shows that the objector's own assumptions are debatable.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Put the objection and refutation together. The objection does not succeed because it assumes ambiguity implies accidentality, but it only implies difference; the author's proof already dealt with that possibility.
The lesson demonstrates how kalam arguments are built and refuted with precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما زيف جواب ذلك الفاضل قال : (نعم) هاهنا اعتراضان ) واردان ) على الوجهين) أشار إلى أولهما بقوله : فإن الوجود مقول) على أفراده بالتشكيك) لا بالتواطئ ( فإنه في ) وجود (الواجب أولى وأقدم وأقوى فيكون الوجود المقول بالتشكيك ) عارضاً لما يصدق عليه ) من أفراده إذ الماهية وأجزاؤها لا تكون مقولة بالتشكيك على أفرادها كما اشتهر فيما بينهم ( فالأشياء التي يصدق عليها أي على كل واحد منها ( أنه وجود لا موجود ) مشتركة في مفهوم الوجود ولا يلزم منه زيادة تلك الأفراد في الممكن لجواز أن يكون عيناً في الممكن أيضاً كما هو مذهب الشيخ، فلا يلزم مما ذكره المصنف ثبوت الأمر الثالث فمدفوع بأن قول المصنف في الدليل المذكور، وقد أبطلناه بدفع هذا الجواز فتدبر حتى ينكشف حقيقة المقال . قوله : ( حقيقة الجواب ) وإن كان ظاهره إدعاء ثبوت المخالفة بين الوجودين. قوله : (خارجة عن قانون (المباحثة إذ لا يمنع السند فكذا ما في حكمه. قوله : ( لا تجدي نفعاً فإن إبطال السند إذا لم يكن مساوياً لا يجدي فكيف إبطال ما هو في حكمه؟ قوله : (أولى) لكونه مقتضى الذات ( وأقدم ( لكونه علة لما سواه ( وأقوى ) لكثرة آثاره . قوله : ( فإن الوجود مقول بالتشكيك إلخ) قال الشارح في حواشي المطالع الوجود في الواجب أتم لأنه مقتضى ذاته تعالى وأثبت لاستحالة زواله نظرا إلى ذاته تعالى، وأقوى لكثرة آثاره فالوجود مقول عليه وعلى الممكن بالتشكيك، وقد يجعل الأقوى راجعاً إلى الأتم الأثبت ويجعل كثرة الآثار، وكمالها دليلاً على الشدة وقد يناقش في التعليل الأول بأن الحرارة مقتضى الصورة الهوائية مع أن كثيراً من الأجسام أتم في الحرارة منه، والارتفاع مقتضى النفس النباتية وكثير من الأشياء أتم في الارتفاع منها فتأمل. قوله : ( فيكون) (عارضاً قيل : لا احتياج هاهنا إلى ذكر أن المقول بالتشكيك عارض بل القول بأنه مشكك فيجوز اختلاف مقتضياته كالنور والحرارة كاف في تمام الاعتراض فتأمل. قوله : (كما اشتهر فيما بينهم إشارة إلى ضعفه على ما حققه في حواشي التجريد، قال في المحاكمات ولقائل أن يقول : لا نسلم أن الماهية وجزءها لا يتفاوتان ولم لا يجوز أن يكون حصول الماهية وجزئها في بعض الأفراد أولى وأقدم من حصولها في بعض، ولم يقم برهان على
And after he refuted the answer of that distinguished scholar, he said: (Yes) here there are two objections (entering) upon the two aspects. He pointed to the first of them by his saying: 'For existence is predicated of its individuals by ambiguity (tashkīk), not by uniformity (tawāṭuʾ). For it is in the existence of the Necessary more prior, more ancient, and stronger. Thus existence predicated by ambiguity becomes an accident (ʿāriḍ) to what it applies to of its individuals, since essence and its parts are not predicated by ambiguity of their individuals, as is well known among them. So the things that are predicated of—i.e., of each one of them—as 'existence' and not 'existent' share in the concept of existence, and it does not necessitate an addition of those individuals in the contingent because it is possible that it is identical [to essence] in the contingent as well, as is the doctrine of the Master. Therefore what the author mentioned does not necessitate the affirmation of the third thing.' It is refuted by the fact that the author's statement in the aforementioned proof, and we have invalidated it by repelling this possibility—so reflect until the reality of the article is disclosed. His statement: (The reality of the answer) even if its apparent meaning claims the affirmation of discrepancy between the two existences. His statement: (Outside the law of disputation) since the certificate is not prevented, so likewise what is in its ruling. His statement: (Does not benefit) for if invalidating the certificate when it is not equal does not benefit, how about invalidating what is in its ruling? His statement: (More prior) because it is a requirement of the essence, (and more ancient) because it is the cause of what is other than it, (and stronger) because of the abundance of its effects. His statement: ('For existence is predicated by ambiguity, etc.') The commentator said in the glosses of al-Maṭāliʿ: 'Existence in the Necessary is more complete because it is a requirement of His essence, may He be exalted, and more established because of the impossibility of its removal with respect to His essence, and stronger because of the abundance of its effects. So existence is predicated of Him and of the contingent by ambiguity. And it may be made that 'stronger' refers to 'more complete and more established', and the abundance and perfection of effects are made a proof of intensity. And it may be objected to the first explanation that heat is a requirement of the aerial form, yet many bodies are more complete in heat than it, and growth is a requirement of the vegetative soul, yet many things are more complete in growth than it—so reflect.' His statement: ('Thus it becomes') an accident. It was said: There is no need here to mention that what is predicated by ambiguity is an accident; rather, the statement that it is ambiguous, so that its implications differ like light and heat, suffices for the completion of the objection—so reflect. His statement: ('As is well known among them') is an indication of its weakness according to what he verified in the glosses of al-Tajrīd. He said in al-Muḥākamāt: 'And an objector may say: We do not concede that essence and its part do not differ; why is it not possible that the attainment of essence and its part in some individuals is more prior and more ancient than its attainment in others? And no proof has been established for'.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage contains the first objection and its refutation. The objection argues that existence (al-wujūd) is predicated of its individuals by ambiguity (tashkīk), meaning it applies to the Necessary Being in a more prior, ancient, and stronger sense than to contingent beings. Therefore, existence as an ambiguous term is an accident (ʿāriḍ) to its individuals, because essences are not predicated by ambiguity. Consequently, the things that are called 'existence' (not 'existent') share only in the concept of existence, but this does not necessitate an additional component in the contingent (beyond essence and existence) because it is possible that existence is identical to essence in the contingent, as Avicenna (al-Shaykh) held. The objector concludes that the author's argument does not prove a third thing in the contingent. The refutation states that the author's proof has already invalidated this possibility, so the objection fails. The commentary then glosses the terms 'prior', 'ancient', 'stronger', and discusses the nature of ambiguous predication, including examples like heat and light. It also notes a weakness in the claim that essences are not predicated by ambiguity, allowing that essence might also differ in priority among individuals.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That ambiguous predication of existence necessarily implies existence is an accidental addition in the contingent.
Correction: The passage shows that ambiguity only implies possibility of difference; it does not rule out identity in the contingent, and the author's proof has already addressed that possibility.

Key Takeaway
The objection that existence is ambiguous fails because it does not disprove the author's earlier proof that ruled out identity of existence and essence in contingents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Second Objection: Dropping Ambiguity
Presents the second objection that drops ambiguity and uses simple denial, with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's strategy of setting aside ambiguity and relying on mere denial, and the analogy with essence and individuation.

Core Idea
Mere denial suffices: existence can be a common accident covering distinct realities.

In the previous lesson, the first objection used ambiguity (tashkīk) to argue that existence is not an accident. The second objection takes a different tack: drop ambiguity and simply deny the claim. Even if existence is a common concept, that concept may be a mere accident that applies to very different realities. The objector uses an analogy with essence and individuation to show how a common attribute can attach to fundamentally different things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection text
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the objector's key move: we remove the burden of explaining ambiguity and content ourselves with mere denial.
Look for the phrase 'نقنع بمجرد المنع' – 'we content ourselves with mere denial'.
2
Identify the conditional statement
The objector says: 'Even if we grant that existence is a common concept... why is it not permissible that this common concept be an accident for its individuals?' Find this and understand the conditional.
The objector is granting the premise for the sake of argument.
3
Analyze the analogy
The objector compares existence to essence and individuation. Both are common concepts yet attach to distinct realities with different properties. Reflect on this analogy.
Essence and individuation are accidents of their instances; why not existence?
4
Summarize the objector's conclusion
The objector concludes that the Necessary Being can have immateriality and principiality that are impossible for the contingent, even though existence is shared.
This counters the original argument that existence must be a nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار إلى الثاني بقوله ( وأيضاً قلنا أن نطرح ) عنا ( مؤونة بيان التشكيك ) ، واقتضائه كون المشكك عارضاً لما تحته ( ونقنع بمجرد المنع ونقول : وإن سلمنا أن الوجود أمر مشترك معني ) بين ما يطلق عليه الوجود ( فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك المشترك عارضاً لأفراده وأن تكون ( حقائق الوجودات متخالفة ) بالكنه مع التشارك في العارض ( فيجب لوجود الواجب ما يمتنع على وجود الممكن من التجرد والمبدئية، ويكون الوجود في ذلك ) كالماهية والتشخص) العارضين لما تحتهما ) فإنه يجب لبعض ما صدق عليه أحدهما ما يمتنع لبعض آخر منه، وذلك لاختلاف ما صدقا عليه ) بحسب الحقيقة (مع الاشتراك فيهما وأقول : إذا كانت الوجودات متخالفة الحقائق ومتشاركة في العارض الذي هو الوجود المطلق، ففي كل وجود حصة العارض ففي الممكنات ماهية معروضة للوجود الخاص الذي هو معروض للحصة، فقد ثبت فيها ثلاثة أشياء، فهذا الجواب الذي طرح فيه مؤونة التشكيك
من ذلك
قوله : (فإن تخالفها لا ينفعنا لأن الكلام في اقتضاء الوجود للتجرد والمبدئية لا في اقتضاء الموجود .
قوله : (أي يجوز إلخ إنما قال : ذلك لأن التشكيك لا يقتضي أن يكون ما تحته مختلف الحقيقة، بل جوازه .
قوله : ( إذا كانت الوجودات إلخ قد عرفت أن مجرد جواز التخالف في الحقيقة كاف كاف في رد الاستدلالين، وهو يستلزم جواز الأمر الثالث وليس فيه اعتراف بزيادة الوجود، نعم لو ادعى التخالف في الحقيقة يلزم ذلك كما لا يخفى .
إبطاله وأقوى ما قيل فيه أنه إذا اختلف الماهية والذاتي في الجزئيات لم تكن ماهيتها واحدة ولا ذاتيها واحداً، وهو منقوض بالعارض على أن من الناس من ذهب إلى أن الاشتداد والضعف اختلاف في الماهية بالكمال والنقصان .
قوله : ( وأقول إذا كانت الوجودات إلخ) قيل : هذا الاعتراض على الاعتراض الثاني على لزوم القول بأن الوجود غير الماهية مطلقاً واجباً كان أو ممكناً وهذا غير مبني
للمصنف
And he pointed to the second by his saying: (And also we say: we remove) from ourselves (the burden of explaining the ambiguity), and its requirement that the ambiguous term be an accident for what is under it, (and we content ourselves with mere denial and we say: Even if we grant that existence is a common concept among that to which existence is attributed, why is it not permissible that this common concept be an accident for its individuals, and that the realities of existences be different in essence while sharing in the accident? So for the existence of the Necessary there is required what is impossible for the existence of the contingent, such as immateriality and principiality, and existence in that is like essence and individuation, which are accidents for what is under them. For what is required for some of that to which one of them applies is impossible for some other of it, due to the difference of what they apply to in reality, despite sharing in them. And I say: If the existences are different in reality and share in the accident which is absolute existence, then in each existence there is a portion of the accident. So in contingents there is an essence that is subject to the particular existence which is subject to the portion. Thus three things are established in them. This is the answer that removes the burden of ambiguity.)
From that.
His statement: (For their difference does not benefit us because the discussion is about the requirement of existence for immateriality and principiality, not about the requirement of the existent.)
His statement: (i.e., it is permissible, etc.) He only said that because ambiguity does not necessitate that what is under it is different in reality, but rather its permissibility.
His statement: (If the existences, etc.) You already know that mere permissibility of difference in reality is sufficient in refuting both proofs, and it entails the permissibility of the third thing, and there is no admission of an addition of existence in it. Yes, if he claimed actual difference in reality, that would necessitate it, as is not hidden.
Its refutation: And the strongest thing said about it is that if essence and the essential differ in particulars, then their essence is not one and their essential is not one. But it is refuted by the accident, since some people hold that intensity and weakness are a difference in essence by perfection and deficiency.
His statement: (And I say: If the existences, etc.) It was said: This is an objection to the second objection regarding the necessity of the statement that existence is other than essence absolutely, necessary or contingent, and this is not built upon the author.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objector proposes to set aside the complication of ambiguity (tashkīk) and simply deny (manʿ) the claim. Even if existence is a common concept among its instances, it can still be an accident (ʿāriḍ) to those instances, and the realities of particular existences can differ in essence. This allows the Necessary Being to have attributes like immateriality and principiality that are impossible for the contingent, just as essence and individuation differ in their instances while being common concepts. The answer (attributed to 'I say') concludes that if existences differ in reality but share the accident of absolute existence, then each existence has a portion of that accident, resulting in three things in the contingent: essence, particular existence, and the portion (the common concept). The commentary then clarifies that the permissibility of difference is sufficient to refute the original argument, and that no admission of existence as an addition is required unless actual difference is claimed. A further refutation of an argument against this is given: if essence and essential differ in particulars, they would not be one, but this is countered by the fact that intensity and weakness can indicate difference in essence. The final note suggests that this objection is directed against the second objection's implication that existence is always other than essence.

Key Terms
منع (manʿ)
مشترك (mushtarak)
عارض (ʿāriḍ)
تشخّص (tashakhkhuṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if existence is a common concept, it must be a genus or species and identical in reality across beings.
Correction: The objector argues it can be a common accident, covering distinct realities, like essence and individuation.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the objector use to argue that existence can be a common accident?
A) Existence as a substance
B) Essence and individuation as common accidents
C) The Necessary and contingent as species
D) The category of quality

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that even if existence is a common concept, it can still be an accident that attaches to fundamentally different realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is merely an accident that could have been otherwise, how does that change the way you value each moment? What would it mean to live today as a gift from the Necessary Being rather than a given?
?
The objection argues that a common accident can cover fundamentally different realities. Reflect: Are you treating your own existence as if it shares the same necessary quality as God's? In what areas of your life do you act as though you are self-sufficient, forgetting that you are contingent?
?
Consider the analogy: essence and individuation are common but impose different requirements. Do you recognize that your personal identity—your 'I'—is also an accident that points beyond itself? How can you use this awareness to break free from self-centeredness and turn toward the Source of existence?

intermediate

The Author's Answer: Three Things in the Contingent
Presents the author's answer establishing three things in contingents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's deduction that contingent beings contain three components: essence, particular existence, and a portion of absolute existence.

Core Idea
Contingents have three things: essence, particular existence, and a portion of the common accident.

In response to the second objection, the author speaks in his own voice. He takes the objector's suggestion that existences differ in reality but share absolute existence as an accident, and draws out its consequences. If each existence has a 'portion' of the common accident, then in contingent beings there must be three distinct elements. This answer becomes the definitive rebuttal of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer text
Read the Arabic passage beginning with 'وأقول' carefully. This is the author's direct response.
2
Understand the conditional
The author says: 'If the existences are different in reality and share in the accident which is absolute existence...' Grasp this conditional assumption.
This mirrors the objector's conditional.
3
Identify the three elements
The author says three things are established: essence, particular existence, and the portion (ḥiṣṣa) of the accident. List them and understand how they relate.
The portion is the share of absolute existence.
4
See the conclusion
The author concludes: 'this is the answer that removes the burden of ambiguity.' Understand why this answer resolves the objection.
No need for ambiguity; the common accident is a portion in each existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, how many things are established in contingent beings?
A) One thing only
B) Two things: essence and existence
C) Three things: essence, particular existence, and a portion of the common accident
D) Four things including absence

Key Takeaway
The author deduces three components in contingent beings to explain how a common concept can be an accident covering different realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that your existence is composed of an essence, a particular existence, and a portion of the common absolute existence. Which of these three do you truly thank Allah for? Do you thank Him for making you exist (the particular existence) or for the fact that you exist at all (the portion)? And if your particular existence is what makes you distinct, do you use that uniqueness to worship Him more sincerely than anyone else? Answer honestly: when was the last time you thanked Allah for your essence — for being you — not for your achievements or possessions?
?
The author explains that the portion of existence is an accident that inheres in you. That means your existence is not your own; it is an accidental gift from Allah. Does knowing that your very existence is a borrowed accident humble you? Or do you still live as if existence is your right? What would change today if you truly felt that every moment you exist is a portion given by Allah that could be taken back at any instant?

Clarifications and Refutation of a Counterargument
Clarifies that permissibility of difference is sufficient and refutes a counterargument using intensity and weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key clarifications to the answer and how a counterargument is refuted using the concepts of intensity and weakness.

Core Idea
Permissibility of difference is enough; intensity and weakness show essence can differ within a common accident.

The author and his commentator follow up the answer with important clarifications. First, they stress that mere permissibility of difference in reality is sufficient to refute the original proofs—there is no need to claim actual difference. Then they present and refute a counterargument that if essence differs in particulars, then universals would not be one. The refutation uses the example of intensity and weakness, which are differences in perfection and deficiency within a single essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarifications
Read the passage starting from 'من ذلك قوله : (فإن تخالفها لا ينفعنا...' and note the key points about permissibility.
The text clarifies that the answer does not admit existence as an addition unless actual difference is claimed.
2
Understand the counterargument
Identify the strongest objection against the answer: 'If essence and the essential differ in particulars, then their essence is not one.'
This challenges the idea that a common essence can cover differing individuals.
3
See the refutation
The refutation uses the example of intensity and weakness as differences in perfection within a single essence. Understand how this counters the objection.
The passage says: 'it is refuted by the accident, as some people hold that intensity and weakness are a difference in essence by perfection and deficiency.'
4
Final note
The final note says that the objection is against the implication that existence is always other than essence. Understand the context.
This ties back to the larger debate.

Checkpoint
What does the refutation use to counter the argument that if essence differs in particulars then the universal is not one?
A) The concept of substance
B) The example of intensity and weakness in essence

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument says mere permissibility of difference is sufficient to refute the proofs. You may desire absolute certainty in your aqeedah, but are you willing to accept that some foundations rest on what is logically permissible rather than what is forced? How does that affect your trust in the scholars and in the soundness of the tradition? What will you do the next time your intellect demands a stronger proof, yet you are told this is enough?
?
Consider the example of intensity and weakness within a single accident, like courage or existence. Look at your own life—you have moments of strong faith and weak faith, yet it is still faith. Do you struggle to accept that a single essence can contain such variation? If you can accept it in yourself, why is it harder to accept about divine existence? What does this teach you about your relationship with the Creator who manifests in degrees?
?
The refutation here uses an accident to show that difference does not destroy unity. Think of an attribute of yours that varies: your patience, your knowledge, your love. Does that help you grasp how existence could be one reality with different intensities? If you can accept that in yourself, why do you push back against the same logic for existence? What is at stake in your heart if you truly accept the permissibility of difference? Will you now view the diversity of creation as a sign of the one existence, and how will that change your worship?

The Two Modes of Predicating Existence
Distinguishes univocal vs. derivative predication of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between univocal and derivative predication of existence and why sharing an accidental property does not imply identity of essence.

Core Idea
Existence is not predicated the same way to essences and existences.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial distinction: existence can be predicated either univocally of the particular existences themselves or derivatively of essences. This sets up the argument that even if existence is a common accident, the Necessary Being's existence possesses unique attributes that contingent existence cannot share.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style and the use of parentheses for commentary.
2
Identify the two predication modes
The passage distinguishes between 'univocal' (muwata'atan) predication to existence-instances and 'derivative' (ishtiqaqan) predication to essences. Find these terms and understand the difference.
This distinction is key to the whole argument.
3
Understand the argument from unique attributes
The author argues that even if existence is a common accident, the Necessary's existence necessitates immateriality and principality, which contingent existence cannot share due to real difference. Reflect on why sharing an accident does not force identity.
The conclusion is that the two existences differ in reality.
4
Test your grasp
Try to explain in your own words how the author uses the distinction to refute the opponent.
The commentary provides further clarification.

The First Observatory - The Third Objective: That existence is the essence itself or part of it. He means the things to which existence is predicated univocally, which are existence-instances (al-wujudat), not the things to which existence is predicated derivatively, which are essences (al-mahiyat); for their difference does not benefit us (being different in reality) i.e., it is possible to be thus, because sharing in an accident does not necessitate unity of reality. (For it may be that the particular existence which is in the Necessary is what necessitates immateriality and subsistence by itself and principality, and it is not necessary that the existence of a contingent should participate with it in that necessitation of immateriality and principality due to the difference of the two existences in reality). And he pointed to the second by his saying: (And also we said: let us cast away from us the burden of explaining the gradation (al-tashkik) and its requiring that the graded (al-mushakkak) be accidental to what underlies it, and be content with mere denial and say: Even if we grant that existence is a common meaning among those things to which existence is applied, why may not that common thing be accidental to its individuals, and the realities of the existent things be different in essence while sharing in the accident? Then the existence of the Necessary must have what is impossible for the existence of the contingent, such as immateriality and principality; and existence in that respect is like essence (al-mahiyya) and individuation (al-tashakhkhus) which are accidental to what underlies them, for it is necessary for some things of which one of them is predicated what is impossible for another of them, due to the difference of what they are predicated of according to reality, despite sharing in them. And I say: If the existent things are different in realities and share in the accident which is absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq), then in each existence there is a portion of the accident; so in contingents there is an essence (mahiyya) which is a substrate for the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas) which is the substrate for the portion; thus there is established in them three things. So this is the answer in which the burden of gradation (al-mushakik) was cast. From that (commentary): His saying: 'For their difference does not benefit us because the discussion is about the necessitation of existence for immateriality and principality, not about the necessitation of the existent.' His saying: 'i.e., it is possible etc.' He only said that because gradation does not necessitate that what is under it be different in reality, but rather its possibility. His saying: 'If the existent things are different etc.' You have known that mere possibility of difference in reality is sufficient to refute the two arguments, and it necessitates the possibility of the third thing, and there is not in it an admission of the addition of existence; yes, if he claimed difference in reality, that would be necessary, as is not hidden. Its refutation: And the strongest thing said about it is that when essence and essential in the particulars differ, their essence is not one and their essential is not one; and this is refuted by the accident; and among people are those who hold that intensity and weakness are a difference in essence by perfection and deficiency. His saying: 'And I say: If the existent things etc.' It was said: This objection against the second objection necessitates the saying that existence is other than essence absolutely, whether necessary or contingent, and this is not built for the author. If it is verified, it is exactly the answer of that one among the virtuous ones, so reflect.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a complex rebuttal within the discussion of whether existence (wujud) is identical to essence (mahiyya) or additional to it. The author begins by distinguishing two ways existence is predicated: univocally (muwata'atan) to the particular existences themselves, and derivatively (ishtiqaqan) to essences. He then presents an argument: even if existence is a common accident (al-ard) shared by the Necessary and the contingent, the Necessary Being's existence necessitates attributes (immateriality, self-subsistence, principality) that contingent existence cannot have, because the two existences differ in reality (haqiqa). Thus, sharing in an accidental property does not force the existences to be the same in essence. The author further suggests that we can simply deny the claim that existence is a common meaning that is graded (tashkik), and instead grant it is common but argue that it may be accidental to its individuals. He then gives an example: just as essence (mahiyya) and individuation (tashakhkhus) are accidents that apply to different substrates with different properties, existence may do the same. In contingents, if existences differ in reality but share absolute existence as an accident, then each contingent will have three things: the essence, the particular existence (which is the substrate of the accident), and the accident itself (absolute existence). The commentary clarifies that this response does not admit that existence is additional (za'id) to essence; it only shows that the opponent's assumption of a common accidental existence leads to a multiplicity that does not harm the author's position. The commentary also refutes an objection that says if essences and essentials differ among particulars, then they are not really one; this is refuted because accidents can cause variation. The final line says this answer is exactly what some virtuous scholars gave, so reflect.

Key Terms
wujud
mahiyya
al-wujud al-khas
al-ard
tajarrud
al-mabda'iyya

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is a common accident, then the essence of all existing things must be the same.
Correction: The passage shows that even if existence is a common accident, the existences themselves can differ in reality, and the Necessary's existence has unique attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives that the Necessary's existence differs from contingent existence despite sharing the term 'existence'?
A) The Necessary does not really exist.
B) The two existences are predicated in different ways.
C) The Necessary's existence necessitates immateriality and principality, which contingent existence cannot.
D) Existence is not an accident.

Key Takeaway
Existence is predicated in two ways; the Necessary's existence necessitates unique attributes despite sharing the term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists' and 'I exist', do you truly grasp that the meaning of existence differs? In what ways does your daily worship reflect a recognition that Allah's existence is utterly unique and not to be compared to your own? Move beyond mere intellectual assent.
?
The lesson argues that sharing an accident does not force similarity in essence. Is there an area in your life where you have mistakenly assumed that because you share an attribute with someone (like kindness or intelligence) you are essentially similar to them? How does the lesson's principle challenge your assumptions about identity and worth?
?
Consider the attributes of immateriality, self-subsistence, and principality that are unique to the Necessary Existence. How do these attributes impact your relationship with Allah? In what specific way can you live today in a manner that acknowledges His absolute principality over every aspect of your existence?

The Common Accident Argument and Its Threefold Analysis
Presents the cast-aside-gradation answer and the threefold implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative response to the gradation argument and the three-part structure of contingent beings.

Core Idea
Contingent existence can be analyzed into essence, particular existence, and absolute existence as accident.

The author now presents another way to refute the opponent: we can simply deny that graded existence forces identity. He argues that even if existence is a common meaning that is accidental to its individuals, the realities of existences may differ. This leads to the conclusion that contingent beings involve three distinct aspects: essence, particular existence as substrate, and absolute existence as accident.

Guided Path
1
Read and locate the turning point
The passage begins with 'And also we said: let us cast away from us the burden of explaining the gradation.' This signals a new approach. Read from there through the end.
Notice the shift from the previous argument.
2
Understand the cast-aside approach
The author says we can simply deny the need to explain gradation (tashkik) and instead just state that a common accidental existence does not force essence identity. Follow this reasoning.
This is a more direct rebuttal.
3
Analyze the analogy to essence and individuation
The author compares existence to essence (mahiyya) and individuation (tashakhkhus), which are accidents that apply to different substrates with different properties. This shows that sharing an accident does not mean the substrates are the same.
This analogy reinforces the point.
4
Grasp the threefold analysis
If existences differ in reality but share absolute existence as an accident, then each contingent has three things: the essence, the particular existence (substrate of the accident), and the accident itself (absolute existence). Identify these three and understand why they follow from the opponent's assumption.
The commentary notes this does not admit that existence is additional.
5
Reflect on the commentary's clarifications
The commentary explains that this answer does not imply that existence is additional; it only shows that the opponent's assumption leads to a multiplicity. It also refutes an objection about essence and essential differing.
This is an advanced point; take your time.

The Third Proof: Necessity as a Relation and Its Rebuttal
Presents and refutes the argument that necessity (wujub) implies existence is added to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that necessity (wujub) is a relation implying added existence fails, and how necessity is identified with the essence itself.

Core Idea
Necessity is not a relation; it is the very essence of the Necessary Being.

This lesson examines the third proof offered by those who claim existence is additional to the Necessary Being's essence. The proof relies on interpreting necessity as a relation between essence and existence. We will see how the response denies that necessity is a relation, and the subsequent discussion clarifies the correct understanding of necessity and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the first part where the third proof is presented: necessity is a relation requiring two sides (essence and existence).
Notice that the proof is introduced as the weakest of the aspects.
2
Understand the initial rebuttal
The passage then states the rebuttal: necessity is not a relation but the essence itself. Ask yourself why the author rejects the idea that necessity is a relation.
The rebuttal is based on distinguishing the Necessary by its essence.
3
Analyze the commentary's criticism
The commentary argues that the simple denial is incorrect because the author's own words suggest existence is identical to essence. This leads to a discussion of philosophical views on 'wajib al-wujud'. Trace how the commentator reconciles different statements from Ibn Sina.
Focus on the distinction between 'necessity in the sense of essence requiring existence' and 'necessity in the sense of independence from others'.

دليل آخر وهو الوجه الثالث من الوجوه الدالة على زيادة الوجود في الواجب الوجوب الذاتي إضافة تقتضي في الواجب طرفين أحدهما الماهية والآخر الوجود لأنه عبارة عن اقتضاء الماهية للوجود فيكون وجوده زائداً على ماهيته قلنا كون الوجوب إضافة ممنوع بل هو نفس الماهية لأن الوجوب هو الأمر الذي به يمتاز ذلك الواجب عن غيره وذلك الأمر هو ذات الواجب لأنه بذاته ممتاز عن غيره والصواب أن يقال : إن القوة . قوله : ( وهو الوجه الثالث إلخ ) غير الأسلوب إشارة إلى أنه ليس بمثابة تلك الوجوه في القوة . قوله : (والصواب إلخ) يعني أن الجواب بإنكار كون الوجوب إضافة خطأ، فإن مقابلته لازم على المصنف إذ لا يلزم هذا القول منه بل الظاهر من كلامه أن الوجود عين الماهية حيث قال : وإن سلمنا أن الوجود أمر مشترك معنى فإنه يدل على منع اشتراك الوجود معنى، وليس ذلك إلا عند الأشعري القائل بأن الوجود عين الماهية وليس في كلامه تصريح بأن هذا الاعتراض من جانب الحكيم، حتى يلزم عدم صحة القول باتحاد الوجود الخاص والماهية في الممكنات لأن قوله : وإن سلمنا إلخ لا يناسب مذهب الحكيم كما تحققت نعم قوله : في تقرير الاعتراض الأول فالأشياء التي يصدق عليها أنه وجود لا موجود يدل على أن الوجود الخاص مغاير للماهية فيلزم منه ثلاثة أشياء . قوله : ( لأنه عبارة عن اقتضاء الماهية للوجود قيل : الواجب بمعنى ما يقتضي ذاته وجوده ليس بمتحقق عند الحكماء، وإنما المتحقق عندهم هو الواجب بمعنى المستغني عن الغير وقسمة الموجود إلى الواجب بالمعنى الأول، وإلى الممكن تقسيم له بحسب الاحتمال العقلي لا أن كلا قسميه موجودان في الخارج، وقد صرح بذلك الشيخ في إلهيات الشفاء، حيث قال إن الأمور التي تدخل في الوجود تحتمل في العقل الانقسام إلى قسمين فيكون منها ما إذا اعتبر بذاته لم يجب وجوده فظاهر أنه لا يمتنع له أيضاً وجوده ، وإلا لم يدخل في الوجود وهذا الشيء حيز الإمكان، ويكون منها ما إذا اعتبر بذاته وجب وجوده، وأقول قال الشيخ في مفتتح رسالة ألفها في بيان كيفية زيارة القبور وجدواها اعلم أن لهذه المسألة مقدمات فينبغي أن تعرف أولاً حتى تستنتج منها المطالب وهي معرفة الموجودات الآخذة من المبدأ الأول وهو العلة الأولى المسماة عند الحكماء بواجب الوجود، وأعني بواجب الوجود أن يكون وجوده من غيره هذا كلامه، وهو صريح في القول بأن واجب الوجود عز وجل يقتضي ذاته وجوده، وأما ما ذكره في إلهيات الشفاء فلا يدل على خلاف هذا، إذ ليس مراده هناك إلا أن حصر الموجود في القسمين حصر عقلي أي لا ثالث لهما عنده ولو بطريق الاستدلال، وأن الشيء الأول هو الممكن لا أن أحد القسمين محتمل صرف لا وجود له في الخارج. قوله : (والصواب أن يقال : إلخ) سيجيء أن الوجوب يطلق على ثلاثة معان ذاته لا من هي استغناؤه
Another proof, which is the third aspect among the aspects indicating the addition of existence in the Necessary: Essential necessity is a relation that requires in the Necessary two sides: one is the essence and the other is existence, because it is an expression of the essence's requiring existence; thus its existence is additional to its essence. We said: The claim that necessity is a relation is denied; rather it is the essence itself, because necessity is that by which the Necessary is distinguished from others, and that thing is the essence of the Necessary because it is distinguished from others by its essence. The correct thing is to say: that the strength. (His saying: 'And it is the third aspect etc.') He changed the style to indicate that it is not on the level of those aspects in strength. (His saying: 'And the correct thing etc.') means that the answer by denying that necessity is a relation is wrong, for its confrontation is binding upon the author, since this saying does not follow from him; rather the apparent meaning of his words is that existence is identical to essence, where he said: 'And if we grant that existence is a common meaning' – for it indicates the denial of existence being a common meaning, and that is only according to the Ash'ari who says that existence is identical to essence; and in his words there is no explicit statement that this objection is from the side of the philosopher (al-hakim), so that the falsehood of the saying about the union of particular existence and essence in contingents is not necessitated, because his saying 'And if we grant etc.' does not suit the philosopher's school as you have verified. Yes, his saying in the formulation of the first objection: 'The things of which it is predicated that they are existence, not existent' indicates that particular existence is other than essence, and thus three things follow from it. (His saying: 'Because it is an expression of the essence's requiring existence.') It was said: The Necessary in the sense of that whose essence requires its existence is not realized among the philosophers; rather what is realized among them is the Necessary in the sense of being independent of others; and the division of the existent into the Necessary in the first sense and the contingent is a division according to rational possibility, not that both of its two parts exist in the external world. The Master (al-Shaykh) has stated this in the Metaphysics (Ilahiyyat) of the Healing (al-Shifa'), where he said: 'The things that enter into existence admit in the intellect division into two parts: among them is that which, if considered in itself, its existence is not necessary, so it is clear that its existence is also not impossible, otherwise it would not enter into existence, and this thing is the domain of possibility; and among them is that which, if considered in itself, its existence is necessary.' And I say: The Master said in the opening of a treatise he wrote on the manner of visiting graves and its benefit: 'Know that this issue has premises that should be known first so that the conclusions may be derived from them; they are the knowledge of existing things taking from the First Principle, which is the First Cause, called by the philosophers the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud). And by Necessary Existence I mean that its existence is from other than itself.' This is his speech, and it is explicit in saying that the Necessary Existence, may He be exalted, His essence requires His existence. As for what he mentioned in the Metaphysics of the Healing, it does not indicate contrary to this, for his intention there is only that the restriction of the existent to two parts is an intellectual restriction, i.e., there is no third for him, even if by way of deduction, and that the first thing is the contingent, not that one of the two parts is a pure potentiality with no existence in the external world. (His saying: 'And the correct thing is to say etc.') It will come that necessity (wujub) is applied to three meanings: its essence... not from... it is its independence.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof that existence is additional to the Necessary Being's essence, based on the concept of necessity (wujub). The argument states: necessity is a relation (idafa) that requires two sides: the essence and existence, because necessity is the essence's requiring existence; hence existence is additional. The response denies that necessity is a relation; rather, it is the essence itself, because necessity is what distinguishes the Necessary from others, and that is its essence. The commentary explains that this answer (denying necessity as a relation) is incorrect, because the author's own words (in the previous objection) suggest existence is identical to essence (according to Ash'ari), but the opponent (philosopher) might not agree. It then delves into a discussion about the meaning of 'wajib al-wujud' among philosophers. It cites Ibn Sina's Metaphysics: the division into necessary and contingent is a rational division, not necessarily that both kinds exist externally. A quote from a treatise by Ibn Sina seems at odds, but the commentator reconciles it: Ibn Sina's definition 'existence from other than itself' applies to the contingent, not to the Necessary. The passage concludes by noting that the term 'wujub' has three meanings, which will come later. The final cut-off line likely refers to the essence, not something else, and that independence is one meaning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof from necessity (wujub) as a relation is decisive and shows existence must be added.
Correction: The proof is weak; necessity can be identified with the essence itself, so no addition is required. The rebuttal denying necessity as a relation is also not the final answer; the correct understanding involves distinguishing different meanings of necessity and analyzing the philosophical context.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the initial rebuttal to the argument that necessity (wujub) is a relation?
A) Necessity is a relation that requires only one side.
B) Necessity is identical to the essence of the Necessary, not an added relation.
C) Necessity is not a real attribute but a mental concept.
D) The argument is sound but applies only to contingents.

Key Takeaway
The argument that necessity is a relation fails because necessity is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being; any addition of existence is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that necessity is not a relation added to God's essence but is His very essence. Do you sometimes treat your awareness of Allah's necessity as a mere intellectual addition to your faith, rather than the core that defines your entire existence before Him? What would it mean for you to live as if His necessity were your essence, not just a belief?
?
Ibn Sina defines the Necessary Being as 'independent from others' (المستغني عن الغير). In what areas of your life do you still depend on created things as if they were necessary, forgetting that only Allah is truly self-sufficient? How can you shift your heart's reliance from these to Him alone?
?
The proof from necessity is presented as weak and rebutted. Are there arguments for God's existence that you hold onto as decisive, yet deep down you know your faith relies on more than intellectual proof? If the 'third proof' were to collapse, would your certainty in Allah remain? Why or why not?
